THE RED PAGE
Nevinson on Freedom
So much journalese masquerades as Litera-
ture, gilt-edged and solemnly bound, that
one is glad to have preserved in covers a
little of the literature which often appears
in newspapers. A writer like H. W. Nevin-
aon, whose Essays in Freedom (Duckworth)
have been gathered from the Nation and
other journals, brings wide knowledge and
a literary instinct to the treatment of a great
variety of subjects, and the results are worth
more than the moment’s notice by the news-
paper reader. In the first place the subjects
are generally of permanent interest. It
would be difficult for the most gifted writer
to make enduring articles out of Wade or
Willie Kelly, for the subjects are ephemeral.
They may, however, have a chance of being
“immortalised in a footnote ” by some his-
torian, or of being embalmed in the amber of
the satirist. The difference between these
essays and ordinary journalism resides mainly
in the fact that they were written, not
because the subjects had to be treated, but
because the author felt strongly impelled to
write about them. Nevinson is not by any
means a “ literary person,” fastidious about
form. In his essay on “ The Crowd Profane ”
he refers to a number of great writers who have
powerfully influenced the crowd, and says
that there appears to be in all of them “a
certain genial carelessness, a vehement exu-
berance, a regardlessness of criticism, an
impetuosity that is often outrageous.” He
thinks the merely literary man is in danger
of losing touch with life ; the dirty world
offends him and drives him into retire-
ment to write for “ the elect.” The imme-
diate influence of such writers is therefore
negligible.
If you wish nowadays to drive a poignant insult
into anyone’s heart, call him “a literary man.”....
Many years ago Mr. Balfour was engaged in a
political controversy with an opponent whom he
could not confute by any argument. “I knew
Mr. So-and-so as a writer of verse,” he said, and
no further argument was needod. The whole
British nation felt at once that an opponent who
wrote verse could not be considered seriously.
When Mr. Oscar Browning was standing for
Brixton and had just been defeated, a woman in
the crowd put her head into his carriage window,
and after accusing him of every natural and un-
natural crime, flung at him the final taunt of
“Poet!” Her mistake was uncultured, but her
triumph oyer the vanquished complete.
Keen interest in the world and its ways
he found in George, Meredith when an old
man, and a freshness about his books as of
to-day. Whereas Swinburne suffered the
penalty of aloofness ; being, in the latter
part of his life, one who pursued heauty in
far-off scenes and times and became deaf and
blind to present things.
There have been two test questions for lovers of
liberty in our day and country—the misgovern-
ment of Ireland and the extinction of the South
African Republics. That the poet of “Songs
before Sunrise,” and the pleader for the Man-
chester Fenians of 1867, should have been blind
and deaf to the struggle for freedom when it lay
close before his feet and was not yet/illumined by
distant time or the glamor of great names and
picturesque adventures, is a more direct sign of
some flaw in his art and method than all the
laughter and absurdity and tragedy into which
the “ aesthetic movement ” sank at last.
All that Nevinson urges in favor of purpose
in literature has been better said by Bernard
O’Dowd in that fiery pamphlet, Poetry Mili-
tant; but in a gossipy article on favorite
books he very sensibly puts the case of those
who, like himself, love literature but fear its
paralysing fascinations :
If they mix largely with action and with interests
not concerned with literature at all, they will find
their craving and their power of enjoyment only-
increased. Yet, perhaps, that is really the best
thing they can do. A'ction gives a penetrating
insight into the value of words, and, after all, it is
better to be wildly drunk with thought or beauty
at rare intervals than to go fuddling oneself day
and night in the tap-room of a library. So if ever
I had a holiday, and felt the craving for literature,
I should try to get hold of a book that would keep
me alert with new spiritual adventures and intoxi-
cated with new perceptions as with new wine.
A large number of these essays are con-
cerned with literature, from the dramas of
iEschylus and the poetry of Milton down to
Elizabeth Robins’ play, “Votes for Women.”
It is always, however, the literary expression
of the spirit of Freedom which has attracted
him ; for his knowledge of life has taught
him to fill what is often an empty word with
immense significance.
I can form no vivid conception of Freedom
apart from what I have seen. To me the word
conveys nothing as a Natural Right, nothing as
an abstract idea, and nothing as a syfnbolic
woman with wings blowing a trumpet into the ears
of marble captives, decoratively asleep in chains.
I have learnt the reality of the thing only from
the misery of its opposite.
The essays are uneven, as most collected
essays are. Some similes are frequently
repeated—like the upas tree in Coleridge.
But on the whole the quality is line, and the
book is worth reading and keeping.
Hewlett on the Free Life
The slight sketch of Sen house, the Gentle-
man Gipsy, which Hewlett gave in Hcilfvxty
Souse made one wish for more. In that
story Senhouse did not moralise very much
—he only outlined his philosophy and came
in effectively at the end to carry off the girl
whose adventures filled the major portion of
the book. Open Country (Lockley Library)
describes Senhouse at an earlier period when
he lived the Simple Life in its most austere
form, and met Sanchia, an English incarna-
tion of the chaste Huntress, Artemis—the
divinity to whom Hewlett has already devoted
a volume of poems. Here Senhouse explains
how he, the son of a wealthy colliery pro-
prietor, came to live a free life, close to
Nature like Thoreau and a vagrant like
Borrow. But here he moralises too much
and loses the girl.
After wandering in Europe, earning his
own living as he went, he returned to Eng-
land, where he made as much as ss. a week
by his paintings, and found it sufficient for
his wants. It was while painting one day
that he first had sight of Sanchia, then at
the innocent and impressionable age of 20.
She, unaware of his presence, had ventured
barelegged into a pool to rid it of duckweed.
When he came forward to offer help, her
complete unconsciousness of the dazzling
legs proved her of the company of Artemis.
“ The artist in our friend admii'ed, and the
man was stirred. He dated his subjection
from that moment.”
Sanchia is unusually innocent for her age
and position ; but that may be given in.
She is captivated by the Gipsy, not physi-
cally—as lie discovers—but intellectually.
He instructs her in first principles, nourishes
her love of beauty in Nature and art, and
opens vistas befoi'e her budding mind.
When she goes away lie writes her letters,
tremendous in length 1 and weighty with the
philosophy of “Poverty, Temperance and
Simplicity.” “Chuck everything,” lie, says,
in effect, “especially property and the 39
Articles, live on vegetables and water,
and sleep under the sky—if you want
to be free.” His principal business was to
sow flowering plants all over England.
“England a garden and Englishmen the
gardeners,” he exclaims in one letter. “ I’ll
die for that war-shout. Isn’t it finer than
your all red map, Kipling, Henley and Co. ? ”
The letters take up a large part of the book,
and are virtually a treatise on the free life.
You may, as Hewlett says, skip them with-
out impairing the story. And the story is
a good one, the members of Sanchia’s family
are particularly well drawn and the style is
Hewlett’s best Meredithian. But the con-
clusion, which justifies the sub-title, “a
comedy with a sting,” is dependent upon the
letters. So if you want the full value of the
story you must also read Hewlett on first
principles. And it is worth reading,
W
Ethel Turner’s latest —Fugitives from For-
tune (Sydney Book Club)—also touches
the “Driven back to Eden” theme,
in a pleasant fashion. But here the
man who fled with his family from his
millions in America and leads the simple life
at a place called Byrock (29 miles from
Sydney, N.S.W.) finds the system break
dowxi. He would insist on all his children
wearing plain white garments, and never
allowed from the, inborn hankering of the
daughters for lace petticoats and colored
things. A young barrister helps to demolish
the old man’s notions, and all ends happily
in a return to civilisation.
A Struggle for Freedom
W. G. Spence’s solid volume, Australia’s
Awakening (Worker office), does not purport
to be a history of the Labor movement in
Australia, but “ Thirty Years in the Life of
an Australian Agitator.” Nevertheless, it
is a very full account of the beginnings of
the Trades Union organisation, the entrance
of Labor into politics, and the history of
the Labor party in each of these States up to
the present time : together with a statement
of the influence of the Labor party upon legis-
lation and its aims for the future. Spence’s
30 years as an agitator happen to be syn-
chronous with the growth of the present
powerful Labor organisations from a very
modest origin. He acted as midwife to the
Movement, looked after the infant, and now
blissfully contemplates its sturdy propor-
tions. His first-hand knowledge gives him
authority to speak about it, and if his
literary gifts are not great he has the
virtues of sincerity, clearness and accuracy.
Wherever we have tested his statements
we have found them correct, and his book
will prove of great value for many years to
come as a work of reference. There is no
other book of the kind. itoydhouse and
Taperell published a useful little book on
The Labor Party in N.S. W. not long after
that party came into political existence ; and
Victor Clark, the Commissioner from Wash-
ington D.C., wrote a criticism of The Labor
Movement in Australasia, which tried to
be impartial, and succeeded in being dull.
Spence’s book covers the whole ground,
so far as the Commonwealth is concerned.
The story of the difficulties in the way of a
union organiser, the heroism of the rank
and file (especially in the country), and the
lies and subterfuges of the capitalists, make
interesting reading. It is the record of a long
series of battles which have been won by
Labor, even when, at the moment, Labor
seemed to lose. Mixed with the story is a
good deal of propaganda matter and fatherly
advice to young agitators. , Spence knows
the wisdom of the soft answer that turneth
away prosecution, knows that guile is often
more successful than force ; and even if you
have right on your side a good bluff has a
better chance of winning.
Of course, Australia's Atvakening is an ex-
parto statement of Labor’s case. It does not
say what Labor in Politics has omitted to do
which it might have done, nor what things
have been done which ought not to have
been done. There is no attempt to meet
the criticism of the State Labor parties by
this paper, summarised in a leader on “The
Queensland Election and Some Side Issues ”
(14/10/’O9). But for what there is we are
thankful. Spence, the Nestor of Australian
Labor politicians, has given great pains to
the compilation of a much-needed history,
and long after Australia is wide awake it will
remember him gratefully.
The Sonnets of Douglas
Everybody knows, by repute, Lord Alfred
Bruce Douglas ; if they do not, then it is not
his fault. His voice is very loud now in
England, and, assisted by Crankey Crosland,
he is reminding the intellectual and aristo-
cratic folk of things no gentleman would
dare make them recall. His organ, The
Academy, has one excellent principle (and a
number of others badly carried out), and it
is a careful consideration of modern verse.
To show that he is not all blatherskite,
Lord Alfred Bruce Douglas has published
another small book of sonnets, and a book of
beautiful sonnets it is. Anticipating silence
on the part of the papers, and. crude notices
by ignorant reviewers, Lord Alfred Bruce
Douglas bade his henchman, T. W. H. Cros-
land, write a note at the back of the book,
where the said henchman remarks “that
several of these sonnets are as fine as the
best in the language.” As a matter of
fact, the sonnets are not great with
emotional fire or with deep thought, but
are written with a delicate and beauti-
ful facility. The second sonnet in the book
(To Olive) appears to us to be a sort of para-
phrase of Shakespeare’s Sonnet XXX
(“ When to the sessions of sweet silent
thought ”). The preordination idea of the
third sonnet to Olive (who is, as you all
know, Mrs. Lord Alfred Bruce Douglas),
beginning “Long, long ago you lived in
Italy,” is a rather worn-out idea. Henley
used it in his book of “ Echoes ” (“ I was a
king in Babylon, and you were a Christian
slave ”) ; and Stephen Phillips makes Paolo
say, in the fourth act of “Paolo and Fran-
cesca,” “ Did I not sing to you in Babylon ?”
There are 19 sonnets iu the book, and five
of them have an octave rhyme in “ess” ;
four of the five use “distress”; three use
“dress,” and two “press”; and in one of
them milord endeavors to satisfy our ear
by rhyming an affix “less” with another
affix “less.” This is just pointed out to
show that, though his lordship may cavil at
other people’s technique, he is not techni-
cally perfect himself. The following is the
best sonnet in the book,:
THE GREEN RIVER.
I know a green grass path that leaves the field,
And, like a running river, winds along
Into a leafy wood where is no throng
Of birds at noon-day, and no soft throats yield
Their music to the moon. The place is sealed,
An unclaimed sovereignty of voiceless song,
And all the unravished silences belong
To some sweet singer lost or unrevealed.
So is my soul become a silent place.
Oh, may I wake from this uneasy night
To find a voice of music manifold.
Let it be shape of sorrow with wan face,
Or Love that swoons on sleep, or else delight
That is as wide-eyed as a marigold.
Wallace Irwin’s Letters of a Japanese
Schoolboy (Dymock’s) are amusing enough
for an idle hour ; but they read better when
they appeared separately in Collier’s Weekly.
If you have not met Hashimuru Togo before,
it is worth while getting the book and read-
ing a little of him at a time. Too much of
this kind of thing palls :
Hello, thank you ! Give me to telephone 3C04
Spiritland, please ! Yes, sir! Hello —it—is Hon.
Win. Shakespeare residing there to talk ? Thank
you again ! Is them you, Mr. Shakespeare? One
question to reply for Japanese boy, please. Who
wrote them trajick of Julius Caesar? Hon. Ber-
nard Shaw?—No? — He improve it, you say ? Oh,
them ain’t no news ! Hon. Shaw know that
already.
Anstey’s Baboo Jabberjee, 8.A., suffered
from the same complaint.
—
Douglas Sladen, with his usual energy, follows
hot foot upon the track of Caine’s White Prophet
with a novel called The Tragedy of the Pyramids,
intended as a defence of the British army in
Egypt. If Caine needed a counterblast, Sladen is
the man for him. The Manxman has the satis-
faction of knowing that he is fighting with an
equal.
As an author, Theodore Martin died long ago,
and had been decently forgotten when the news
of his death came. Born 1816, his best work was
in the Bon Gaultier Ballads, written in conjunction
with Aytoun, and some fair translations of Horace,
Schiller, etc. He also wrote ads. for Pears’ Soap,
and a Biography of Albert the Goodie-Goodie.
Dead, also—G. Manville Fenn, an undistin-
guished writer of books which are given to kids at
Christmas, but which are never bought by boys
themselves.
WHAT THE DEVIL SAID.
It was the night time, God the Father Good.
Weary of praises, on a sudden stood
Up from His throne and leaned upon the sky,
For He had heard a sound, a little cry, -I
Thin as a whisper climbing up the steep.
And so He looked to where the Earth asleep
Rocked with the Moon, He saw the whirling sea
Swing round the world with surgent energy,
Tangling the Moonlight in its netted foam,
And nearer where the white and fretted dome
Of the ice-capped Pole spun back a larded ray
To whistling stars, bright as a wizard’s day.
But these He passed with eyes intently wide,
Till closer still the mountains He espied
Squatting tremendous on the broad-back’d Earth ;
Each nursing twenty rivers at a birth.
And then minutely sought He, for the cry
Had climbed the slant of space so hugely high.
He found it in a ditch outside a town,
A tattered, hungry woman crouching down
By a dead babe—so there was nought to do,
For what is done is done, and back He drew
Sad to His heaven of ivory and gold ;
And as He sat, all suddenly there rolled,
From where the woman wept upon the sod,
Satan’s deep voice, “O, thou unhappy God !”
James Stephens (in the Nation).

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Angel Jones’ Experience.
I was a man of influence on earth,
Respectable—aye, eminently so;
An enemy to all unseemly mirth.
And hoped, of course, to go where good men go.
Thus when I died I carried quite a swag
Of virtues, testimonials and “sich’'
Away with me ; but there ! I need not brag.
For I was known as pious, strict —and rich.
What then my terror (you will sympathise)
When, chucking down the lot at Peter’s door,
There lay before my horror-stricken eves
A little, quite-forgotten sin of yore.
It was what you might call an— er—romance ;
The foolish boy, the customary girl,
And—well, a lot of other things, perchance
A kiss, a vow, a letter and a curl.
You take my word, I felt a trifle bad !
But only for a moment; for the load
Of charitable deeds and thoughts I had
Would surely hide that painful episode.
And with a smirk of smug expectancy
I started to explain about my life,
My contributions to the local see,
My record, my relations and my wife.
But may I never ! if the grim old Saint
Did not stand by, and calmly sneered and
poohed,
“Are ye not satisfied?” And, “No, I ain’t!
He coolly said ; which I considered rude.
At last I flourished the certificate
Our bishop sent me (you remember it ?).
And do you think that pass improved my fate ?
Well, let me tell yo’u—not a tiny bit!
Fact, I was fairly beat, and wondering if
I had perhaps mistaken the address.
I glanced below, and caught the faintest whiff
Of beastly sulphur. Fancy my distress !
When suddenly the Saint began to grin,
And in a condescending sort of way
Stretched out his hand and lugged that wretched
■ sin
Into the glare of open, naked day.
All hope is lost. I groaned with trembling lip ;
And watching with the courage of despair
The Saint’s inspection of that luckless slip,
I noticed all at once a change of air.
A laugh rang forth, a portal swung ajar !
A blinding flood of golden light ahead !
A strain of song—and then a Voice afar
That spoke to me ; and this is what it said :
“I shall forgive your virtues, Pharisee,
Even that diocesan reference,
All for the sake that ONCK you worshipped Me,
Not in the letter, but the spirit’s sense ! ”
STEFAN VON KOTZE.
Emu Bay.
Early morning, and the shadows scarcely lifted
from the deep;
Purple veils of dark still linger on the misty trail
of night;
Silence on the dark-spread waters, earth that stirs
from drowsy sleep,
With the grey shapes of the headlands touched
with gleams of tender light,
Past the yellow curve of beaches and the hills that
face the dawn ;
And away,
Flushing cleft and cliff and gully, fairy glen and
sloping lawn.
Early morn of early summer on the blue of Emu
Biy.
Still more' vivid grows the glory in the pathway
of the east,
Splendid out across the waters shines the gre it
sun’s path of gold,
Deeper pink on flushing hills grown paler with
the light increased.
Pearl and amethyst turned crimson, set in shining
manifol d ;
And the dark rocks boldly standing where the
wavelets sighing low
Melt in spray.
’Tis but a storied picture from the Book of Long
Ago,
When the sweet November morning broke in gold
o’er Emu Bay.
See! the grey is swiftly deep’ning into dusk
across the waves;
The waters all are hushed and still, the beaches
growing dim ;
A pure splendor of white starlight all the blue of
Heaven laves,
And the hills have sunk to slumber with the late
bird’s ev’ning hymn.
Now, shyly do the lights begin to twinkle in the
town,
And the flowery scents of summer hang like in-
cense in the air,
And the day
Has died behind the blue hills on a cloud-draped
crimson bier,
And the silent night is dreaming on the dark of
Emu Bay.
I see the dark hills ’gainst the stars, the quiet,
heaving sea,
The white road in the gloaming leading downward
to the town,
The red light on the waters by the little hidden
quay,
The white massed garden blossoms and the hedges
low and brown.
All unseen upon the curving beach the soft waves
murmur low,
Aye and aye,
Oh ! I see this dusky picture from the Book of
Long Ago,
When the stars are backward gleaming from the
blue of Emu Bay.
VICTORIA.
KATHLEEN WALKER.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by talcing some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscript ions, advertise-
■mients, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
•but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
•invite inattention.
It is not a good thing In address editorial letters to
ianyone by name. “ The Editor. Bulletin.” is the proper
superscription for “copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes mused by addressing io persons instead of
'officials. The person may not- he there; the official
•always is.

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 28, 1909.
THE N.S.W. RAILWAY SYSTEM AS AN
INCENTIVE TO SECESSION.
The block of traffic on the N.S.W. State railways
grows worse, if the language used by producers
and consignees is any criterion, and vague things
are said in all manner of quarters about “vigorous
measures.” The favorite remedy mentioned—in
fact, almost the only one mentioned either by the
Government or by the people who clamor exceed-
ingly on theGovernment’sdoor-mat—istheduplica-
tion of the railway lines between Sydney and New-
castle in the north, Sydney and Bathurst in the
west, and Sydney and Goulburn in the south.
These items will cost several millions, but it is
reckoned that the great scheme of dx-agging to
Sydney all the trade gathered by 3624 miles of
lailway from the face of 310,372 square miles
of country can’t be carried out otherwise.
And even the wildest applicant seems, in
a general way, to take it for granted
that all the trade of N.S.W. must go to Sydney or
else not go at all—that it would be uncanonical
and sacrilegious to send it to any other port, even
to any other N.S.W. port —so it would appear that
these costly duplications are inevitable. There
may be suspicions in certain quarters that they
are premature and nil at the traffic doesn’t really
call for them—or wouldn’t call for them if it was
worked on saner lines—but such ideas are mere
heterodoxy. Still there are countries which carry
far more tonnage per inhabitant with only a quarter
as much railway per inhabitant to carry it on,
and yet they have no congestion, no block, no huge
losses through sheep and cattle and wool and
wheat and other produce being left behind, and
they have no need to load themselves, yet awhile,
with a heavy debt for duplications. But these
countries make it a fixed rule to rush the goods for
export to the nearest port which is big enough to
take in the right size in ships. If there are five
good ports scattered along their coast they believe
in using them all—not in needlessly wearing out
their rails and their rolling stock, and burning
hundreds of thousands of tons of needless coai,
and paying gigantic sums in needless wages, and
charging huge sums in needless freight, to drag
all the trade to one town down in the south-east
corner of the State. This congestion policy causes
the last section of the l-ailway line to be so
crowded that it is necessary to spend millions on
duplications which the traffic shouldn’t really call
for till another 20 years are past, and in the mean-
time the congestion method leaves large masses of
goods behind to rot and perish. The people who
shove through on an average seven or eight times
the N.S.W. traffic per mile of line without
turning a hair don’t work in that fashion.
* * * *
If the N.S.W. Government ciiose to build about
20 miles of railway in the right spot it could end
practically the whole congestion trouble for many
years to come. All the pretence that it is a dis-
tracted Ministry doing its best in the face of a
very grave emergency is sheer hypocrisy. All its
professions of sympathy with the producer are
bogus and sham and fraudulent. Twenty miles
of railway, a few wharves and sheds, a customs
house on the beach, and an honest system of rail-
way rates would enable the export and import
trade of all the northern railway districts to be
dealt with at Port Stephens, a
splendid harbor a few miles north
of Newcastle; and straightway
one-third of the load which Sydney
has to carry would be gone. The
profit to the State would be very
large. It would pro-bably gain a
lot in freights, for the trade that
is now left behind by the wayside
because the congested lines can’t
carry it would be saved, and the
new faculties would create new
settlement and new business. The
State would avoid the waste of
large sums in coal, wages and wear
and tear by cutting many miles
off the average railway journey. It would
save the price of much new rolling-stock, for
two trains that don’t get blocked and delayed
by having to struggle over a final 100 miles of
in tensely-congested railway can do as much ser-
vice as three or four which have to perform that
feat. It would save, for quite a number of years,
the cost of many miles of duplication. Further,
N.S.W. would have a new city as an asset—it is a
fool of a country which deliberately throws away
cities—and, in consequence, it would have a
stupendous increase of land values for the State
to tax. But, so far from proposing to divert the
trade of the existing northern railways to Port
Stephens, the North Coast line, now under con-
struction, is made to take a detour in order to
avoid that large and useful harbor. It does this
so as to force the traffic of another 300-odd
miles of railway on to the hopelessly-over-
crowded track which leads to Sydney. The
new line follows the coast closely till it
gets near to Port Stephens, and then it does
a long and costly sweep inland in order to dodge
it. Certainly it touches the coast at Fermnount
and Coff’s Harbor, far north ; but then Fern-
mount and Coff’s Harbor are small places, only
fitted to take in the little coasting vessels that
discharge their cargoes at Sydney. Fernmount
and Coff’s Harbor are things that the Congested
Population party can’t object to ; but it is con-
sidered worth the spending of large sums of money
in order to avoid the big port where great oversea
steamers might load and unload. It is the sort
of fool’s trick which is calculated to make the
angels weep, and a Government which squanders
the public money on that kind of swindle is a
mere fraud and hypocrite when it declares that
it is animated by a sincere desire to help the
troubled producer.
* * * »
Again, a main line from Goulburn to Jervis
Bay, if built and managed with an honest inten-
tion of making Jervis Bay a great port, would
relieve the insane rush of traffic that comes upon
Sydney from the south and south-west. And
when the line to Bombala is finished it only needs
a few miles further extension to open up Two-
fold Bay and give further relief. These works
would end all questions about duplicating rail-
ways until the traffic had at least doubled;
they would wholly remove the congestion
which makes so much trouble; they would supply
N.S.W. with two new cities in addition to the
new city on Port Stephens; and they would save
hundreds of thousands of pounds in the cost of
needless railway haulage and maintenance and
sundries. Also they would enable thousands of
new settlers to get their produce to a profitable
market, whereas at present the few existing
outside settlers sometimes have to see their
produce rot because the railways can’t carry it to
Sydney, and at other times they have to pay the
value of about half their crops in order to get the
other half dragged over needless hundreds of miles
to the octopus city. The trouble is now affect-
ing the manufacturer as well as the settler.
Recently the cement works at Portland
(N.S.W.) had to discharge 20 men, though they
would much rather have engaged 20 extra men,
the fact being that the paralysed railway system
couldn’t get the product away, and there was no
profit in heaping up dead stock at the works.
The N.S.W. Government is now constructing,
oris about to construct, fully 540 miles of new
railway, all of which miles are to pour their traffic
into Sydney, often dragging it at great cost past
first-class harbors on the way, and thus make the
trouble worse. This sort of folly leads to curious
results and side issues. In the first place, as the
lines spread out and increase, it takes less and
less trade to make the same amount of congestion;
in other words, 40 small or half-loaded trains con-
verging from 40 branches on to one line can make
vastly more trouble than 20 large or fully-loaded
trains doing the same thing from 20 branches.
Distance counts for as much as weight, and when
it is remembered that the average distance that
each ton of hay, straw or chaff had to be hauled in
1883 was 77 miles and that in 1908 it was 187 miles
it i 3 plain that one ton can make more congestion
now than two could in 1883. Another result of the
construction of new lines in the coastal districts,
all pointing towards Sydney, is that they
block the traffic of the far-out country railways
and make these far-out railways unprofitable.
There are remote N.S.W. lines which were worth
building a dozen years ago, but which seem only
worth tearing up now ; and part at least of the
reason for this deterioration is that the block
between them and Sydney—the only port with
which they are allowed to trade —grows con-
stantly worse. Here is a two years’ record on a
few of the far-out railways of N.S.W. :
Loss after Paying Working
Expenses and Interest.

Tne two big Riverina lines—Narrandera-Hay and
Narrandera-Finley—with their joint loss of
£48,910, are shocking examples. Yet just along-
side them the little privately-owned Deniliquin-
Moama railway made a profit of £6021 (over 6 per
cent, on its cost), and part at least of the reason
for this difference is that the little private line
takes the shortest possible route to the nearest
port and thus makes business, while the State line
takes the longest and dearest route to a port 250
needless extra miles away and thu3 kills busi-
ness. There is really very little use in'building
lines in the remote parts of N.S.W. if their
chance of doing any good depends entirely on the
capacity of the single railway which runs through
the bottle-neck at the entrance to the Sydney
peninsula. Even the proposal to duplicate the
principal railways at great cost for some distance
north, west and south of Sydney won’t meet the
case. The traffic from the new lines now in hand
will probably eat up the extra accommoda-
tion. And even if the lines close to Sydney were
duplicated six times over, it would make no differ-
ence in the fact that Bombala is within 40 miles
of a first-class N.S.W. port with which it isn’t
allowed to communicate, and that it is proposed
to make it pay railway freight for 318 miles to
AUSTRALIAN RAILWAY BUILDING.
N.S.W. has a splendid deep water harbor at
Port Stephens which could accommodate all the
export and import trade of the northern half of
the State and thus relieve the congestion at Sydney
and on the railways around it. Port Stephens
has to be kept shut up in the interests of the con-
gestion policy, so the northern railways pass it a
few miles away , and the new North Coast line
takes a costly detour in order to avoid it.
Sydney, where the port is no better for its pur-
poses—to say nothing of tne fact that Sydney is
300 miles farther from Europe, where its wool and
wheat have to go. This sort of business pays
nobody. For the year ended June, 1000, the
N.S.W. railways earned less profit for every mile
run than they have done since 1904. And at the
same time the area under crop in N.S.W. in 1909
was 125,000 acres less than in 1900. The increas-
ing difficulty of reaching a market must be at least
partly responsible for this lamentable position of
things.
* * * *
The inevitable result of the N.S.W. Congestion
Policy is secession. There would probably have
been secession before this time but for the fact
that the person of British descent is slow and
patient, long-suffering and not easily moved to
wrath. Consider the situation of the southern
part of N.S.W. —say the area from Shoalhaven
to the junction of the Murrumbidgee and the
Murray, or from Shoalhaven to the S.A. border—
if it set up for itself. It has already got railway
connection with Sydney, so that port is secured to
it. By building about a dozen miles of cheap
railway from Finley to Tocumwal, and another
short cheap section from Finley to Deniliquin,
it could get the connections with Melbourne
which the N.S.W. Government denies it,
and thus have the run of a port and a great
market that are about 250 miles nearer to it than
Sydney is. It could join on to other Victorian
railways at Mildtira, Swan Hill and Koondrook,
and thus connect its farthest-western section
with the big adjacent city, whereas now that far-
western section has no connection with any city.
It is too remote to allow of any possibility of a
railway to Sydney that will pay expenses, and the
Government at Sydney won’t allow it to connect
with Victoria ; so, to all intents and purposes, it
might as well be in the middle of Sahara. These
suggested lines would also give the new State access
in half a dozen places to the Murray, from which
it is now almost entirely cut off, and thereby it
would have cheap river connection with South
Australia. On the east it could build a short line
to its own natural port at Twofold Bay, access to
which is rigorously prohibited by the Sydney
Government, and then it would have its own
outlet to America, Asia and Europe, and the big
liners would call in amid great enthusiasm and
depart with popular applause. And if a railway
were built to Twofold Bay the trade of eastern
Gippsland, which is now shut up and isolated and
dead, would also find an outlet there, to the great
profit of the new State. The State would ha.ve
its own bank (the Bank of Riverina), its own in-
surance companies and its own trustee companies.
Wagga, its capital, would have 100,000 people
instead of the 5000 or so that it boasts at present.
These are some of the things that might be if
commerce were allowed to take its natural course,
yet they don’t nearly exhaust the list. But
N.S.W. is run to a great extent on the lines which
burst up the Spanish Empire in olden days. Spain
insisted that its colonies should trade only with
Spain, and that their ships should unload only at
Cadiz. Sydney insists that the outlying parts of
its great territory shall trade only with Sydney,
and that their railway trucks shall be unloaded
exclusively at Darling Harbor. The principle is
the same, and if the principle isn’t altered the
result will almost certainly be the same.
THE TRANSCONTINENTAL RAILWAY
AND THE PROPOSED EASTERN DE-
VIATION.
The Northern Territory Acceptance Bill drags
slowly through the legislative halls of Parliament.
As it drags, the clamor of the State representa-
tives, each demanding that the transcontinental
line shall go, if possible, through his bit of ground,
grows more virulent, and the member for Aus-
tralia—the one who tries to think for the whole
continent—is conspicuous by his absence as per
usual. The habit of dividing the business of the
country into water-tight compartments leads to
everything being done on small parish principles.
In the matter of the Federal capital, there are the
five States which don’t really care because the
capital can’t be in their territory. In the case of
the Port Darwin railway there are the two
States—Tasmania and Westralia —which don’t
care a straw because the line won’t go
through their territory, and the other State —
Victoria —which doesn’t care much, because,
even if the line is diverted into the eastern
States and joins on to the Victorian system at
Swan Hill or some such place, there will be no
money spent on new railway-building within its
borders. In the affair of the equally urgent and
equally important line to Perth, there are the
four States —Queensland, N.S.W., Victoria and
Tasmania—which can’t see that it matters much,
because the work lies outside their State jurisdic-
tion. This watertight compartment policy doesn’t
make for large ideas, and yet a community with
such a large country as Australia might almost as
well have no ideas at all if it hasn’t large ones.
* * • *
Even an enlightened selfishness should make
the remotest States take a deep interest in
the Territory question. The offer now before
Parliament is that the whole Territory shall be
handed over to the Commonwealth at the mere
price of its debt, that a railway shall be con-
structed through the centre of it, that the S.
Australian railroad from Port Augusta northward
shall be sold to the Commonwealth, and that the
Commonwealth shall have its own southern sea-
outlet at Port Augusta. In other words, it is
proposed that there shall be, right across the con- i
tinent, a uniform-gauge railway, fit to carry express
traffic, and that every inch of it shall be the com-
mon property of Westralia, S. Australia, Tas- 1
mania, Victoria, N.S.W. and Queensland. The
port at the north is to be the common property of
these States; likewise the port at the south. The
line is to open up and make profitable 523,620
square miles of country which will be the common
property of these States —that is to say, that Tas-
mania’s interest in it on a population basis will
represent an area almost as large as Tasmania,
itself. The profits of the land and the rail-
way will belong to the six States, and even if-'
the average Tasmanian or Victorian never sees,
his great asset the asset will be there all the same.
It is absolutely necessary, in order to connect thes
Territory with the more settled parts of Austra-
lia, to carry a railroad through one State or
another, but so long as the least possible extent of
the line lies outside the Federal area, and it is
Federal property all the way, and there is a
Federal port at each end, and the profits are all
Federal property, the best has been done to con-
serve the interests of the six Federal partners.
• • * *
The eastern deviation idea is the direct con-
trary of all this. The line will be vastly longer
and consequently more expensive ; even to carry
it from Port Darwin to Rockhampton, away up in
Central Queensland, means covering more mileage
as the crow flies than the whole distance across
the continent from Port Darwin to Port Augusta.
And to connect with Brisbane, Sydney and Mel-
bourne the distances will be very much greater.
The deviation will leave most of the Territory—
which is to be the common property of Westralia, ,
S.A., Tasmania, Victoria, N.S.W. and Queens-
land-undeveloped to the general loss, and
yet the bill which the States must meet for
railway-building will be much larger. Instead of
the least possible extent of the line running
through State territory the largest possible extent
of it will run through State territory—and very
dry State territory at that. In fact, it will be
essentially a State line, yet the States —Queens-
land and N.S.W. —which propose that the biggest
part of the line should be diverted through their
country don’t offer to pay for the extra and need-
less mileage within their borders. Nor do they
offer to sell any of their State lines to the Common-
wealth and give it a port, as S.A. does, so that the*
line and the profits may be Federal property from i
sea to sea. Apparently the idea is that the'
Federal line shall end in the wilderness of no-
where in particular, and the State lines shall filb
in the rest of the communication—in other words,,
that the Federal line shall be a mere feeder of the-
State lines. Nothing is said about any provision
for a uniform gauge from sea to sea ; in fact, as
the Commonwealth won’t own the deviated line
from sea to sea it is difficult to imagine how pro-
vision can be made for a uniform gauge.
• * * *
The proposed deviation through the eastern
States will bring Sydney no nearer to Port
Darwin than the straight line ; the differ-
ence will be that by the deviation N.S.W.
will only have to build, at its own expense,
about 120 miles of iron road from Bourke to the.
Queensland border, whereas to connect with the.-
straight Port Darwin-Port Augusta line it will
have to build about 340 miles at its own cost
via Broken Hill. The eastern deviation will
bring Melbourne no closer to Port Darwin than
would the straight track; its advantage will
be that the eastern route will directly connect
Broken Hill and western N.S.W. in general with
Melbourne, and bring some of their trade there.
The eastern deviation will bring Tasmania no
nearer to Port Darwin than the straight line ;
the difference will be that the Hobart person,
who wants to try his fortune in the Territory
will go, in one case, by Melbourne and Adelaide,
and in the other he will go at greater length by-


Melbourne and Broken Hill. And the eastern
deviation will make both Adelaide and Perth
more remote from the Territory than by the
straight cheap road.
* * * *
The advocates of the eastern deviation all say,
for very shame’s sake, that “South Australia
must be treated liberally.” S.A. took over the
administration of the Territory when ic was aban-
doned by N.S.W. and Queensland and was in
danger of becoming a nigger-infested appanage of
India or some such place. The principal con-
dition which the British Government imposed as
the price of the definite cession of the Territory to
Australia was the building of a railway from
Adelaide to the northern coast. The most profit-
less section of that compulsory line —the one
through the desert—is already built, and if the
Commonwealth proposes to take over the northern
province and construct a new and wholly different
line through a larger and more remarkable desert
further east, it is admitted that it must offer S.A.
some compensation for the work already done.
Therefore, if one takes the case as it affects Tas-
mania—that being a State which neither trans-
continental railway will traverse, and which can,
therefore, take an unbiassed view of the case—
this is how the Island will be affected if the
eastern deviation is adopted :
(1) It will be brought no nearer to the Territory ; in
fact, it may find itself rather further away.
(2) It will have to pay more as its share of the cost of
the new railway, for the line will be longer and more ex-
pensive.
(3) It will have to pay its share towards the proposed
“liberal treatment” of S.A. for the abandonment of the
partly finished line to Oodnadatta.
(4) It will have an interest in the port at one end of the
line only instead of in the port at each end.
(5) Instead of having an interest in a Federal line
which runs from sea to sea, it will only have an interest in
a Federal line which starts from the sea and ends in a dry
wilderness. The rest of the track and the profits will be
State property'.
(6) Instead of the Territory, of which about one-twenty-
second part will belong to Tasmania, being opened up by
the railway through its whole length, so as to turn it into
a paying asset for Tasmania’s profit, the eastern devia-
tion will cut across a corner of it, and spend the rest of
its energy in opening up the eastern States—partly at
Tasmania’s expense.
That is how the eastern deviation will affect Tas-
mania. Its effect on Westralia and S.A. will be
worse. Its effect on Victoria will be just the
same, barring the one compensating fact that it
may bring a certain amount of the Broken Hill
trade to Melbourne. Its effect on N.S.W.
won’t be widely different. In fact, when
the mileage is carefully measured it is difficult to
see that the proposed eastern deviation will
profit anybody outside Queensland. And if the
deviation happens to be connected with the port
at the mouth of the Macarthur River, in the
eastern part of the Territory and on the western
side of the Gulf of Carpentaria, it might be rather
a dreadful surprise to Queensland than a benefit.
For in that case it would probably take the trade
of Cloncurry and Longreaeh to the Macarthur,
and leave Queensland with nothing for its pains
but a fairly large element of astonishment and a
stomach filled with the east wind.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Capital with an Alleged Rainfall of
47 Inches.
The struggle to fix the Australian capital at Yass-
berra, where it will be within the business radius
of the Sydney morning papers, is naturally an
effort which has a lot of influence behind it. It
not only has the influence of the Sydney morning
papers behind it, but it has also the influence of
all those N.S.W. politicians who feel that their
political existence depends to a great extent on
the support and approval of the Sydney morn-
ing papers. And as most of the politician g
from the other States ceased to take any
very active interest - in the matter when
Queensland, Victoria, Tasmania, S.A., the
Northern Territory and Westralia were all
disqualified as possible sites for the capital, the
matter was mostly left, so far as any real fighting
went, to those N.S.W. politicians who feared to
offend the Sydney morning papers; though one or
two outsiders departed from the usual habit of
indifference and took sides in one cause or another.
About the most remarkable of these is Senator
Russell of S.A., whose frenzy for Canberra has
led him into some remarkable statements. The
following, which appeared last Thursday in a
Sydney daily paper, is a fair sample :
Senator W. Russell (S.A.) championed the claims of
Yass-Canberrn. He had visited the district on several
occasions, and was still of opinion that it was the right
place for the Federal capital. The rainfall in the catch-
ment area of the Cotter River averaged 38 inches—in 1906
47 inches were recorded. He wished to do his duty con-
scientiously, and would vote for the Bill; but, first of all,
he desired to make a certainty that the water supply was
adequate. He had seen the Cotter River, and it seemed
to be about as wide as the Torrens. The lowest rainfall
was 24.5 inches.
It seems natural to inquire where Senator
Russell got these figures. He doesn’t appear to
have explained. There are four meteorological
stations close around the Canberra capital site
and around the Cotter River, and the rainfalls of
these four places are recorded in the N.S.W.
“Statistical Register.” They are Cooma, Quean-
beyan, Yass and Lake George. In the “ Statisti-
cal Register” for 1907 (the latest issue) the
following figures are given ;
Lowest year’s Highest year’s
Rainfall in Rainfall in
inches. inches.
Cooma .... 13 16
Lake George 19 29
Queanbeyan .. 11 25
Yass 13 28
Also the Federal Government Meteorologist re-
ports that the average rainfall of the Canberra
district is 23in. Therefore it is natural that one
should ask Senator Russell, of S.A., where he
found his average of 38in. rainfall on Cotter Creek,
and where he discovered the alleged 47in. rainfall
of 1906. No such figures appear in the N.S.W.
official statistics. They don’t appeal: in any
official statistics. They are flatly contradicted by
all the published statistics. There is no ex-
planation as to where the so-called figures were
published, as to who invented them, or as to why
they weren’t invented earlier ; and no one seems
to explain what person named Smith or Biuggs
measured the alleged downpour, and whether he
did it with a kerosene tin or a flower-pot. Yass-
berra, in fact, seems to be wrapped in mystery all
round. It is a place where some strange hole-
and-corner business is always happening, and
where amazing rainfalls and water supplies that
can’t be discovered in any official records are
always taking place, and where big rivers that
aren’t marked on any map or mentioned in
Statistician Knibbs’ huge list of rivers are always
being discovered. It is a pity that this curiously
furtive Federal capital doesn’t come out from its
cavern and declare itself more openly.
The Patent Law.
The Foreign-trader’s alarm lest anything should
be manufactured here really amounts to a disease.
It has been proposed that Australia should fall in
line with Britain and most other civilised lands
by enacting that no patent shall hold good in this
country unless the holder arranges, within a
reasonable time, to manufacture the patented
article here, or allows some one else to manu-
facture it on reasonable terms. The multipli-
cation of patents renders some such law abso-
lutely necessary. A patentee is a monopolist. If
a man invents a thing which is so far and away
the best of its kind that a country must use it or
be industrially left behind, he becomes a person
of great power. For instance, if he produced a
new cotton-spinning device which reduced ex-
penses one-half, and patented it in every country,
and then sold his rights for a fabulous sum to
(say) the German Government, and the new holder
was allowed to supply or refuse to supply the
new machinery at pleasure—well, by refusing, it
could transfer all the British and French and
U.S. cotton industry to Germany, and shut up
Lancashire, and make Manchester a waste.
Obviously, to recognise patent rights to that
extent would be absurd, but that sort of thing
was already happening on a small scale ; hence
the new British law, which has already led to the
establishment of a swarm of fresh industries in
the Bull country. But Senator Pulsford and
the S. M. Herald have both implored Australia
not to follow suit. The possibility of Australia
getting some new industries seems to strike them
both as a shocking thing. They say we have so
small a population that in many cases it wouldn’t
pay to make the patented article here, though they
quite fail to mention what valuable article it is of
which so little is used that a population of about
4J millions can’t support even one workshop, or
what article it is that is manufactured on such an
enormous scale that unless at least 5,000,000
people are supplied from one workshop the
place will be run at a loss. A great
yearning to know the name of that article fills
this paper’s soul, but it doesn’t really believe that
there is any such article ; on the appearance of
things the only trouble with old Pulsford and
the aged Herald is their terror lest Australia
shall make things for itself, and thereby damage
their god, the importer. Pulsford’s other great
argument is that if Australia adopts the proposed
patent law, all the other British colonies may
follow suit, and when St. Helena, St. Lucia and
Malta, for instance, insist on every patentee
putting up a local factory the position will be
absurd and ridiculous. But on the same basis it
is strictly logical to point out that it was an insane
proceeding for Pulsford’s State to build 3400
miles of railway, for all the British colonies might
follow suit, and to put 3400 miles of railway on to
the Falkland Isles would lead to the engine fall-
ing into the sea. And the folly of having any
such paper as the S. M. Herald becomes
plain to the meanest intelligence, for every
British colony might do likewise, and the
Rock of Gibraltar (two square miles) couldn’t
possibly provide advertisements and circulation
for another S. M. Herald: In fact, it seems that
Australia should have left almost everything
undone that it has done, lest every other British
dependency should insist on doing the same, for
there is hardly one of our institutions that would
fit, say, Labuan (30 square miles). Labuan
divided into six federated States, with six capitals,
and a Senate, and adult suffrage, and a Rabbit
Act, and six land laws, and Local Govern-
ment, and a Barren Jack reservoir, and a Han-
sard, would be a spectacle for the dogs. Why
didn’t Pulsford realise the danger earlier ? Even
now they may be introducing Queensland’s Prickly
Pear law in Beluchistan, and trying to lay 400
miles of waterpipe amid the swamps of the,&iger
in imitation of the Kalgoorlie water scheme, and
if these things happen all is indeed lost.
A Curse from the Stock Exchange.
This attempt to drag everything into Sydney is
having its inevitable result—not only a block or
dead-lock on the railways, but a growing curse at
the system of State ownership and control. “In
proportion to size of territory, production, trade
and wealth,” says a Sydney Stock Exchange
circular, “ there is probably no other fully
civilised country so backward as N. S. Wales.
That our railways are unequal to the
needs and requirements of the country is
due entirely to our system of State railway
monopoly.” As a matter of fact, N. S. Wales is
one of the most heavily-railwayed countries in the
world ; moreover, the proportion of railway to
population has increased enormously during the
last few years. In 1880 there was one mile of
railway to every 881 people; at the end of last
year there was one mile to every 456. France even
now has only one mile to every 1587, Germany
one to every 1721, and Great Britain one to every
1912. Spain, Russia, Italy, Japan, India and other
countries have much less railway accommodation
—Japan has only one mile to every 9910 people.
Also, in proportion to area, N. S. Wales is heavily
railwayed. Canada has one mile of line to every
168 sq. miles of territory ; Russia one to every
212 ; but N. S. Wales has one to every 89. These
are comparisons that can be made definitely. But
“production,” “trade” and “ wealth ” are matters
largely of guesswork ; and the common plan of
lumping exports and imports and calling it
“trade” is ridiculous. Still, even on these
“ trade” figures, N. S. Wales has one mile of rail-
way for every £22,700 of “ trade,” while
Great Britain has only one for every £46,450. It
is one thing, however, to be heavily railwayed
and another to be well railwayed. If N. S.
Wales had compelled the big lazy landlord
to make room for the small energetic settler it
wouldn’t have seen its expensive railways run-
ning for hundreds of miles through country from
which it gets no traffic except a few bales of wool;
and if the crowd which has been responsible for
this lunacy hadn’t also struggled to drag every-
thing to or through Sydney, the country
wouldn’t have seen millions wasted in rail-
ways that crawl unnecessary hundreds of miles
to the bloated city instead of making a bee-line for
the nearest outport. So N. S. Wales isn’t as well
supplied with railways as it ought to be, in spite
of the fact that already every 456 people have to
carry the interest-burden of one mile of line,
while in Great Britain that much weight has
only to be borne by 1912 people. But in-
stead of cursing “our system of State
railway monopoly” for the shortcomings, the
Antl-Sosh crowd ought to heave all its curses
at itself for having stood in the way of a progres-
sive land-tax, and for having been the head and
tail and most of the middle of the gang whose
object in life has been to further bloat an already
over-bloated city.

Certain Laborites and. Their Contempt for
the People.
The Yassberra Federal capital site struggles
along in the Senate by small majorities; last
week it just escaped being thrown into the scrap-
heap by two votes. It is expected to do better in
the Reps.—in fact, its passage there is supposed
to be easily assured. After that—well, after that,
it will still be competent for the country to reject
the whole political swindle at the general election
and to send the gang of political swindlers who
engineered the job back into oblivion ; but un-
fortunately, in the absence of a referendum,
it is difficult for the country to clearly express
its views. The Yassberra business has been,
from first to last, a high-handed attempt
to prevent the people having any effective voice
in their own affairs. The original selection of a
capital was made in 1904. The people had a
chance to announce their approval or disapproval,
so far as anything can be definitely announced at
a general election, in 1906, and apparently they
approved—at least they showed no definite sign
to the contrary. Yassberra wasn’t even men-
tioned to the people then; in fact the place
wasn’t invented in those days. Then after a
long series of intrigues the Congested Population
Party came temporarily on top, and the new site,
about which the people had never been consulted,
got a small major.ty. Straightway there was a
frenzied hurry to get it fixed beyond recall —if such
a thing were possible—before the people could
have a say. The fact that the Tory crowd was in
that movement is nothing, for the Tory can never
quite realise that the people should have a say in
anything; but the fact that certain Labor men,
who profess to believe in the self-governing
rights of the people and in the referen-
dum and so forth, did their utmost also to
deprive the people of any effective voice
in one of the greatest questions which can ever
come before the Federal Parliament was an act of
common, sordid, disgraceful backyard treachery
which should be remembered against them to the
last day of their lives. The most utterly shame-
less of these apostates was W. M. Hughes, who
openly clamored for a hasty settlement lest the
people should have an opportunity to condemn the
whole wretched job— Hughes, who said that
“the special circumstances which made for the
selection of Yass-Uanberra might never occur
again ” (in other words, that the electors, if they
weren’t muzzled, might never let these circum-
stances occur again), and demanded that the
business should be rushed through without wait-
ing for a referendum or an election or any
other expression of the popular will. Other
Labor members who have a tine old Tory con-
tempt, when it suits them, for the referendum
and the popular will are J. C. Watson and
Senator Russell of S.A.; and there are
a few more whose names will probably
appear when the division is taken in the
House of Reps. Every democratic elector
should make a note of these members who have
turned dog in such a brazen fashion on the first
principle of democracy, and should write that note
large and nail it up conspicuously. Men of
that kind are not the sort that any democrat
should vote for. They are unsafe. The next
time they are struck by the complete
unimportance of the people they may curtail
the franchise suddenly between elections, or
abolish the White Australia policy, or some trifle
of that sort. In a sense, the people are wholly, at
the mercy of their members. At any ordinary
general election these times colored immigration
is no more mentioned than Yassberra was in
1906, for the White Australia policy is regarded
as a thing which hardly needs mentioning now.
Yet it would only require a few people like
Hughes, Russell and J. C. Watson to turn their
coats, and there might be 100,000 Chinese or
Hindus in Australia before another election gave
the people a chance to say things. In that case
also the matter might be hurried on because, to
paraphrase the words of W. M. Hughes, “the
special circumstances which made for colored
immigration might not occur again.”
The Bank Clerk.
The N.S. W. Clerks’ Union has been saying things
—and saying them none too soon—about the
wretchedly-paid bank clerk, an individual who
has probably' a worse outlook than any other
equally well-dressed and shiny-hatted person in
the Commonwealth. His life, as a general thing,
is genteel poverty before he marries and genteel
starvation after he marries. Among the rank
and file of the banking profession an average rise
of 100 per cent, wouldn’t be too much to meet the
case. Certainly a large proportion of banks, as
matters stand at present, can't afford to pay
double the present salaries; but it is mainly’
waste, muddle and incompetence which create
the impossibility. The bank clerk is the victim
partly of greed and partly of insane competi-
tion. In quite small, ordinary towns, where
the business could support two well-paid
bank managers, two well-paid staffs of clerks
and the rent of two offices, there are four
office rents to pay, four ill-paid managers,
and four hungry and wretched staffs. The story
of the A. J.S. Bank might be cited as a case in
point. In its roaring days its great ambition was
apparently to run level with the far bigger and
more solid Commercial of Sydney. The Com-
mercial spread pretty recklessly in those
times, and whenever it went to a new town-
ship the A.J.S. was apt to do likewise.
What were called “ banking facilities ” sim-
ply rained on the small towns 20 years ago.
The clerks in the less fortunate and more bubbly
institutions had to be ill-paid, for the business
wouldn’t stand good salaries; though even if it
could have stood good salaries the clerks would
almost certainly have been ill-paid just the same.
The shareholders didn’t make very much real
profit even with all the poor payment. These
were the days when a mouldering little town like
Mittagonghad three banks, and Penrith had three,
and other places.were likewise, and when almost ipiy
kind of business was taken because the superfluous
institutions had to do something to justify their
existence. When the smash came branch banks
were closed wholesale, officials were discharged in
droves, and salaries were cut still lower to make
up for past losses. The bank clerk’s luck has
been out in good times and in bad. So wretched
is his pay that rigid restrictions are made to pre-
vent him marrying and helping along the birth-
rate till he has reached an almost impossible
salary, because, if he married at a reasonable
age, the burden of a wife and family would drive
him to embezzlement. No parson, preaching
about “Race Suicide,” has, to The Bulletin’s
knowledge, alluded to this aspect of the case ; yet
the evil is so gigantic that in one big head office
there are said to be only three clerks who are
qualified by their salaries to marry. When a
large bank runs its affairs on an average salary list
of £217 per annum for each office—head office and
branches all included —the fair inference is l that
not more than one in 50 of its officials can possibly
get a wage at which even a highly economical
form of matrimony is possible. For all this
misery there is only one possible remedy
—to materially increase salaries on plain
Wages Board principles and let the manage-
ments adjust themselves to the new conditions.
It is no more impossible in their case than in that
of the manufacturers who also assured the world at
large, when Wages Boards were first invented,
that the results would be blank ruin. In fact,
that remark about blank ruin may be taken as read
in all such cases.

The Possible Exodus of Holman.
“ Henry Horsecollar ” speaks:—
State-member Holman, of N.S.W., has broadly inti-
mated that if the Labor party carries the principle of
compulsory education much further he will leave his old
political associates for good and all. At present this
country has compulsory grammar and history and
geography, and even to some extent compulsory gym-
nastics and physical training. Now it is proposed to
compulsorily teach shooting and military drill, and
Holman reckons that if this education business doesn’t
stop it is time for a pale, serious lawyer to quit. After
all, it might not be a bad thing for the party if he did
quit. Speaking seriously, it is difficult to imagine
any progressive party that wouldn’t be the better
for losing Holman. He is hopelessly Conservative
and unprogressive, though he mistakes himself
for an advanced Liberal. He is crowded with old
book-learning taken out of the text-books of his
student days—crowded so full that he hasn’t room
for a new idea. Holman is badly afflicted with the
English superstition—the old Podsnap variety of English.
He is firmly convinced that Freetrade is the right thing
because it is English, and that no Englishman would
stand “food-taxes.” It is no use to turn up last year’s
official records, and show that the Englishman does stand
food-taxes, for Holman has read in the old books that
Freetrade is the absolute opposite of food-taxation. He
is convinced, because he has read it in the literature of
Podsnap, that comp jlsorv military training leads to war,
that it (combined with Protection) has loaded “conti-
nental nations” with imposts from which happy England is
exempt, and that it is a ruinous proceeding which takes
the people away from productive work. And it isn’t the
least use to show Holman that Britain has more wars
than any other European State; that it is, per head, with
one possible exception, the most heavily-taxed country
in Europe; that it charges the community more
per head under its tariff alone than the Unitedi
States, and more than any other European coun-
try, barring perhaps France; that it has almost the
biggest military and naval expenditure bill in all the
world, and that its voluntary military and naval system
keeps more men permanently away from reproductive
work than the universal military education method of
most of the despised and contemned Continental nations.
These are the facts of to-day, and are not to be found in
the old, unchangeable Freetrade text-books. Holman
never seems to realise that the world keeps moving, and
that the ideas which seemed to be immovably based on the
circumstances of 1870 may be hopelessly burst on the evi-
dence of the circumstances of 1909. He wrote once, I
observed, to The Bulletin to point out that the stupend-
ous emigration from Germany proved that Protection was
disastrous in its effects. Of course, German emigra-
tion has almost wholly ceased under Protection,
but Holman was back 40 years or so among the
figures of Germany’s dead old Freetrade past. He
didn’t know that things had changed; he didn’t
know that they could change. In Australian politics he
seemingly doesn’t know that a new Power has arisen in
the Pacific, and that our old safe isolation is gone. He
doesn’t know that with the rise of Germany the old safe
reliance on Britain isn’t what it was. He hardly knows
yet that Federation has happened. He is a State-Frighter
of the narrowest sect of the Pharisees—the old Cabbage
Tree Mob of the ’6o’s and ’7o’s—the people who believed
that N.S. W. was all Australia, and that Sydney was all
N.S. VV. He is not an Australian, and never will be one ;
he is not a New South Waler : he is merely a Sydneyite
and mostly a cityite—his political sympathies, though he
probably doesn’t know it, hardly extend as far as Botany.
A youngish, very-old man, with an ill-fitting varnish of
new Democracy, is William Holman—brilliant and alert,
but narrow to an almost incredible degree except where
the L„< ty programme compels him to widen out, in theory
at all events. And now he thinks of leaving the
Labor party because he believes he sees in it a
leaning towards compulsory military education. He is
doubtless aware that the right to call out the adult male
population in time of great national danger is regarded
as the absolute prerogative of every Government, but he
holds that if the Australian has to go out he should, in
the blessed name of Liberty, go out untrained and un-
armed like a sheep to the slaughter, for that theory is to
be found in some of the venerable tomes that Holman has
in his head. A look at the outer world and at the circum-
stances of the hour might tend to create a doubt upon
the accuracy of this view, but Holmax either never looks
at the outer world or looks at it with an eye which sees*
nothing.
The Children of the Light.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rpHE LIGHTKEEPER at JERVIS BAY offers com-
fortable Home .to Lady or Gentleman wanting a
change, in return for primary instruction to four
young children. R.S., Herald Office.— Advt.
When night is on the deep
The Light throws out its arm
To shield the crews that sleep
And keep them from all harm ;
But all the ships pass by
The empty harbor mouth
To prosper ports that lie
To Northward and to South.
The children of the Light
That like bright Knowledge beams,
Watch ever from the height
The ships that pass like dreams
For ever past the Bay,
And wonder where they go.
(The cities far away
The children do not know.)
The brilliant, wheeling Light,
The dark and idle Bay,
The lone, surf-haunted night,.
The long and careless day,
This is the children’s life
Above the restless sea,
While cities in hot strife
Blot out their destiny.
The empty harbor waits
Before the thriving lands ;
While by its open gates
Commerce with greedy hands
Between the rivals steers
The riches that by right
•Should build a city’s piers
Below the lonely Light.
But there no city rears
Its towers unto the skies;
There Desolation stares
With wild and vacant eyes;
No temples, schools or marts
Are seen. ’Tis but a rude
Prison for brains and hearts,
That surf-bound solitude.
The white arm of the Light
Flies seaward to the Ships,
And sweeps the heaving night
For Knowledge—but it slips
For ever out of reach.
Now answer! Cannot we
Learn from —if we can’t teach —
These children by the sea ?
n.s.w.
E. FRANCIS.


In pursuance of the Closer Settlement policy of
the N.S.W. Government, another estate has been mopped
up ; this time the Ooreen and Back Paddock portions of
the Gunatnbil Estate, in the Corowa district, about 18 to
40 miles from the town of the same name. It has been
divided into 63 farms, of an average area of 615 acres,
worth, roughly, £2456, in a district well suited to growing
wheat, oats, vines and fruit trees. All portions of the
estate are well within the radius of the Murray River irri-
gation scheme. The terms are 5 per cent, deposit on the
capital value (which includes the improvements), and the
balance payable by yearly instalments of 5 per cent., in-
cluding principal and interest at 4 per gent. The rainfall
is about 18 inches ; 23 of the 63 farms already contain
tanks, and all are fairly free from rabbits.*
There is no finer cigar than that made of the
tobacco grown in the Philippine Islands. He who has
not enjoyed the flavor of a good Manila cheroot has not
yet learned to live. He has merely existed. But when
one remembers that Manila cigars are made by nearly
naked women in factories which are not under the super-
vision of any Government inspectors to ensure cleanliness,
it is apt to spoil the enjoyment of the Manila. The States
Tobacco Co., an Australian concern which has its fac-
tories in Australia, has determined that the white Aus-
tralian shall have a good Manila eig'ar that is beyond all
reproach, made by a clean Australian in a clean,
wholesome Australian factory, "under the juris-
diction of clean Australian factory legislation. So
the superintendent of the co. hied him away to
Manila, and spent much time hovering around the
factories where the reeking Filipino makes the smokes
of the connoisseurs. He went, he saw, he noted, and en-
gaged four of the best Filipino cigar-makers to aid in
instructing the Australian workers how to make the
various shapes peculiar to the real Manila. Then he came
back, bringing with him, not only the four cigar-makers,
but 100,0001 b. of Manila leaf from the famous Isabella
district, which corresponds to the Old Belt in Virginia.
This is easily the largest purchase of cigar leaf ever made
on Australian account. So henceforth the Australian who
wants to smoke Manila cigars made in Australia should be
careful to inquire for “ The Gem of the East,” and then
he will know he has got the best Manila in the world.*

The “ Two Party System ” Again Disgraces
Itself.
A courLE of smashing' arguments against the out-
of-date two-party form of government materialised
last week ; and they were voiced, oddly enough,
by the identical Sydney papers which laud this
bit of political foolishness most enthusiastically.
Seven weeks ago a general election occurred in
Tasmania. Elliott Lewis formed a Fused Ad-
ministration, and a cry went up in Tory circles
that, henceforth, everything in the Apple Garden
was going to be lovely owing to “ the establish-
ment of a rational two-party system on the Eng-
lish model.” Then Ewing seceshed from the
Lewis party, and Jack? Evans and others found
themselves in disagreement with its financial pro-
posals, and as an immediate result the Lewis
Ministry burst with a loud report, and in the
pained words of S. M. Herald (20/10/’O9) :
It may be, now, that Tasmania will have to go through
another general election before she reaches solid ground
again.
The Herald in deploring this happening unlooses
a lot of froth about the “ party disloyalty” which
brought about so disgraceful a state of things.
However, what the Herald terms “party dis-
loyalty” may be described with equal, not to say
mor#, justice as “adherence to principle.” If
Evans and the rest disapprove of Lewis’s
finance, what law of ethics counsels them to be
liars and say they approve of it? Neither in
Tasmania or any other civilised part of
the world, these days, is it possible to
get together a dozen thinking men who
agree on all political subjects. To take a case in
point, the Freetrade anti-Socialist is liable to
be a convinced anti-Defenceite. The Cabinet
wherein he earns his comfortable remuneration is
impelled by the force of public opinion to go back
on its previously expressed views as to the advan-
tage of retaining the volunteer system and intro-
duce a Universal Military Training Bill. The
Freetrade anti-Socialist is straightway faced with
the alternatives of either being a liar and turning
what he has been wont to call “militarist,” or
being honest at the cost of smashing up the
Ministry, throwing his party out of office, putting
the country to the expense of a general election,
losing his own salary, maddening his party news-
papers, and generally playing Hades with the
established order of events. All this uproar
bitterness, dishonesty and needless expenditure
of cash might be averted by the adoption of the
Elective Ministry arrangement, and that is the
only way, under present conditions, that these
undesirable things are going to be averted. Yet
another aspect of the two-party idiocy was pre-
sented by the Sydney Telegraph (21/10/’O9) in a
leader entitled “Abusing the Public Patience.”
The article had reference to the notorious difficulty
private M’s.P. have with their Bills ; and the
urgent matters on which' private members are
so often driven, in desperation, to attempt to
legislate. Thus ingenuously, in the above regard,
did the Telegraph give the two-party show
away:—
Many of these so-called private Bills affect large public
interests. Before certain works can be started it may be
necessary to get an Act passed, but the prospect of doing
so is so dispiriting that men who would embark in enter-
prises employing labor and developing the country often
turn away, and so great opportunities are unused.
Matters of very grave importance, as affecting the social
life of the community, are also frequently the subject of
attempted legislation by private members, but failure
awaits them from the same cause. The Government of
the day is hampered by the necessity of keeping its party
always solid, which only permits it to deal with a limited
number of questions, so that certain Bills, which a
majority of the House, IP IT COULD GET AT THEM
FREE PROM PARTY ENTANGLEMENTS, might be
willing to pass, can never be passed unless taken up by
.private members.
Here is a frank admission that one of the effects
•of the vaunted two-party system is to prevent the
public from getting the sole thing, that it pays its
representatives to give it—namely beneficial laws.
It is a confession that Ministries under the exist-
ing arrangement provide the country not with
the measures the country needs, but only with
the ones that are not calculated to imperil the
existence of the party. In short, the in-
terests of the nation go by the board
that the party may flourish, and that those
holding Ministerial jobs in it may draw their
screws with regularity and despatch. If Aus-
tralia’s only aim in enduring the expense of two
Federal Houses and 12 State ones is to provide
jobs for a horde of temporising opportunists who,
eight times out of ten, are only game to do the
things, legislatively speaking, which don’t matter,
ic will continue in the old ruck. But if it wants
vital measures—the sort that stir people to a
frenzy of disapprobation or approval, as the case
may be—that is to say, the sort that are best
worth while—it will adopt the Elective Ministry
scheme without delay.

The Vic. Railways Standing Committee has re-
ported officially on the much-discussed railway
across the Western plains; and recommends that
a line, 100 miles long, be built from Maroona (on
the Ararat-Hamilton line, just against Ararat) to
Gheringhap (close to Geelong). This line, for fully
90 miles, will go through nothing but big stations,
the holdings of the western sheep kings, almost
the whole of which are fit for closer settlement.
Here and there a few blocks have been sold to
farmers, who are growing wheat; but the bulk of
the land is still as God made it, or almost so.
Wherever this line passes, and for at least 10
miles on either side of it, the land will get an
added value of from £1 10s. to £3 per acre. So,
in fear and trembling, the standing committee re-
commends its construction :
Conditionally on those whose land will be enhanced in
value by the construction of the railway handing over to
the Government, free of charge, the land required for
railway purposes. . . .
Also—
Conditionally that, in the event of the railway . . . not
earning an annual revenue of £23,000 during a period of
20 years from the date of the opening of the line, those
whose laud will be enhanced in value . . . shall be rated
on the acreage basis. .. so as to make good any deficiency
in the £23,000.
It is a fairly cheap proposition for the big land-
lord. The hundred miles of railway is estimated
to cost £418,270. If it adds, on an average, £2
per acre to the value of the land for three miles
on each side of it—and it will probably add that
value for 10 miles instead of three—it will make
the landlord a present, at the country’s expense,
of £772,000. And he is requested to promise that
if the line doesn’t earn £460,000 in the first 20
years he will make up the difference. The differ-
ence will probably be nil, or Bd., or somethin#
like that; but even if it is the whole £400,000, the
landlord will still be £312,000 in pocket. In fact,
he will be a lot more than that in pocket, for the
£772,000 will happen now, and the payment of the
Bd. or £460,000 or whatever it is, will be spread
over 20 years, O yes, the landlord is all right!
