Newcastle is decked out these days with placards
announcing that the Sydney morning papers will
shortly begin to arrive earlier than they used to.
For a long time the Sydney morning papers have
been of opinion that the Railway Commissioners
should provide a very early train to Newcastle—
even one so early that, if the Sydney papers went
by it, they would arrive in time to cut into the
circulation of the local morning paper in the coal
city. Now it is always difficult for a Commis-
sioner to kick long and hard against the city press
in a supremely centralised State like N.S.W.—
especially a Commissioner who has had the worst
kind of luck with his McMylek hoist, and who is
in great trouble because of the block on his rail-
ways. These things are calculated to give him
the tired feeling. Certainly the very early
train hasn’t been forthcoming, but there is
to be an earlier one than of old. And
probably the very early train will come later on,
for it is the business of the State railways to
bring everything to Sydney and to supply the
country with everything from Sydney. So, when
there is a prosperous paper anywhere within 100
or 200 miles of Sydney, it becomes the obvious
duty of the Railway Department to start a train
so early at the public expense, and run it so
furiously at the public cost, that the Sydney
dailies may get to the scene of action in time to
compete on level terms against that local paper.
For the existence of any prosperous local paper
within easy reach of Sydney is a sin against the
doctrine of centralisation, and if the Railway
Commissioner doesn’t recognise that fact, he has
obviously failed to grasp the true inwardness of
the centralisation policy, and ought to have the
thing explained to him.
As The Bulletin goes to press comes the glad
news that that eternal litigant, Premier and
Attorney-General Wade, has fallen in again.
One Giddy put in 30 years’ service in the N.S.W.
railways. When he retired he was supposed to
be entitled in the usual way to a month’s pay for
each year of service. But on some obscure tech-
nical point he was offered instead a month’s pay
for each of the first. 20 years of his service
(£454 in all), and in place of the £l4O due on
account of the remaining years the Ministry insult-
ingly offered him 7d. Now the Full Court has
decided that the Wade Government’s law is bad
once more, and judgment has been given against
it for the entire amount. It looks a shockingly
mean and petty-minddd case such as only a shock-
ingly mean and small-souled Government would
be capable of.
+
If the Tory crowd in Victoria doesn’t stop crying
over the “ poor farmer,” his crops will rot before
long. It is Murray’s proposed land-tax that has
started the downpour. The poor farmer is going
to be ruined. Of course, there is an exemption,
so that the poor farmer will either have to pay
nothingat all orelse very little; but Mr. Employers’
Federation Blackwood told the Treasurer the
other day that the financial institution which
holds the mortgage will tell the poor farmer to
pay up; so the poor farmer will be hurt
even though the tax may not touch him
directly. This point is liable to cause the
poor farmer some concern. But he really
needn’t worry. If the private Cohen won’t
keep his mortgage going, the public Cohen
will. And, moreover, if the overlooked State
Land Bank takes over the mortgage it will prob-
ably charge him less interest. So the poor farmer
can go on eating his pumpkin pie in peace. With
the State Land Bank waiting for customers in the
background, the “financial institutions” are not
at all likely to empty him out, and if they did
they would very likely be doing him a good turn.

“The Bells,” the hallowed old drama
made famous by dead Henry Irving, as revived
at Sydney Palace suffers somewhat bv the scratch
scenery provided by the management ; but, as the
play has only a matinee lien on the stage, an ela-
borate setting is perhaps out of the question.
E. Hoggan-Armadale, a well-seasoned and robust
English actor, grapples with the part of Mathias,
which, according to the programme, he has played
for 800 or 8000 nights in Great Britain. As the
impoverished young Mathias, the overseas player
is at a disadvantage by reason of his years—for he
is something over 21. The 10 years’ interval
which elapses between Act 1 and the pro-
logue wherein he murders the travelling Jew,
brings him to the haunted, grev-haired stage,
and as the haggard Mathias who hears
the jingling of ghostly sleigh-bells and dreams of
vengeance until he goes mad, E. Hoggan-
Armadale does his heavy scoring with a generous
old-school hand. But he is more effective in the
occasional quiet passages; one or two of his
minor-key speeches—more particularly that to his
prospective son-in-law—are excellently rendered.
Miss Nellie Fergnsson is more successful as Mar-
garet the young wife than as Margaret the
matron ; Kenneth Hunter makes a satisfactory
Christian and bears his uniform and testimonials
bravely; Reginald Goode, as M. Bussey, the
young but villainous landlord who would turn his
tenants out in the snow, is more Goode than
Bussey ; his unlawful suggestions to Margaret are
voiced in the gentlest tones, and he flinches as he
utters them. When he is ordered out by the over-
heated Mathias he goes gladly, with a half-hearted
show of baffled fury. This player is plainly an
incurable hero. The Father Herman of J. P.
O'Neill raises the question: What proportion of
the population of Alsace-Lorraine is of Irish
descent? At times this scribe trembled lest he
should invite poor, haunted Mathias to join him
in a jig or to help him sing “The Wearin’ of the
Green.’’ Mr. O’Neill has a cheerful habit of
almost singing his lines, and what in another
would be merely a plain invitation to have a
drink with him becomes a loud aria with trills in
the middle of it. Most of the other parts are
fairly done, and on the whole it has to be
admitted that the old story is moderately well
presented.

Sydney Palace will get its divorce from
the Wrong Man after the performance on Friday
night, October 29. On Saturday evening the
public will be invited to shed tears over “ East
Lynne” at popular prices. The woman who
feels inclined for an emotional bolt, or who has
grief to spare, need not weep alone if she can
contain herself until Saturday, October 30. And
after that there are five more nights when Woe
will prevail, and the damp handkerchief of Sor-
row and Bereavement be welcomed at the box-
office.

Of all the modern English dramas “A
Village Priest ” breathes most of the remorseless
spirit of ancient Greek tragedy. The Wheel of
Fate is shown in all its inexorableness, and the
Sin that started with an almost imperceptible
homicide goes rolling amok down the years, and
spreading as it rolls and cursing at large as it
spreads. As the abbe says, “The thing goes on.”
It goes till it even crashes up against the awful
impervious respectability of the stodgy widow
D’Arcay. There it stops ; even Fate is shattered
“THE BELLE OF NEW YORK ” AT
SYDNEY HER MAJESTY’S.
Four humorists — Nable, Percy, Lissant and Leslie.
against the invincible complacency of the
fat old lady who stands behind her barri-
cade of platitude and defies, quite uncon-
sciously, God, Man, Devil and Circumstance.
“ The Priest ” is always an intellectual treat, but
with Titheradge in the name part and Cyril
Mackay as Armand D’Arcay, it is a snack for the
immortals. The show is materially helped out by
Miss Griniston and Miss Murray as the Comtesse
and Marguerite de Tremeillan, respectively. As
Jeanne, the convict’s daughter, Ethel Warwick
has not much chance, but her last moan as the
curtain comes down and her father goes back to
prison to save the feelings of a stolid old lady is a
fine effort. Miss Murray has a profile which,
when backed with hair done in Grecian style, is
going to do a lot of damage in this world, and her
acting is in keeping with her profile. Miss Grim-
ston’s efforts to make herself appear somewhat old
and somewhat withered are the weakest part of her
performance. Writer lastsaw “The Priest” played
with Brough as Jean Torquenie and Joseph Carne
as the abbe. Titheradge makes the abbe much
more shabby—he is almost squalid as compared
with the impersonation of Carne. But he has an
excuse. The Church in France has struck a
mountain of trouble and lost much revenue since
the days of Carne; wherefore the abbe’s clothes
are naturally somewhat more rusty and his head-
gear faded. But there is a simplicity about
Titheradge’s character that was. not in that of
Carne —a simplicity that well becomes this kindly
old man, who is an honorable gentleman first
and a priest afterwards. Whereas Carne made
him a man of much reserve strength, Titheradge
makes him a tottering ancient, strong only in his
determination to do the right thing. “How will
the dear old thing live?” anguished a young
matron at Sydney Royal on Saturday, when Tith.
announced the fact that he had left the Church.
Nobody was worried about the future of Carne’s
abbe. He was quite able to take care of himself.
Miss Temple’s rendering of Madame D’Arcay is
hard to comment on. If she tries to make the"old
lady the incarnation of Stodge, her purpose is
fully attained.

On Saturday there will be a new drama at
the Royal—“ The Cheat,” for the first time in
Australia. It probably has some reference to the
miserably short odds which Moss Palestine is
offering on the stiff horse for the Cup. So
Diogenes of this paper will take his lantern and
go out in search of a dishonest man, and find him
without any difficulty.
** * *
The “Belle of New York” still rings
vigorously at Her Majesty’s (Sydney). All other
bells—including the dinner-bell, the cow-bell, the
church-bell, and the bell which starts Johnson’s
train—have long since taken a back seat. They
are things of no account, and there is nothing in
them, though if they were turned upside down
they might be useful to hold beer. It is difficult
to say exactly what it is that has made
this musical comedy so much more dur-
able than other plays of its kind and
day and generation—plays which started with
equally bright hopes, and are now lying dead by
“THE BELLS” AT SYDNEY PALACE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The prologue shows the Inn on Christmas Eve ;
Outside a paper storm of torn MS.
Is raging. One could easily believe
Some godlike editor was in distress.
Of course, ’tis but the snow provided by
The faithful management; but one and all,
As quarto sheets come drifting from the sky,
Express the wish that they had torn it small.
The young wife of Mathias—he who keeps
The Inn—is moaning sadly o’er her child—
A doll —that in the old wood cradle sleeps
All heedless of the paper storm so wild.
Three thousand francs they must have, else within
Some days they will be cast out in the snow
By him that owns the pub—a man of sin
Who yearned to be the good wife’s noble beau
In times gone by. So much we understand
From his fierce lines when he strides in and taunts
The wretched girl, and makes some underhand
Proposals. But a dim suspicion haunts
The minds of all who listen that he can’t
Mean what he says, for oh ! his voice is mild
As that of any gentle maiden aunt,
And who by maiden aunts was e’er beguiled?
Still, the good wife uprises in hot wrath
And orders him to quit. Then in his arms
He clasps her, and Mathias hurries forth
From the dark wings. (Excursions and alarms).
Repulsed, the villain-landlord staggers out
Into the paper night, leaving in peace
The couple who agree there’s little doubt
They’ll homeless be when run out is the lease.
Later, the sound of silver sleigh-bells breaks
In on their counsels, and an ancient Jew
Arrives with blessings, and of food partakes
Whilst frenziedly Mathias eyes the few
Bags of red gold the careless Jew has cast
Down by his plate, and broods upon his need,
Till he decides the scratch meal is the last
The Jew will ever take. To fetch,his steed
He hurries forth. The Jew, who’s overheard
The dull, financial wailings of the two,
Leaves money with a note. (’Tis most absurd
When you remember he’s a Polish Jew.)
And then Mathias, with a madman’s eye,
Comes in and says the horse is at the door ;
The venerable Jew bids him good-bye,
Departs—and is not heard of any more.
Somewhere off-stage poor wild Mathias snares
The Jew and kills him with the household axe ;
Then to the kiln across the snow he bears
The corse. (The paper snow wipes out his tracks.)
The body safely burnt, homeward he hies
Laden with gold; and straight his joyful wife
Waves the Jew’s gift before his bulging eyes
And thereby curdles all his after life.
That is the prologue. Afterwards two acts
Of wild remorse and ghostly sleigh-bells bear
The slayer’s head towards earth. (In furtive tracts
I’ve read of murderers opprest with care
But feel inclined to doubt if it be so ;
Or else why should the morning papers tell
The public—which seems gratified to know—
“ The murderer slept soundly in his cell”?)
But a grim dream, wherein a travelling quack
Draws from his close-shut lips the story dread,
Tortures his sleep behind a wall of sack.
He wakes and, ’fore the family, falls dead.
The thing most wonderful about the show
Was how Annette —a dainty girl was she—
Developed and grew up from what I know
In Act I. was a doll at one-and-three.
N.S.W.
KODAR.

** * *
Oscar Asche will varnish or dado himself
a pale black on Saturday, and will take his hostile
expression out of the wardrobe and put it on; and
Lily Bravton will suddenly remain her natural
color, and the two of them will play “Othello”
for a limited row of nights. The awful lesson
re the evils of being the childless wife of a bad-
tempered nigger is to start at 7.50, so as
to give it an extra 10 minutes to soak in.
All good White Australians are cordially invited
to attend, and not to weep, for Desdemona’s death
was a blessing in disguise. It made a White
Venice possible. She didn’t survive long enough
to leave a piebald family behind her, and her fate
was a warning which the saddened maids of
Venice took to heart. They buried her with
tombstones at her head and feet, and then went
out and married white men, and nobody with a
countenance like a large polished boot ever led the
armies of the half-drowned republic again.
** * *
Sydney’s Bijou Picture Palace is under-
going enlargement and extension ; but neverthe-
less the management contrives to present the
regular programme on a proper time-table. One
film throws a weird light on the possibilities of
fate. A nicely-dressed gentleman and charming
lady stand by a boxed-in sort of arrangement,
which is discovered to contain eggs. Later on,
the drawers which contained eggs are discovered
to be full of chickens, and the lady can be seen
saying most distinctly, “The darlings!” And
the darlings are nurtured, fostered with tender
care, dosed with medicine to keep off diseases,
fed, petted and brought to maturity. And then
there flashes red on to the screen the vision of
Hell—the shooting flames, the turning spit, the
basting fat. It makes one shudder. What
are we being kept for? At the Glaciarium West
is showing pictures of pigeons, pouters, angels,
tumblers and all the rest of them. Spain, where the
revolution sizzles and the ghost of Ferrer haunts
the mighty, reels past, a gigantic panorama of
sierra.and plain, ancient hamlet and modern city
—beautiful but horribly insanitary. At the
Lyceum Spencer is now giving Shackleton’s
films in with the ordinary picture show ;
and the place is crammed in consequence.
Duplicate films are also being shown in Mel-
bourne Town Hall, and great is the rush to
see. One point about these pictures is their evi-
dent genuineness. Had there been any fake
there would have been more hairbreadth escapes;
but they bear the stamp of authenticity all over
them. The rest of the programme is made up in
true Spencerian style. Cowboys chase alleged
murderers after the manner of the cowboys, and iin
Indian love story suggests that the Yankee did
not make for any more romance when he displaced
his copper-colored brother.
#* * *
Sydney Tivoli sat up and began to
take notice and nourishment on Satur-
day evening last, when the trio of Trapnell
Sisters and their gentle brother George
somersaulted on to the bill. The Trapnells
have broken the ice of dulness which h d
become rather thick at the Riekardian establish-
ment. A large proportion of H. Rickards’ latter
importations have been acrobats; but the four
Trapnells compare favorably even with the whirl-
ing Arabs of some months back. Their per-
formance is novel and smart, and itisreceived with
gratitude for the rest of the programme has few
gleams. Brown and Robinson, the bricklayer-
laborer pair, still persist in singing at the har-
monium instead of building the music warehouse ;
and the Two Bells, Irving Sayles and Fred Bluett
supply moderately refreshing turns. Ronald
George, the young instrumentalist, departs at the
end of the week, and with him go Blake and
Granby. The bill will then either have to lean
up against something fresh or holler for help.

In the round of plays presented at Ade-
laide Royal by the Allan Hamilton Co., “A
Message from Mars ” shines out like a gold tooth
in a nigger’s mouth, and General Desire has
shown his good taste in obtaining several extra
performances of it. It gives George Cross a
chance to be some other sort of hero. The Con-
servatorium is losing Miss Hack, after long years
of teaching the S.A. girl to sing; her farewell
concerts have been the scene of remarkable de-
monstrations. Arthur Otto has also been fare-
welled. (He is going to Europe. She is only
going to marry.) At the Empire are Frank King
and Lucy Lavinia. Other things are mostly
Race Shows by day and Pictures by night.
-*• # * *
“ The Flood Tide ” is making ample
amends for “The Great Rescue ” at the Kings
(Melb.). In some respects this story of the bogus
Denny Thompson and the wealthy crank from
Chili is the best of the Drury Lane dramas Bland
Holt has conferred upon Australia. It is
humorous. It gives a nasty smack to Britain’s
military snobocracy ; but is otherwise destitute of
uneasy virtue in its moral teaching. The climax
is wildly original; the tragic episode of the Flood
has a droll centre-piece ; the side-lights bring
cvnical reflections with them, and a lot of
human interest attaches to the horse-race scene,
which is a specially important consideration
just now. The drama, to be sure, goes
far beyond the limits of the company’s acting
capacity, and the performance looks woefully crude
in many places; but the action is so rapid that
the gravest causes for critical complaint can be
dismissed with a smile.
Bland Holt has somewhat improved his
rough caricature of the principal character ; like-
wise Mrs. Bland, as the level-headed Polly who
pulls the strings of his fraud in the interests
of her genial old dad. Styan, on the other
hand, has grown careless in his handling of
the boozy man from Chili. Maxwell ap-
pears to great advantage in comparison
with a job lot of brother officahs. Brown is the
same old sport as before. Miss Ireland wears the
white wig of a blameful life as the highly unscru-
pulous Baroness, whilst Miss Pollock walks
jauntily and unfeelingly through the interesting
part of the Baroness’s daughter, formerly played
by Miss Ireland aforesaid. Another lady—Miss
Dale—is amateurish in a sentimental part, and
there are about 40 minor characters whose names
are recorded in oases of programme amidst an
awful desert of advertisements. In the scenic
department the horse race and the rising of the
waters are only two of numerous moving inci-
dents. “The Flood Tide” is full enough of
entertaining matter to keep business brisk at the
King’s until further notice.
“THE CATCH OF THE SEASON” AT
HER MAJESTY’S, MELBOURNE.
D. B. O'Connor
(Lord Dundreary).
Andrew Higginson
(Duke of St.
Jermyns).
“The Merry Widow” is to occur once
more at Her Majesty’s (Melb.) on Saturday, the
dainty 7 eccentricity of “ The Catch of the Season ”
being deemed not quite the cheese—not suffi-
ciently pungent in flavor—for Cup time audiences.
The Widow on this occasion is to be Miss
Florence Young, for that virile songstress has
recovered from her motor mishap, and will re-
appear like a Florence refreshed by con valescence.
However, the Widow and her proverbial Hat
have still two nights to wait before bursting into
notice. And those people who have held aloof
from “The Catch” would do well to abandon
their aloofness and seize one or other of the
remaining opportunities by its forelock.
#* * #
The revival of “ Cinderella” at the Royal
supplies about as much novelty as was required to
bring Melbourne back to its old allegiance. The
songs are new without exception—or with one
exception, the personality of Thorley is at least
different from that of the effeminate young man
who played the Prince’s valet a while ago, and
the dances of Tom Payne and Miss Perrin have
been reconstructed. The antics of Thomas, always
an extraordinary feature of the panto., are, in
their latest developments, acutely remarkable.
Also his burlesque of the Apache dance with Miss
Perrin as a sweet girl-bloke who grabs him by the
neckcloth and pulls him about, seems to go one
better than previous burlesques of the same un-
godly pastime. Miss Daisy Belmore looms
heavily in the part of Cinderella’s largest step-
sister, where a more mellow-toned lady used to
roam ; but Cromwell, Brett, Miss Meredro and
Miss Lincoln stand where they have ever stood,
and the spectacle in general, is unchanged, save
for a fresh infusion of limelight or a more tender
expression in the front row of the ballet.
** * *
There are times when the Princess’s
awakens from its slumber and shrieks for patron-
age. Another of those periods is about to begin.
Derby night is to bring Nellie Stewart and cq.
back to the acclamations of Melbourne; and the
first item in a list of revivals will be “Sweet
Kitty Bellairs.” On Wednesday of next week
Sweet Kitty converts herself into Sweet Nell, and
on the Saturday following the same lady, having
dropped some of her self-respect at the Court of
King Charles, will defy the eternal proprieties in
the character of Zaza.
** * *
Wirth’s Circus continues in strong demand
in Melbourne, especially on Saturday nights,
and the roar of the odorous menagerie is
loud in the land. Also the Skating Rink,
next door to the tent, is haunted by a
multitude of restless citizens; and the Pic-
ture shows thereabouts are prospering as usual.
The Circus doesn’t need to call special attention
to its pitch nowadays. Great streams of humanity
flow in that direction from force of habit, and cir-
cumstances are widening the streams.
* * * *-
Melba’s second concert at Melbourne
Exhibition Building proved a brilliantly memor-
able climax to her long-drawn-out tour. The
crowd was even bigger than on the previous
Saturday, when the chairs in the reserve were
less closely packed. The orchestra put in some
better work. Una Bourne, at the piano, surpassed
herself, under the inspiration of the fact that
it was her birthday. Ranalow (for the first
time in Melbourne) rose to a comparatively
giddy height of popularity with his warble of
Stanford’s sea song, “ The Old Superb,” supported
by the male chorus of the Philharmonic Society.
And Melba, her bodice a blaze of diamonds and
emeralds, was the Queen of Song in her most regal
mood. Rossini’s “ Inflammatus,” of course,
brought down the dome, in a showman way of
speaking ; but it is not as a high C
vocalist piercing a grand musical din that
Melba is at her very best. She sang Massenet’s
“ Sevillana,”and the passionate “ Preghiera” from
“LaTosca.” Her duet with the flute in “Lo! Hear
the gentle lark ” was a pure delight. The critical
ear may detect here and there a speck on the
pearly surface of Melba’s voice. It is a little less
supremely beautiful than it was, but the com-
bined effect of voice, singing and singer thrilled
the multitude on Saturday. After the city has
recovered from its Cup fever, Melba will give a
concert in aid of Lady Dudley’s “ district nursing
extension ” scheme. This is dated for Monday,
November 15.
** * *
Amy Castles is to give concerts at Mel-
bourne Town Hall on Thursday and Saturday of
Cup week. She also will sing the “Inflammatus,”
with the Vic. Festival Choir in chorus, and Peter
Dawspn and tenor Nicol will be the other soloists,
as before. When these two carnival concerts are
disposed of, the Castles starts her Victorian coun-
try tour.
*# * *
Olympia, the large iron structure that
forms a forefront to Wirths’ Park (Melbourne), is
to break out again as a Picture house. Its innards
have been removed and replaced with other
fittings that will considerably enhance the value
of the building for cinematograph displays. The
man behind the machine is Mr. King, who will
project the films upon the sheet by means of a
tidv-sized engine. The show is announced to open
on Saturday night next.
** * #
The arrival of Captain Winston’s educated
Sea Lions is bound to quicken the slackened pulse
of business at the Opera House. On their first
appearance last Saturday the uncanny creatures
were said to be feeling the effects of a long train
journey from Sydney, yet they went through their
duties in almost their best manner. Performing
animals as a rule invite the sympathy of thoughtful
citizens. A dog on his hind legs or a cat
standing on her head is a joyless quadruped ;
but a sea lion revelling in his natural sense of how
to balance something on his nose is a legitimate
matter for amazement. The clever Australian
Wheelers are cycling some more at the Opera
House, also Walter Emerson is revealing his
abnormal height, and colored brother Johnson is
behaving much the same as Irving Sayles. Gwen-
nie Hasto dances rather cleverly, and Bertha
Stuart gives strange renderings of familiar ballads.
Bertha, however, is about to sob herself to another
State, and Franco Piper, a juggling banjoist,
will join the programme when she has gone.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“ D.” : I see that J.C. W. has engaged Charles Wyndham
for a season in Australia. He will, no doubt, have a suc-
cess of curiosity, but I warrant that that is all he will
have. As with women, so with actors ; if we have loved
them when they were young and beautiful, we go on in
the old w r ay, more or less, when they are aged and worn
and tired. Londoners have known Wyndham all their
lives. I don’t suppose there is any regular playgoer
of Bullopolis who hasn’t seen Wyndham’s “ David Gar-
rick ” half a dozen times at the least. Londoners never
tire of “ David Garrick ” with Wyndham in the name
part. “Garrick” is to Wyndham’s Co what “ The Belle
of New York ” is to a musical comedy combination—-a
thing to be revived when business gets slack, and an ab-
solutely certain draw whenever and wherever it is staged.
But Australians will see Wyndham play this romantic
role stripped of the glamor of years. They will see an
old, old man (6S and beyond his years), with a face fur-
rowed with a thousand wrinkles and the gait and voice of
patriarchy (excuse the word —I made it myself). He
doesn’t convince any more as the debonair, chivalrous
young mime who made a public exhibition of him
self to prove his great love. Wyndham plays
middle-aged characters incomparably—cynical, genial,
good-hearted worldlings with stacks of excellent advice
to offer those of the company who meditate a false social
step. In speeches of admonition to these foolish ones his
curious booming voice is heard to great advantage.
Mostly, in the pieces he affects, he winds up, after spill-
ing much wise counsel upon the bowed heads of all
parties, by himself marrying the most attractive female
present. But he is now too advanced in years even for
an impersonator of middle life, and the portrayal of
extreme age is out of his line. As aforesaid, I don’t think
those Australians who haven’t known him in his prime
will be much impressed with Wyndham for all his newly-
garnered knighthood.
“ Ita”: On 15/10/’O9 at Melb. Waxworks, Sacco Homann
began his fast of 53 days “ in defence of certain theories
and for the honor of holding the world’s fasting record.”
The honor he can have if it gives him any joy to be classed
with the club-swinging and piano-playing fiends. But I
should like to know what the “certain theories” are.
Mr. Homann is shut up in a glass case with 40 dozen spa.
Does he want to prove that if a man always carries 40
dozen spa he needn’t fear starvation, even if he gets
bushed in the wilds of Woollomooloo or Collingwood for
seven weeks? It seems a silly theory for a man to shut
himself up in a glass case. Besides, if has to load
Matilda with a glass case in addition to 40 dozen spa the
lady will be very liable to burst and hit the theory in a
vital place.

A watchman at Wonderland City, Bondi, writes in
bitter protest against the par. of “ Canopus ” (14/10/’O9),
wherein Bilanderson’s place of entertainment by the sea
is described as “a gone-bung amusement resort.”
William’s employee explains that the city' 'vhich he
guards is not dead but sleeping. In one respect it resem-
bles the bear of the uttermost North —it hibernates
during the winter months. “Canopus” is urged to pay
his shilling like a man on December 24 next and pass
within the City gates, and then he will see such things in
the merry-go-round, switchback, scenic railway, katzen-
jammer castle, roller skating, haunted swing and other
lines as will convince him that when he said this vital
resort was a corpse he was screaming up the wrong tree.
“E.M.K.”: Re the business done by the J.C.W. firm
in M.L. “Spot Light” avers that that sound combination
is fairly tearing money from Ward’s subjects, and that I
am, therefore, wrong in saying things theatrical are dull
in the land. I will put just one query to “ Spot Light ”
in this connection. If there is any money-tearing in pro-
gress, why did the Firm try, by public advertisement
and priv itely, to sell a comprehensive tour of the islands,
also seasons in Auckland and elsewhere ? It is a financial
axiom that obtains in stage, as in other matters, that a man
rarely sells the thing he is making big money by.
Randal, of the Clarke, Meynell and Gunn co., claims to
have shed his appendix in record time. He played in
“ Tne Hypocrites ” at Adelaide, went into hospital there,
had the encumbrance removed, and was only a fortnight
to the bad when he rejoined the co. at Kalgoorlie.
•John Gunn, theatrical manager, who died so unex-
pectedly in a Sydney private hospital, was the son of
J. Gunn, who, with his brother Michael, erected the
Gaiety Theatre in South King, off Grafton, street,
Dublin, in 1871. The original of the family was an organ
and piano tuner in Clare-street, close to Merrion-square,
Dublin ; and the female founder of the fortunes of the
house carried on the business of a “Parisian stay and
corset maker ” in the same building. That was in the ’4O s
of last, century. Some years ago a peculiar accident hap-
pened in Dublin, in which one of the Gunns (writer thinks
the father of the just dead John) lost his life. A 'bus-
horse jibbed. Then it backed furiously and shoved the
’bus and the rest of the team into a canal, and some of
the passengers perished miserably, the vehicle being end-
up with the door jammed in the mud at the canal
bottom.

tbe Clarke-Meynell-Gunn firm, Williamson and B’l Ander-
son, whereby, instead of flying at each others’ throats,
they agree to browse peaceably side by' side on the green
herbage of the public. The principal reason for this is

“ Vulcan If Williamson carries out his threat to im-
port Beerbohm Tree and Charles Wyndham en bloc next
year, this country 7 will be invited to enthuse something
extra over two middling mummers at the fag-end of their
days. Writer has his doubts about it generally enthusing
worth tuppence. At their best, both actors stood above
the ruck simply because of the distance between the pass-
ably 7 good and the unrelievedly bad, and they became
“great ” almost solely from the fact that their own measure
was the only measure with which to measure them. Things
have improved considerably since then, but not before
Tree and Wyndham had safely reached the top step.
As with the late Irving in the last decade of his life, they
have become a sort of British institution, like Paunch or
Cobdenism, and now sustain their popularity by lavish ex-
penditure on spectacular effects. The great British pub io
now, therefore, places them beside the Bible (English
version), the Cons’tution, and the Englishman’s Tub ; and
all doubts are blasphemy.
“S.S.”: How much talent does an ordinary brunette
need to equalise the pull that a bunch of fair hair and a
blue eye give to a true blonde?
The success of Miss Essie Perrin,
with “ The ’Ook of ’Olland ”
co., is a strong demonstration
of the value of a fair com-
plexion and a heap of pale-
yellow native wool. This
young lady is a first favorite,
and vet her talents are dis-
tinctly on the mild side. She
sings infrequently and indiffer-
ently. She dances just nicely,
without a trace of the devil-
ment that gives the necessary
lilt and lift to good dancing.
Yet she is an accepted type
of ingenuous, blonde, British
beauty, much in demand for
stage consumption, and the
public has whooped her up,
while remaining dumb to
the superior merits of girls
with brown and black tresses.
Of bourse it is possible for
any girl to be fair nowa-
nights, cut the peroxided
brunette can never get
the lustrous effect of the
blonde who couldn’t help
it.

H. R. Roberts is to play the chief character in Jerome’s
“ The Passing of the Third-Floor Back.” The character
in question is the Redeem r in a boarding-house—or so it
was universally diagnosed when Forbes Robertson played
it in London recently. Roberts’air of placid saintliness

“0.K.”: In its “Personal Items” of
Sept. 16th issue, The Bulletin announced the
decease of William Henry Boussarde, aged 89,
“ the oldest advance agent and theatrical manager
in the Commonwealth.” Since then Boussarde
must have grown weary of death. It palled on
him, apparently. Anyway, he is still about, and
the other Wednesday afternoon, when he toddled
into the D.U. at Melbourne Opera House, manager
Fred Ayden expostulated with the old chap for
leaving his coffin on a windy day. This was
Boussarde’s first news of his own death. In fact, he
was surprised to hear The Bulletin had buried
him. Boussarde, at 90, is still keeping abreast of
Harry Lyons (also of Melbourne just now), his
rival ancient in theatrical annals. But whereas
Lyons wears the disguise of middle age, the resur-
rected Boussarde owns up to his years.
AMITY AT THE ZOO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is true that the Zoological Gardens are a show-place
for children, and the council is happy to think that it is
so, for it has used every endeavor to cultivate in the little
ones the love of animals. —S. M. Hf.bald, 201101'09.
See where little Willie stands
By the monkeys’ cage':
Keep an eye on Willie’s hands,
M irk the monkeys’ rage.
Hark ! a yell to raise the dead.
Willie’s new straw hat
Snatched is from his little head.
Swiftly plait by plait
His incarcerated friends
Shred the tile like flax ;
Willie’s love for monkeys ends
f In a shower of smacks.
Unto where the llama lives
Willie’s led. Grave, tall,
Calm and bland the llama gives
Confidence to all.
Wherefore Willie with a stick
Prods the creature’s frame,
Likewise hits it with a brick —
All in friendship’s name.
Straight the llama —courteous, he,
Full of old world grace—
Gazing downwards, absently,
Spits in Willie’s face.
As, with anguished sob and pant,
Willie’s madly run
To be cleansed, the elephant
Commandeers his bun.
Hungry 7, hatless, tear-stained, sore,
Willie leaves the Zoo. ;
Loving dumb things more and more—
Worshipped by them too.
N.S.W.
10-2-4.

This recent advertisement represents the
ill-gotten half-crown of the S. M. Herald :
MOHAMAD SHAH— MORLEY.— October 15, 1909, at the
residence of the bride, according to Mohammedan,
rites, Mohamad Shah to Lizzie Morley.
There is, unfortunately, no law as yet whereby
the people who thus adulterate the blood of the
white race can be gaoled; but it is to be hoped
that the S. M. Herald has at least the grace to
be ashamed of that half-crown, and to take it
home surreptitiously by a back street.
N.S.W.’s Wade Govern-
ment has decided to send a re-
presentative to Yankee Presi-
dent Taft’s stocktaking of the
world’s natural resources. The
TJ.S. has turned most of its
silent leafy forests into roll-
top desks and yellow jour-
nals, while its iron has been
transformed into railroads and
dyspeptic millionaires. The
wealth with which’’ Nature
stuffed its pockets before it
started out in life is visibly
diminishing, and in its middle
age it finds that it has been
considerable of a spendthrift.
So it is anxious to see what
the other countries have got,
and G. Wade’s representative
will attend with a catalogue
of the Ma State’s Heaven-
sent possessions in his tail-
pocket. The Bulletin is not
sure whether China is to be
represented at the congress.
No one knows exactly what
natural wealth China pos-
sesses; but it has been as-
serted that, in one province
alone, there is enough good
steaming coal to supply the
demand of the whole world
for 4000 years—that is, sup-
posing China complies with
the demand of the world
when the world yells in its
ear that it is out of the sub-
stance. Of course there is no
dead certainty that it will do
so if exhausted Western civi-
lisation is ever forced to ap-
peal either for coal or any-
thing else. By that time the
white man may not be in a
position to talk loudly about
the open door and such mat-
ters, and China may not be
disposed to furnish foreign
fleets with the fuel neces-
sary to enable them to scoot
upon the Chow seas and boss the Chow
roost. To-day the rest of the world is raging
round its doors like a herd of demented bag-
men and demanding admittance for its finished
iron, steel and wood products ; but China goes on
calmly raising vegetables and visiting its relations
on the family wheelbarrow. This chronic con-
servation of natural resources may mean nothing
or it may mean that China is destined to make a
sprint at the finish when the rest of the earth’s
crust is bald of timber and empty of minerals.
In the old age of the world, China may stand re-
vealed as the thrifty Dragon who went slow and
saved up his natural resources by wheelbarrowing
sedately about instead of rushing everywhere in
steel railway trains, and by reading Confucius
instead of Hall Caine and Marie Corelli.
“ 0.,” in reply :
In Tun Bulletin of 7/10/’O9 “Canopus” makes some
rather uncomplimentary remarks about the attendants at
Sydney Museum.
I’m an attendant, and I know “ Canopus ” and hun-
dreds of things like “ Canopus.” They come here and
expect me—a poorly paid attendant—to be a biologist
and zoologist and geologist and Heaven knows what. Yet
when they go into a court of justice they don’t expect the
cop or tipstaff to know the law. If they’re after law,
they interview an old gent with horsehair on his head.'
And when they go to church they don’t ask the verger
for theology. Then why don’t they go to the fountain heads
when they come to the Museum? There are Hedley and
liainbow and Thorpe, with half the alphabet after each
of their names. Then there’s the latest arrival, Hall-
man, B.Sc. of the Sydney University, one of the smartest
young scientists in Australia. These gentlemen and
others give lectures at 10.30 o’clock on Saturday morn-
ings. I told “Canopus” that, but he would persist in
talking to me. I suspected something, and looked round
quick, and caught a man who appeared to be a mate of
“ Canopus” clearing out with the skeleton of the whale
under his arm.
With regard to the stuffed rhinoceros (for whose safety
I am responsible) as many attempts have been made to
steal that bulky specimen as to steal the crown jewels of
England.
“Canopus ” also talks about the attendants at the Art
Gallery. Now, if he really expects an attendant to be an
artist of the first water, he has less savee than a spherical
picture ora spiritual unicorn.
The next time he calls at the Museum I’ll sool the pen-
guins on to him, and let the preserved dragon off the chain.

The Bulletin believes that the man who
sends this is more or less of a liar :
Since the Chief Commissioner of Police advised Mel-
bourne suburban residents that a good watchdogis cheaper
and more effective than a policeman, the price of live
dog s meat, especially with a bark in it, has risen so high
in the markets that frankfort and saveloy manufacturers
are at their wit’s end to satisfy the demands of
ravenous racecourse punters. The writer knows for
a fact that at least two leading manufacturers
have been compelled to use beef and mutton.
Dogs, which have hitherto been the unconsidered
perquisite of the official metropolitan dog-catcher,
are now worth much gold, and private practitioners in
the dog-snatching profession are buying Queen Anne
residences. Anything that can chuck a bark louder than
a cough is priceless. Last Saturday, in the course of
conversation with a leading racehorse owner, who retired
about three weeks ago from the dog-snavelling business,
he informed me that he contemplates removing his eldest
son from his theological studies in order that he mav de-
vote himself entirely to following up his father’s late'pro-
fession ; and it is well known that at least three barristers
in Melbourne have left the law, bought a lump of liver
at the end of a string and gone dog-lifting.
A DEAL OF HONOR.
LUCY (passionately): " If you thought that, why
did you marry me?”
HAROLD (gloomily): “ ’Twas a bet!”
J. W. McLachlan, the strange pietist
whom Gippsiand North recently unloaded on the
Victorian Assembly, has stepped serenely for-
ward with yet another measure for the improve-
ment of his fellow-man’s morals. It is in the
form of an amendment of the Crimes Act.
If it becomes law any male who is intimate
with a girl under 20 years of age shall,
unless he marries her within eight months
of the offence, be liable to three years’
penal servitude. An exception is made in
the case of youths or men discovered con-
sorting with acknowledged daughters of Rahab.
It is an enactment calculated to fill the Vic. gaols
up nicely, and with an interesting assortment of
the guilty and the innocent. The blackmailing
female who is not technically Rahab but as near
to being her as not to matter, will here find an
engine of profit ready to her hand. There is
nothing easier to allege than this kind of miscon-
duct, and nothing harder to disprove ; and where
the allegator is young, fair and a female there are
few charges that a male jury credits more readily.
One effect of the passing into law of such
insane legislation as J. W. McLachlan favors
must be the stoppage, in great part, of
harmless, friendly, social intercourse between
the sexes. The lone man will flee like mad from
railway carriages, rooms, houses, beaches, woods
and so on, the moment a lone woman materialises.
Youthful males of a cautious disposition will start
to jib violently at the ancient practice of seeing
young women home. Country walks, wherein
man and maid collaborate, will take on a new
aspect of peril for the man. Maidservants will
be at a discount in homes which shelter bachelors.
The trouble-prospect that opens out is simply
illimitable. One benefit and one alone, so far as
the writer can see, will be conferred on the com-
munity if the proposed amendment is carried.
The mostly-blackmailing breach-of-promise action
will become less frequent. No threat of an action
for breach will be half so efficacious as the threat
which McLachlan seeks to place in the mouth of
her who is after either matrimony or cash.
• * • *
The shrieks of Peary become a trifle less
deafening these days. Peary’s strong point was
that the Eskimo who accompanied Dr. Cook
denied that the doctor had been at the Pole or
anywhere near it. The weak point in this story
was that the Eskimo didn’t call on the New York
papers and say this ; Commander Peary called
and said that the Eskimo said it. A good many
people had begun to think that Peary was wild
enough to say that almost anybody said almost
anything, and they felt that they would rather hear
from the Eskimo through some other source.
Now Knud Rasmussen, a Polar traveller and the
chief authority on the language of the blubber-
eating people, has gone to Greenland and inter-
viewed the Eskimo, and he reckons that their
evidence shows that Cook did reach the Pole. So
the doctor’s stock keeps looking up, while Peary in
the background kicks up a dust of adjectives with
both feet.
The masterly dead silence of the Victorian
police on the subject of crime is giving the re-
porters a little extra trouble and affording con-
siderable amusement to the people at large. The
Department of O’Callaghan has put up such an
astoundinglv bad record within the last two or
three years that it became plain something would
have to be done. The reports in the newspapers
showed that Victoria was a sort of picnic ground
for criminals of all descriptions, and the average
Victorian was tired to death of reading that the
footprints of the murderer on the ceiling were all
that the police had captured. The obvious remedy
was a thorough overhaul and reorganisation of the
entire force ; but the Bent Government was in
power, and Bent was too busy buying land. So the
Master Mind evolved the great idea of withholding
news of crimes from the reporters. Where there
was a corpse and much blood it was impossible to
keep the intelligence from the public; but the lesser
bloodless crimes were wrapped in official secrecy
by men with large feet, and it was fondly but
fatuously hoped that people would gradually get
the idea that crime was practically extinct owing
to the genius of the Department of O’Callaghan.
Of course, as might have been foreseen, the news-
paper men managed to get hold of the criminal news
all the same, and the great idea was killed stone
dead in infancy. But O'Callaghan still sits up in
Russell-street in a mute condition, and the absurd
ukase remains unrepealed.

Hawthorn (Vic.) has a municipal council
after Gregory Wade’s own heart. A while back,
through its neglect of a drain, oneof its ratepayers
took it to Court and recovered damages. The
council then entered on a long course of appeals
right up to the Privy Council, the sole result of
which was a whole mountain range of Costs. But
that didn’t cure it. Recently it perceived a
ratepayer—a co-operative brick co. —creating a
smoke nuisance, so it ordered the co. to quit
making bricks altogether. Though it had power
to regulate the industry, it had none to prohibit,
so the co. appealed. The case got as far as the
High Court this time, with the custotnary result;
and the co.’s bill of Costs has just been handed to
the council to liquidate—£2Bo, to wit. The coun-
cil’s own bill has yet to be met, and the ratepayers
are talking firmly to their representatives, and
asking them whether they have got a son-in-law
in the Costs business, or whether it is just merelj'
cussed ness.
Victoria’s honorarium still indulges his
deep-rooted aversion to the Minimum Wage law
by fining offenders the minimum whenever there
isn’t a P.M. alongside him to correct his re-
actionary tendencies. The other day a P’ran
man-milliner was convicted of paying two em-
ployees 7s. 6d. a week each when the Law had
fixed their wages at 20s. and 10s. respectively.
He had been, in effect, robbing these two persons
of 15s. a week—probably for months. Yet the shop-
keepers on the Bench reckoned a ss. fine in each
case was a fair thing. Now what sort of a penalty
would those same magistrates
think a fair thing for a woman
who went into that shop
and annexed a 15s. hat
every week ? And
how many hats
would she annex
before seeing
the inside of
Pentridge ?

A POSSIBLE OUTBURST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In binding accused over to appear for sentence when
called upon, Judge Murray said “As to these marriage
shops, where people are married at so much per head, I
will not sav anything ; much has alreadv been said by the
Supreme Court Judges about them, if I did add any-
thing, I would speak strongly ; so much so, in fact, that
my language would, perhaps, be too strong for a Court.”—
Daily Paper.
Suppose he spoke ! Suppose he lit a match
And, taking off his wig, applied the flame
With trembling fingers to the horsehair thatch
Until the thing a blazing mass became !
And then suppose he flung it at the Court,
And with a virtuous, judicial snort,
Suppose he spoke !
What would he say? What batteries of speech
Would open fire upon the shuddering globe?
What she'ls of thought would whistle, burst and
screech ;
And would his sanctified, judicial robe
Burst into flame as hotter grew his wrath,
And would red cinders and black smoke fly forth?
Or would he choke
And gurgle like a flooded watercourse,
As words like logs between his learn6d teeth
Piled up and jammed until, with one loud, hoarse
Ejaculation pumped up from beneath,
He shot them out to crush, and maim and slay
The consciences of folk who run awav
And buy a ring?
My word 1 it makes you wince to think what he
Might do if he put on his kicking boots,
And charged full speed right at the diction’ry,
H'd tear the blessed thing up by the roots !
And afterwards he’d gallop round and hit
Each mother’s son of us with junks of it !
The mad typhoon
That takes the tropic forest by the hair
Would b ! dead slow compared to him if he
Cast off restraint and slithered down the stair
Of his great eminence to earth where we
C-awl round and prattle ! What man could outlast
The blist’ring horror of the slightest blast
Of that simoon ?
Good Lord ! we’d rock upon our heels and toes
And fall down on the desert’s face. The sand
Would pile about us till at length it rose
Above our bodies. Then on every hand
A stillness dead would come upon the place
Till from the waste One raised a blistered face
And wailed “ Whaffor ! ”
Then out of Space a Voice judicial might
Vouchsafe to answer: “ With these few remarks
I will resume—although I have not quite
Completed my discourse on love in parks
And so forth. But I trust that what I’ve said
Will be reported by the press and read.
Please shut that door I ”

WIFE: “It seems to me we've been married a
century. I can’t even remember when or

HUSBAND (emphatically): “I can. It was at a
dinner party where there were 13 at table.”
Another champion tired man has been
picked up on the Adelaide to Melbourne railway,
near Horsham. He was a drover, and, being very
weary, lay down for a snooze on the railway
track. Then the Adelaide express came along—
and he is still resting.
The death of Elsie Sigel,
the pretty girl “ missionary ”
of New York’s Chinatown,
has made a notable slump in
the preaching business.
When Miss Sigel’s naked
body was found doubled up
in a box on the premises of
a Chow who is still at large,
she was at first hailed as a
martyr. ■ Then her love-
letters were unearthed, and
people awoke to the fact
that she was merely one of
a band of girl missionaries
whose “mission” was a
cover for a particularly
dreadful form of Asiatic im-
morality, and that her
death had come to pass
UCittu uau oouiv w'
because she abandoned
one Chow lover for an-
other—in short, that she
had been practically torn
to pieces between the
two Asiatic lovers. At
this stage of affairs the
martyr aspect of things
slumped. For a while
after Elsie Sigel’s death
a few girls kept up
the pretence of going
heroically into the
haunts of the heath-
en to save his soul,
and dispense the
Word, and scatter
the Bread of Life.
But the police be-
gan to tell so
many unpleasant
truths that even
the most hardened
young saint among
the m presently
gave it up. Cer-
tain of the older
women, some of
whom were real
saints, still stuck to
the work, but when the
beautiful church of sincere
Chinese oonvei - found that the
pretty young gir.s weren’t coming
any more it revolted, and threw off
its Christianity, and jeered at the Scriptures,
and threatened the elderly missioners with
violence if they didn’t get out. So they got
out, and Chinatown rubbed off its bogus Christian
varnish and went back undisguisecfly to Joss.
Things were possibly hurried upa little by a casual
sort of a row at Hull (Massachusetts). At that place
there were also certain good people who took an
interest in the Chinaman’s soul, and thus an
intimacy sprung up between the yellow and the
white, and one result was a picnic at which the
converted Ah Sin was present in force. But ap-
parently Ah Sin realised that, after the New
York revelations, the game was nearly up, so he
let himself loose in a last great burst. As a con-
sequence, 36 white girls were found, partly
drugged and partly drunk, on an island along
with a very considerable number of Chinamen,
and the U.S. decided that it had found still
another reason why the yellow man must go.

“ Jareel ” speaks :
What a muddled mind the parson displays, as a general
thing, when he gets on his legs in public! Yet it is
natural that he should do so, considering that he spends
about three parts of his waking hours colloguing with
children, old women and male Rechabites. That sort of
mental exercise is enough to render anyone inarticulate.
Rev. Charles Matthews, as well-meaning a young man as
ever went soul-salvaging, gave a dreary exhibition
last week of the cloth’s tendency in the direction of
meaningless verbiage. According to the cablegram he
said that: —
“The woman voter in Australia hail neither advanced
nor retarded reform.... Her often pathetic belief in the
idfeal of home, however, had kept Australian Socialism
sane, and will yet prove a safeguard against doctrines
which menace family life and religion.”
What does it all signify'? Used Australian Socialism to
be insane or to tend that way ? If so, why did Rev.
Ohas. Matthews barrack for it from public balconies
and other high and often egg-haunted places when he
was in Australia? If the woman voter here has had no
reforming influence, one way or the other, how comes it
that she has availed to modify the alleged lunacy of the
indigenous brand of Socialism? How, in any case, is her
“ belief” in the “ ideal obthe home ” calculated tp affect
Australian Socialism, or any other policy, and why
is this belief of hers “often pathetic”? What are the
local doctrines which menace “ family life and religion,”
and require to be leaped upon and rendered innocuous by
“pathetic” female “beliefs in ideals.” Where do the
doctrines live? What State or city harbors them,
and why haven’t their lairs been condemned by the
inspector of nuisances for the district which they in-
fest? And what is the “ ideal of home,” anyhow? Home
regarded as a hollow place to eat in is understandable
enough, but the “ ideal ”is vague and intangible. Then,
according to Mr. Matthews’ clerical brethren out here,
woman is really a childless person practising race suicide
in a flat or doing mixed bathing in a brief costume at
Bondi, and the two pictures don’t agree somehow. In
fact, nothing agrees somehow.

Fat Man may still find a difficulty in
dodging the Undesirable Immigrants Act of the
Heavenly Commonwealth, but lie’s all right when
it’s a question of getting into the front seats of the
earthly tabernacle. Next month, there are to be
gay doings when imported Anglican Archbishop
Wright is to be dumped into his stall at St.
Andrew’s Cathedral, Sydney, and the faithful are
generously invited to apply for tickets (free) to
see the process. And when the faithful do apply
as aforesaid, they receive a reply stating that the
tickets will be forwarded “ if available,” the reply
being added to a form of receipt for subscriptions
to the cathedral. It’s a million to one that, if the
faithful don’t take the gentle hint and send a
cheque along, the tickets will not be available.
As Holy Writ hath it: “I came not to call
the scutn, but the wealthy lower orders, to enter
into the presence of his Grace the Lord Arch-
bishop.”
BRINGING DISCREDIT ON THE PARTY.
MAUDIE: “Hoi’ up, can’t yer (hie) Muriel?
People’ll be thinkin yer (hie) drunk.”
Pretty well every measure passed nowadays
dealing with the dishonest tricks of “commercial
men’’ emphasises the need for providing plain
bleak “jug” as the penalty for this brand of la,w-
breaking. When Premier Murray passed his
Chaff Adulteration Act, a few; months back, the
usual yell was heard declaring that it would
hamper trade and cripple Private Enter-
prise ; but now that the Act has been in
operation a while things are seen to be
different. Last week a deputation repre-
senting a majority of the chaff-dealers of
Melbourne called on the Minister of Agri-
culture and asked him to enforce the Act
more vigorously. Their reasons were in-
teresting. They had tried to act honestly,
and they found it had paid. They had
put a pure article on the market, and
they had found their customers counter-
manding previous orders for the “mixed”
article. Also they were winning back the
export trade they had lost, and were get-
ting a better name outside the State. It
was Sitate regulation and Government in-
terference, and they liked it and wanted
more of it. But there was a minority that
was intent on swindling the public, which
minority still continued the old dodges of
selling a mixture of a little hay and a.
lot of straw cut up small, and this minority
was not only robbing unsophisticated cus-
tomers, but was trying to undo all the good
tlie honest dealers were doing for themselves
and the State. So they wanted the swindle
squelched. The Minister promised to prose-
cute ; but he didn’t seem sanguine of the
efficacy of the fines that could be imposed. And
that is just where the plain gaol remedy would
come iii. A £lO fine is a trifle to a man with a
£lO,OOO swindle; but a month’s hard labor is a
different affair ; and when the second or third
conviction entails 12 months’ enclosure, the cul-
prit usually alters his point of view.

“ Amos M.” : In the “ General News ” of
Sydney Telegraph I recently disinterred a para-
graph which ridiculed a confused constable, who
stated in the witness box that he didn’t know on
what day of the week Good Friday or Easter
Monday fell this year. If that inaccurate paper
will turn up its issue of October G and look under
the same heading, it will find the following, and
then it can sack itself: —
The holidays that are by statute celebrated on the days
they fall on, or, if on Sunday, on the following day, are
New Year’s Day, Christmas and Boxing Days, Anniver-
sary Day, Good Friday and Easter Saturday and
Monday.
Ed. VII. ’s approaching anniversary recalls
the fact that the heartiest birthday of the kind
which Australia has seen was probably that of
1810. George 111. was King then, and Lachlan
Macquarie was Governor. Government House was
decorated witli greenery, and oranges were inter-
woven with the vegetation until the place looked
like an orange grove. The chain gangs had a day off
and the colony kept holiday. A vast crowd was
invited to Government House, and 80 or 90 sat
down to dinner. Mr. Robinson composed and
recited an ode of stupendous length, and contain-
ing many allusions to the status of the guests.
Behold where Mercy’s genuine fruits appear
Formed by contrition’s hand and fostered here,
said Robinson, with a graceful sweep of his arm
which seemed to embrace all present, and the
men whose sentences had expired and who vvere
then making money applauded joyfully. l'he
Governor, supported and aided by Mr. Robinson,
proposed 19 toasts —including Wellington, the
Archbishop of Canterbury and other notables so
the company had 19 drinks. In degenerate modern
days it would only have about three. Robinsons
ode was printed in the Gazette, and below it was
a notice of the birth of a little Robinson.
Altogether it was a crowded week for Robinson.
“P. Melon”: I found this in my daily
paper recently :
Sir A. Oonan Doyle has published a powerful
indictment of Belgian misrule and atrocities in
the C nitto Free State and is heading a move-
ment for mass meetings for the purpose of
forcing the British Government to take de-
cisive action.
It is the sort of thing that makes mo
tired. While Britain was trailing its
flag in the mud of S’Africa, while women
and children were passing out like
flies in Kitchener’s concentration camps, while
Boer homesteads were lying waste and Boer
fathers and sons lying dead or dying broadcast
all that a few Jew-British syndicates might snare
big dividends, Doyle was hard at it justifying the
infamy in his epic, “ The Great Bouth African
War.” For that- masterpiece of “patriotic'
misrepresentation he was flung a knighthood.
Seemingly he pines for further distinction. It is
well known, and has been for many months, that
much the same .Tew-British speculators who
interested themselves in the Band a decade ago
have now got their predatory eyes on the Con-
go. Belgium is weak. The speculators reckon,
with some show of reason, that if the
little Continental nation won’t grant them
the commercial concessions they want,
it may he made to by the mailed fist of
Britain. Of course, the first step in the
direction of getting the fist in question into work-
ing order is to stir up a quantity of snuffling in-
dignation in the U.K. anent the alleged atrocities
and misrule that characterise the desired land.
That scheme worked well in S’Africa, and it is
going to do the same in the Congo district if those
concerned can manage it. As to the iniquities
alleged against the Belgians in tropic Africa, I
discredit them almost entirely. I lived among
these people for three years, and a milder, more
forbearing lot I never met. To the typical
Briton the mote in his neighbor’s eye ever assumes
the proportions of a locomotive or an Alp, while
the extensive beam in his own dwindles to vanish-

THE LONG FLIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A. H. Forbes aercmlaned from St. Louis, Missouri, to
Richmond, Virginia, 731 miles, in 19J hours an average
speed of 38 miles an hour.— Cable.
Valley, hill and river
’Neath the wide-spread wings
Silent flow for ever
As my flier swings
Over sunlit pastures
And wide, golden fields,
Where the great cloud-shadows
Glide like 'phantom shields.
In the sky’s blue silence
Hear my motor’s song !
Like a gun it clatters
As I sweep along ;
Hear the fierce, low purring
Of the driven blades
In this deep air-ocean
As the sunlight fades !
Since the dawn I’ve travelled
With the autumn wind,
Now the sunset ruddy
Dies the hills behind ;
But I still sweep onward
In my lonely flight
While the world to stillness
Sinks before the night.
Silver stars above me—
Golden lights below,
And the motor crackling
As through Space I go ;
High aboge strange cities,
Little do I heed
Of Life’s distant troubles
In this dream of speed.
While I steer in silence
Underneath the moon,
Clearly I remember
Things that passed at noon ;
Startled Shouts of farmers
Working,’midst the hay,
As I skimmed above them
On my tireless way.
Now the air grows colder,
And I think I feel
With a gloved forefinger
Dew upon the steel;
I am numbed and dreaming.
Let me think 1 Have I
Fifteen hours been floating
In this empty sky ?
Faint and far the earth-sounds
On my dreams intrude ;
That’s eleven chiming
In the solitude.
One note trembles to me,
But ten others drown
In the dark air currents
O’er the sunken town.
I am lost in darkness,
And an ecstasy
Born of Space and Freedom
Fills the heart of me 1
. Yes, I shall remember—
Earth above my head—
How I flew unhindered
Over quick and dead !
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
Under the scared heading, “A Tiger in a
Fog,” the Sydney Telegraph (13/10/’O9) published
an excited message from Jamberoo (N.S. W.). It
appears that a man of Jamberoo lately reported
having seen a full-grown tiger, and Jamberoo has
been looking and waiting for the beast ever since.
On Tuesday, October 12, two Jamberooers were
working on a mountain road in a fog when a series
of loud, indignant roars close handy jarred on
their ears and urged them to fly. Being unarmed,
the two Jambrutes flew. It would be a terrible
thing if someone had sewed some striped football
jerseys on a calf and had taken a day off for the
purpose of roaripg terribly in the wilderness.
The jersey dodge was successfully worked some
time ago by a country jester of 9. Australia, and
some gay spirit of Jamberoo may have imitated
the idea. The manufacture of an Australian bush
tiger must be a joyous and stirring performance—
especially when the perspiring tiger-maker runs
the bodkin into the calf’s stummick, and the
lantern gets kicked 1 over by the maddened child
of the cow.
TOO SUDDEN.
" Beggin yer pardon, boss, ’ow far am I from the
nearest pub!"
" Good gracious, why there's one just opposite!"
“ But that's the one I've just left."
“Wang’’draws on his memory for facts
about dogs : .
The most marvellous escape from death 1 ever saw was
that of a fox terrier I once possessed. Foxy was lying
curled up alongside a huge dead gum tree, while I was
digging post-holes near by. There was a strong wind
blowing at the time, and suddenly a great hollow branch
broke off high up, and came hurtling down, endways,
straight for the dog. I shrieked a warning; but I
was too late. The branch buried' itself a couple of
feet in the ground, apparently driving my tyke in-
to the soil beneath it. Presently', to my astonishment
(this is a fact, blokes), I heard him howling in the interior
of the hollow branch. It had dropped clean around him,
like a barrel. I chopped him out, and he had only a bit
of a scar on the back of his neck to show for his narrow
escape. There was another dog I knew—but, no, I’ll
give some other liar a chance.
“Harry J.,” on leatherheads :
There are exceptions to the general bush rule that the
unwhiskered are lacking in taste and talent for blas-
phemy. Take birds, for instance, and in so doing pay-
particular attention to leatherheads, which are noto-
riously free from face herbage. Why the alternative name
of these sin-stained fowl should be friar-birds passes my
comprehension. It is a libel, by implication, on a
respectable body of clerics. Are friars persistent, open
and unashamed purveyors of bad language? Do they
howl horrible oaths in our open spaces regardless of the
decencies, and passing cows and things? Certainly not.
Yet such conduct is, in the case of leatherheads, so com-
mon as to amount to a chronic habit. Some months ago,
whilst travelling a dreary stock route in the North-West,
I squatted myself ’neath the bee-laden branches of a
friendly old box-tree. Presently I noticed a bevy—or,
perhaps, I should rather say a push—of leatherheads
advancing noisily. The members of it were discussing
poison-carts. As they settled over my head, the conver-
sation was in full blast. I have listened to some chat

calculated to paralyse Sunday-schools, and provoke wide-
spread breaches of law and order in my time. During a
varied career it has been my lot to take part in the

N.S.W. anti-gambling M.L.A. what time the Oup horse

heads’ efforts surpassed all the above in flavor, fruitiness
and virulence. Besides the astounding wealth of lan-
guage and the ingenious diversity of the blasphemies
which they screamed forth, there was a passion and a
force which I have never heard attained by mere humans.
Other curSers whom I have encountered were mostly solo
performers. Leatherheads do their blaspheming in a
deafening chorus. There is a sameness about the human
curser, however hair-covered; the leatherhead never
repeats itself, though it may have had a clean shave that
very morning. In this field of enterprise there is nothing
to touch the leatherhead : Dowie and certain of the
Prophets and early Christian Fathers were his only peers,
and they, alas ! are dead.
* * * *
The Australian ornithologists, meeting in
Adelaide, have been engaged in robbing the
cuckoo of its claim to intelligence. There has
been a kind of idea that the bird always laid its
eggs in the nest of some species with eggs of a
similar marking, so as to aid deception. She
doesn’t do anything of the kind, says the associa-
tion, speaking officially. She slops her eggs
about anywhere, even in nests that can’t hold the
young bird when grown a bit. She even billets
her grub-eating offspring on birds that would
never think of feeding it with anything but grain,
and so it perishes miserably. In fact, the cuckoo,
long admitted to be a knave, is now written down
a fool as well.

“ Waltz ” writes about an animal which he
may have invented for all this paper knows to the
contrary
Let the eastern gohanha experts hush their chatter
while I talk to them about the western article. The
largest survivor of the antediluvian reptilian monsters
that crawls the earth is undoubtedly the Westralian
bungarra, which reaches a length of 14ft. At first sight
it can almost be mistaken for a young crocodile. It has
always been the staple food of the blacks, who learn to
track it from infancy. When run to earth the blacks will
take hours in digging it out, and then roast it in their
usual manner among the ashes. Its flesh is firm and
white, resembling that of a flathead. Fried in dripping
it makes a good feed. Fried in butter, with pepper and Lea
and Perrins’ added, it makes a man smack his lips for nine
days. A 14-foot bungarra is as big round the chest as a
year-old child, and steaks can be cut off his tail as large
as schnapper or jewfish steak. His top bark is plated
with heavy scaly armor, and: he is brown in color with
yellowish-white stripes, lighter colored belly and striped
legs. A Sierra Nevada runs from the head along
the spine, tapering off at the tail. He is easily'
tracked by his wallowing gait and short steps;
small bushes are trampled on and stones knocked aside
in his path. Bungarra can get a move on when pursued.
His favorite haunts are the enormous excavations of the
boody rat, or in rock crevices. His principal diet now is
rabbit, especially the young, as the rabbits find ready-
made warrens in the boody holes. Opened a 12-footer,
and found stomach to contain 22 lately-bo-rn rabbits un-
digested, several pounds-weight of centipedes, numerous
scorpions, feathers galore, and bones of a young emu.
Best method of catching is by a strong gin trap
securely fastened with a chain at the entrance to a bur-
row. Bungarra is fond of visiting camp-fires at night,
and will steal pannikins, spoons and bright articles. The
“ hens ” lay nine eggs at a sitting equal in size to a fowl’s
egg.. Should this meet the eye of Paddy Doyle, Ntd
Keenan, Doc Silbey', or any of the old Widgeemoola hands
they could all bear me out about the tremendous bun-
garra that resided at Emu Rocks on the old Widgie-road.
He always visited our camp-fires for scraps, and was well
known and respected on that track. He measured 13ft. ;
we several times had the tape over him to settle argu-
ments.
S. C. O. Jessop writes from “ the Edge ” :
I have just been looking at 1000 head of fat bullocks.
When they have a mile spread on them in the early morn-
ing, in grass up to their briskets, they present a fine
spectacle. They feed so earnestly one would think that
grass had not passed down their throats for days. It is
too cold now for flies, else 1000 tails would be waving to
and fro in the brisk southerly. They are from the
Northern Territory, and are going “inside” for sale. I
am reminded of Kipling’s soldiers: “Pore beggars,
they’ll never see ’ome.” Well, the owners don’t breed
them to stay at home—with fats £9 a head.
There’s not a poley in the mob. Two thousand horns
glisten when the bullocks stand and gaze curiously at a
passing camel-train. Long after the desert beasts pass
the cattle lift their glistening noses up in the air, and
sniff uneasily, for the camels must remind them of the
well-oiled myalls, who sometimes spear them from the
lignum when they go to drink. Cattle have good
memories.
There is not a bad-colored beast in the lot. They con-
sist mostly of reds, roans, red-and-whites and whites.
Having filled themselves now, the leaders lope lazily along
in strings, only to be steadied by the ever-watchful
drover, who turns them back gently before the body of
the cattle begins moving.
One seldom hears a good stockman use a whip. Only
newchums and woolly-stockmen ( i.e., sheep-men) use the
whip as a plaything. One loud crack on a cold morning
is sometimes sufficient to cause a thousand restive
“ hornies” to stop feeding and to walk, walk, walk. If
they start that, then the luckless person who was the
cause of it is reminded of his foolishness all day, and
occasionally all through the trip.
It’s not always easy to steady a big mob and make them
feed again. The leaders may be blocked, but the reap
guard marches stolidly along and pushes through what
were the leaders till the whole column is once more
moving. Of course, they can be stopped. They can be
“rung” and worried into a confused mass; but that’s
bad droving.
Ah well, they’re going to market, 1800 miles away. I
look wistfully at the dwindling cloud of dust which hides
my mates, and heave a sigh. - I must stay.

“ Bilbax ” describes a State funeral on the
tinfields: —
Three days’ tramp from Coen, where I had worked d
duffer and “ bust,” landed me at the Excelsior Camp,
where five 0 x B’s and a bough shed, dotted on the red
ribs of a desolate ridge, mark the humblS beginnings of
one of those thriving and populous towns that will deco*
rate our happy land so soon as we get rid of the Labor
party and give Capital a chance to develop our great
natural resources by private blanky enterprise. Four
miners sat under the shed. From their bowed shoulders
and the angle at which their elbows were extended I
divined that “ dinner was now on,” and presently I had
accepted a hearty invitation to “ cut in.”
“Is there a chance of anything here?” I asked. “Yes,
there’s a ten-bob job for you if you know the ashes-to-
ashes patter.” They explained that Battling Joe, two
miles out, had dropped dead yesterday' and would be
buried to-day. Young Bob Benson had gone out with
the tip-dray to bring in the corpse. Before we had
finished our meal we heard the rattle of the approaching
dray. A choked exclamation of “ Spare me days,
boys! What’s this?” brought us to our feet to get a
view. “ It’s old Joe driving his own blanky hearse, and
I’ll swear I see him a stiff’un yesterday. There ain’t
nobody else here got a blue-and-white shirt but Joe.”
The nearer approach of the dray relieved the excite-
ment. It was Bob, all right, and he cheerfully explained
when he pulled up. “ Yairs, ’twus a better shirt nur
mine. Pity ter bury it. So I swapped ’im. I’m goin’ on.
Youse blokes kin ketch up.”
We leisurely finished dinner, but we didn’t “ketch up.”
We met the dray coming back.
“ Cripes ! ye’re slow,’’said Bob. “I wuzzent goin’ter
wait all day for yeh, so I buried him meself.”
“ How did you lower the coffin in ?”
“Backed up and tipped it in, o’ course,” said Bob.
“But it’s orl right. He earn’t get out, don’t you fear.
He’s wrong side up.”
Thus was I robbed of a much-needed 10 bob, and, what
hurt me nearly as much, a chance that I had often de-
sired. For I believe I could deliver an impressive and
improving funeral oration.
“ Boh ” : Young women visitors are “ off”
in one of the new cocky districts of Westralia. A
married couple tookiup land, and then the wife
got the lonely germ badly. She yearned to have
heart-to-heart talks about two-and-elevenpenny
bargains ; so the dearest friend was invited on the
scene. She arrived, gushed over the two-year-old
son of the house, exhibited all her frocks, talked
herself red in the face and then she got the lonely
germ. To fill in time the visiting lady offered to
take out the afternoon tea to the men, who were
“ scrubbing” assisted by a nigger or two. She was
started off, armed with two willie-cans filled with
afternoon tea and buttered scones. She followed
the track for a while, when it suddenly struck
her that she had gone a long way and that she
felt lonely ; so she made a remark that sounded
like “coo-ee” very badly done, and one of the
black assistants jumped out almost from under
her feet. With a wild yell she ran—and ran and
van. Hours later the scrub-cutting husband
arrived home, growling like Father Bear for his
afternoon tea. Then the pantomime started.
First the husband had to ride 30 miles for a
trooper and black tracker. Then he and
his brother tramped round that tract of
country all night. Then they separated and went
in different directions. In the middle of the
next night the husband crawled home on his hands
and knees, his boots in rags. Somewhere the
following morning the dead-beat brother got as
far as the gate. They had to carry him in and put
him to bed. Then the next afternoon the trooper
and the black tracker got back to the happy
home. So a fresh search party started out, and
that afternoon they found the lady. She was
seated on a log eating the last of the scones. In
all her fright she - had stuck to the willie-cans,
and hadn’t been very frightened, because she
knew they would find her. But the hostess who
had two sick men to look after, and the men who
had lost two days’ work, and the trooper who had
put off his wedding to find 9, strange woman, all
took a different view, of the situation.

THE WRITING ON THE DOOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A hard old rusticated clam
Was bound’ry-rider Slimy Sara.
He lived alone
On Mumblebone,
An’ couldn’t read or even tell
A T from L.
As his pet name insinuates,
He wasn’t one as speculates
In soap an’ scent,
Or ever went
To any superfine shivoo,
Like me an’ you.
Well, this ’ere Sam one day returned
To where his humble camp-fire burned,
An’ found some talk,
In glarin’ chalk,
So big it spread from top to floor,
Writ on his door.
Some urgent message from the boss,
He thought it was, an’ at a loss
What else to do,
He turned him to
An’ took that door, with many, twinges,
Off its hinges.
With utmbst care he gripped it, and
Five miles he humped it Overland,
'Cross stony hills,
O’er ruts an’rills,
Now on his back, now on his head,
To have it read.
He stood i t Up in Murphy's bar,
And shearer-men fronl near and far.
Drew round to see
What; it might be.
As Murphy, pullin’ at his chin,
Read with a grin :
“ Old Slimy Sam of Mumblebone
'S the dirtiest beggar ever known.”
My eye ! when it
Had soaked'a bit.
That party felt so mighty sore
He smashed the door.
N.S.W.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.
