“ Dagon,” about a pub and some bones
Leaving Melbourne by its main nor’-west entrance, the
Mount Alexander (old name for Castlemaine) Road, and
following this highway for about 17 miles, you come to a
peculiar and dreary spot, redolent with old-time
memories. It is where the road crosses the Saltwater
River. The stream runs at the bottom of a ravine, with
bleak, stony sides, suggesting a Scotch glen in mid-
winter. Down this steep place winds the road until a
small flat, about six acres in extent, is'reached. On this
flat and alongside the river bank still stands the moul-
dering remains of the old “Oat and Fiddle Inn,” kept
during the height of the digging days by V Tulip ”
Wright, an old Port Arthur Vandemonian—one of
Price’s men,” who, when his time expired, moved to
Victoria, and was celebrated there as a thief-taker, and
later as a property-owner. He possessed houses in
Wright’s-lane, Little Bourke-street West.
The old hostelry was situated in an ideal place for a
pub, for, being at the bottom of the ravine, it was natural
for the teamster to have a spell there before tackling the
long hill in front. There still remains an immense pile'of
bottles in the garden at the back of this place, and the
sandstone steps of the old bar are worn into deep grooves,
as from the hoofs of multitudes.
A lot of lucky diggers disappeared hereabouts, and
there is still alive an ancient servitor of the dead-and-
gone pub—himself a lag, and one of Price’s flock —who is
reputed to tell weird stories as to their vanishment.
A relative of mine was engineer to Sunbury shire (1872),
and when his men were digging holes for the piers of the
new stone bridge they discovered a large quantity of
human bones. I have handled a portion of a human skull
in vself from these excavations. The common explanation
is that they were the bones of diggers who, after being
hocussed at the Oat and Fiddle Inn, were led down to'
the ferryboat, and in midstream robbed, knocked on the
head, and thrown overboard. Public-house methods’
were loose in these days, and such horrors as keeping the
bar open after-hours and Sunday-trading were quite
common—to say nothing of worse things than these.

From “ Mallarapponie ” :
The Bulletin (16/9/’O9) makes allusion to such persons
as extra tall dwarfs and very short giants, though it
appears to entertain considerable doubt as to the exist-
ence of the same. I arise to remark that such persons
do exist. I have met them.
“Stump” Hagan was an opal-gouger down at Sand-
stone, and he measured sft. in his hoof-gear. He was a
curtailed sort of little man was Stumpy ; but the main
deformity in his growth was his legs. Bodily speaking,
he was designed for a six-footer.
Then there was “Long” Stevens, the tall cattleman
from Merlin’s. Stevens’ build was remarkable insomuch
as he resembled that curious fowl, the stilt. His alleged
elevation was something over a fathom in his bald feet,
but had the length of his understandings been in propor-
tion to his spine, he must have measured a good deal less.
One evening we were carousing at Nulty’s pub down
at Wild Oat, and both “Stump” Hagan and “ th’ Long
’Un ” were there. Hagan was the sort of man that didn’t
drink grog—he fairly rushed it. Anyhow, early in the
evening he accidentally drank Stevens’ beer—at least, he
said it was an accident. But Stevens became annoyed,
and made some bitter remarks about “ hums.” “ Stump ”
took the bitterness to heart and offered “ ter give th’
long cow a fly fer it.”
“Gam,” muttered the good-natured Stevens, “I’d
hate ter ’ave ter ’it such a little cove as you.”
That made Stumpy furious. Out of the profanity of
his language we discerned declamations to the effect that
he was"a bigger blankv man than Stevens.
The long cattleman remarked “ Garn 1 ” again and
smiled disinterestedly.
Then the opal-gouger flourished a clenched fist under
the cattleman’s proboscis and fairly howled.
“ I’m a taller blankv man than you sittin’ down, yer
blanky long cow ! ”
“ Betcher 1 ” drawled the long ’un, lounging against the
bar.
Hagan was forcibly cooled down, and level money was
put up—£l each.
A slab stool was placed in position, and the tall stock-
man first took his seat, sitting bolt upright against the
bar door. We scratched his sitting elevation on the
door post with a corkscrew, and then the gouger took
his seat. Stumpy squatted like a Hindu idol while we
scribed the height of his skull on the woodwork, and
when he arose it was found he had topped the cattle-
man’s altitude by a good inch. When the rest of us re-
tired for the night we left the tall dwarf and the short
giant fraternising at obtuse angles to the bar and hitting
successive long beers to leg with great energy.

“ Edyson ” : I see that Albert Spicer, the
British commercial person, concerned himself
somewhat with our birth-rate problem, and made
the,very luminous suggestion that the Australian
baby crop would, in • all probability, be improved
if motherhood’ were a more honored institution.
His notion of jthe proper method of glorifying
motherhood; was “ a badge of honor ” for mothers
Of large families. • The exquisite notion that the
birth-rate would be boosted if the mother of ten
were made a sort of K.C.M.G. displays an in-
genuous type of mind one would scarcely have
expected to discover in big trade. But Albert is
not original. The idea has been put into execu-
tion to my own knowledge. Thai lon, who was
chief engineer on a poultry-run outside Geelong,
and had a reputation for his diverting moral
kinks, tried out the Spicer theory years ago.
When we visited his hen farm I noticed that one
proud barndoor fowl was strutting about, with a
dazzling pewter medal hung on her crop.
“What’s that for?” Tasked. Thallon answered
behind his hand. “ That’s a
medal for egg-laying,”
he said. “I give it
to the hen that scores
highest for the month.
You’d be surprised to
see how it encourages
them.”
Peary, the man who
lost his temper looking
for the North Pole, made
one discovery whilst
among the ice—he ob-
tained some information
regarding the Devil’s wife.
Most people were under
the impression that the Devil
was single ; but, according to Peary,
the two Eskimo who travelled with
him, on their return to camp, yelled
and danced and ascribed their fast
progress to the fact that the Devil was
asleep or having trouble with the
partner of his flames and ashes. How
the small, oily man, whose life is one
long freeze, managed to get hold of
the picturesque idea of the Devil’s
missus is a blank, cold mystery. It
may be that having to stay at home
for six months at a stretch in his ice
wigwam has made him feel bitter
towards the sex. The stale blubber and
the stale arguments and the yelling of
the baby must get terribly trying after about
the fourth month. The only possible outlet for
temper is to rush round to the back of the mansion
and kick the family bear hard. When you come
to reckon things up, the Eskimo has as good an
excuse as anyone for thinking the Devil is
married.

THE SINGING SOLITUDE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When, in dim lights, you wend along
ways of Narbethong,
You’ll hear a quavering elegy,
A quaint, elusive threnody,
A changing, echoing forest song
Sung in the hidden umbrages.
For there, when all the world’s asleep,
The lonely fells and gullies steep
Ring loud with native minstrelsy ;
Like beating wings of melody
It swells and dies down valleys deep
In evanescent cadences.
The trees are pillars in vast halls
Through which some tuneful spirit calls
Some ancient mystic monody ;
And out of night’s immensity
The wandering moonlight frets, and falls
On aisles in chequered traceries.
Out of abysses, wells and rings
No human music born of strings
Of harp, or flute, or psaltery,
Of shawm, or lute, or cymbalry ;
But some aerial being sings
Of dim eternal mysteries.
When, in dim lights, you pass along
The singing fells of Narbethong,
Give ear to that strange monody,
That weird elusive melody—
The echoing, changing forest song
Sung in the hidden umbrages ! ,
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
J. B. Watson, the Bendigo mining mag-
nate, died in 1889, leaving about £500,000 worth
of property which, according to his will, was to
be hoarded up for 20 years and then realised and
divided. Owing to the financial storm that blew
in 1893 the fortune hasn’t accumulated as was
expected; still it has borne up fairly well. The
old man’s pile got a casual mention in court the
other day, when our expensive friend Gentleman
One hired his costly friend with the brief in his
hand and the traces of the dead horse on his head
to ask for “ directions.”
NO WONDER!
No less than three-quarters of an hour behind time the
Sydney express, with the New South Wales Parliament-
ary team on board, arrived at the Spencer-street station.
—Recent News Item.
Last week saw the end of the beginning of
a new sect in Sydney. When Tremayne Dunstan
oarnea financial cropper at the Pitt-street Congre-
gational Church—“ Mr. James’” church, where
the S. M. Hekald’s young men used to graduate
in holiness —he set up a tabernacle of his own,
and called it “the Whitefield Church,” one
Whitefield having been a mighty preacher in
Wesley’s day. Between times the “church”
made a precarious living as a marriage-shop; but
finally T. Dunstan (the gentleman who expressed
the pious wish that The Bulletin office would be
the first place in Sydney that a Japanese bomb
would drop on) moved on to some other stand,
whereupon “the Whitefield Church” fell into
other hands, and last week it straggled back into
the Congregational fold.
x Mrs. Waterhouse, who died at Hawthorn
(Melbourne), recently, was the widow of Rev.
Joseph Waterhouse, at one time in business at
Adelaide, who was drowned in the wreck of the
Tararua on the Maoriland coast in 1881. He was
induced to join the ministry by Rev. .T. Draper,
who was drowned when the London sank in the
Bay of Biscay in 1866. Clerics seem to be a
drownable lot.
Australia’s old cursory acquaintance, Wil-
bur Glenn Voliva, formerly “Elijah” Dowie’s
chief apostle in these parts, has blasted his way
into gaol at last. It was a small matter of defa-
mation somewhere in Illinois, and the Court said
gaol in default of the payment of a 10,000-dollar
judgment. Voliva’s remarks are not recorded ;
but at latest the successor to the prophet had
started to do the six months.
“R.,” from Melbourne :
The faculty of clearing the House by speech is a gift*
like music or a talent for absorbing, unharmed, vast
quantities of beer. In the Reps, the three leading ex-
ponents of this quality are Crouch, Willy Kelly, and King
O’Malley. Crouch is unpopular personally. He has at
one time or another got many members’ backs up by his
harsh, sly and unconscionable methods. There are quite
a number of men who consider they have been let in poli-
tically in some small (but none the less exasperating)
fashion by the man with the doubled-up name. So
whether Crouch has something to say, or whether he has
nothing, the House empties like a punctured billy the
moment he rises.
Willy Kell}' aspires to a mastership in the way of flouts
and jeers. About once in 18 months he gets rid of a pal-
pably pre-considered impromptu that is worth a mild
smile of approfiation. But mostly he gibes with a heavy
hand, and in the polysyllabic phrasing of a Danvell
O’Connor. Willy never uses a word of one syllable
if a longer one is available, and he won’t use
a longer one if he can find a longest one.
He does these things with the elaborate man-
ner of the Prime Minister of drama. And as he
appears to know nothing, or as near nothing as not to
matter, has no ideas beyond those he culls from his daily
Argus or Sydney Telegraph, and is invariably from 18 to
27 miles behind the legislative’bus of the moment, his
orations make for dulness. Kelly drives his fellow-
members forth because be bores them, and in despite of
their better feelings. Personally, he is well enough liked
and esteemed as an amiable youngster of the harmless
order. Of late he has become dejected over the recep-
tions accorded his'speechifyings, and has lapsed into more
or less gloomy silence. And the House is the fuller for it.
King O’Malley, with his hat, his awful voice, his wild
whirling geyser of verbiage, ever ready to be exploded all
over the heads of the people’s elect, is a different proposi-
tion altogether. Crouch and Kelly merely weary their
associates. King O’Malley renders them murderous and
suicidal. He takes rank with Jawbone Neild as the Com-
monwealth’s leading political posturer. All the world
dislikes a posturer—most of all when he is a talkative
posturer. In the present state of our criminal law it is
inadmissible to silence such folk with guns, and as the
Reps, are, on the whole, a law-abiding lot, they take a
firm hold on their emotions when the wild man from Tas-
mania starts, and break for the outer air. There are other
Parliamentary bores. Johnson of Lang acts on the
House like some overproof soporic ; the snarls of Joe
Cook are unpleasing to its collective ear ; Bruce Smith’s
platitudes tire members, though respect for their utterer’s
sincerity makes them conceal the fact to a certain extent.
But the three weariers of the flesh and spirit enumerated
above stand at the head of their class. They are top-
notchers—true champs at the gentle art of House-
clearing.
Sydney papers the other day, noticing the
death of Mrs. H. H. Kater at the age of 89,
stated that the lady was sister of the “first
Attorney-General, Mr. J. B. Darvall, who was
appointed in 1857.” The daily papers’ history is
astray. Mr. Saxe Bannister was the first Attorney-
General, appointed in April, 1824. That, of
course, was before Responsible
Government. When that happened
William Manning was first Attorney-
General ; and Mr. Darvall did not
become Attorney-General until May,
1857, in the Parker Ministry—the
third under Responsible Govern-
ment. He was Solicitor-General in
the first, or Donaldson Ministry,
and for a time also in the third.
Mr. Saxe Bannister, the pioneer in
the law business, fought a bloodless
duel with a brother barrister, Dr.
Wardell, in October, 182(5, and re-
tired from the Attorney-Generalship about the
same time.
An old N.S.W. Pfess Gallery scribe on
the idiosyncrasies of Alf Edden, the strong man
of the N.S.W. Parliamentary Labor party:—
There is probably more individuality in Alfred Edden
than in any dozen members of the N.S.W. Assembly.
From the coalpits of England he emigrated to the
collieries of Newcastle. He is broad-shouldered and
stout-limbed, and only his snow-white hair and iron-grey
moustache convince you that he is no longer young. He
is as athletic as Tommy Burns, with a rich olive complexion
and a robust, ringing voice. He first entered the
Assembly for Northumberland over 18 years ago, and has
held Kahibah since the Redistribution (July 17, 1894). It
would be easier to lift Nobby’s, at the entrance to the
Hunter River, than to shift Edden from Kahibah. He
made an impression with his maiden speech in the House in
Macquarie-street, andhe hasdeepenedthatimpression ever
since. Had he belonged to any other party but cold-shaded
Labor he would have been Minister for Mines long ago.
Had he been less robust, audacious and decisive he might
have been considered sufficiently “ safe ” to lead Labor
in Parliament. There is, strangely enough, an idea in
N.S.W. Parliamentary Labor circles that the leader must
be possessed of Sunday-school traditions, as witness
Joseph Cook and James McGowen. That’s possibly why
Labor, after 18 j'ears in Ma State politics, is least success-
ful in pursuit of power, with the exception of Labor in
Victoria. Edden might have improved the pace if
placed in the lead —and there’s more Parliamentary
wisdom in Edden than in the collective Wade Cabinet-
eers. Generous instincts and family responsibilities
have kept him in the financial position of his
least lucky fellow coalminers, and he has never
been able to gratify a natural desire to revisit the
English coal pit in which he was an apprentice, and to
mingle once more with his “townies.” A few years ago
he lamented that he had never been able to spare £lOO
for that trip. W. P. Crick, then new to office, happened
to hear of Edden’s ungratified wish, and he filled in
and signed a cheque for £lOO. “Here, Alf. This will
see you through. See your people and come back to
bury your old bones with us.” Alf modestly demurred.
Crick insisted. “ No, Billy,” said Edden, next day, with
a lump in his throat, “I can’t cash this. It wouldn’t be
right. My party is supporting - your Government. A
wrong construction would be placed on your kindness.
Take it/old man, and my thanks all the same.” “Well,
give it to the missus for a new dress and a bit on my
horse,” and Crick walked off. In the presence of some of
his colleagues Alf Edden committed the cheque to the
flames, and thereby destroyed his only chance of revisit-
ing England.
Sid Long is another art-bird who medi-
tates a Bullward flight. He hasn’t been doing so
much work lately as was expected of him, and
has given up to the laborious and thankless job
of teaching the time and energy and talent that
were meant for Art. But now he is gathering up
his tooth-brushes and other utensils, and is going
down into the country for a spell of landscape-
painting. Next year he will come back to Sydney
like an elephant refreshed, and with a cubit or two
added to his stature round the waist. Then he
will exhibit his pictures and take his ticket for
Bull-parts and Europe generally.
MATTHEWS, M.H.R. FOR “ THE PORT,”
MELBOURNE.
Clement Giles, the Adelaide man who has
done most to build up the biggest thing in the
way of farmers’co-operative societies in Australia,
threw up the job of managing-director the other
day, and went to England to represent the con-
cern there. Most of Giles’ work for the society
has been done in the last 14 years, during which
period the members have increased from a few
hundreds to about 5000. This year the concern
expects to handle about 4,000,000 bushels of wheat,
besides wool and other stuff ; a big lot of it goes
to the co operative mills run by the big wholesale
co-operative societies in Bull’s Country. Before
Giles took to co-operating he was squatting ; but
the usual drought happened. There was luck in
that particular drought for the S. Australian
farmer—it has saved him from the exactions of
many a middleman since then.
They have discovered a new method of
dealing with militant Laborites in the Cold Land.
They make them knights. Two
years ago, William Crossman
was a stonemason of Cardiff,
and in between whiles he re-
presented, in the Labor interest,
a ward in the local city council
and for so doing was paid a
salary by his party. Ned VII.
came along, and before the
wretched Crossman knew what
was happening, he was knighted.
It ruined him. Last month the
Cardiff Trades Council told
Crossman that it didn’t want a
rep. who bore the stigma of a
knighthood, and it called on Wil-
liam to terminate his agreement
as a Labor Councillor at the end
of the current year. William
complied sadly, but without
argument. He appreciated the
justice of his colleagues’ atti-
tude. Now William is out of
the Labor movement and ad-
dresses his energies exclusively
to stonemasonry. Meanwhile Laborites m a score
of centres are watching the movements of Ed.
VII. with feverish anxiety. They realise that he
may be a fairly ruinous monarch to meet.
E. D. Williams, one of the founders of
the big Castlemaine (Vic.) Woollen Mills, and for
a long time past the managing director, died sud-
denly on October 17. He arrived in Parliament in
James Beef Patterson’s day, after the “retrench-
ment dissolution,” when the ex-butcher said he was
going to cut the civil service to the bone if it
didn’t watch out. (Patterson was a retrencher all
through ; “ I call that a dashed blanky waste,” he
said once when he inspected a slaughter-house
and saw the blood being allowed to run
down the gutter.) Patterson and Gordon had
represented Castlemaine, and it was thought
that Gordon would just squeeze through, carry-
ing the Premier in on his back. But instead
of carrying Patterson in, Gordon couldn’t get in
himself. Williams was first, Patterson second.
This was the first election in which the Vic. Labor
party succeeded in making a decent show. A
year or two afterwards J. B. Patterson died, and
Lawson took his seat. Then, when Castlemaine
electorate was altered and one member was taken
away, Williams decided that he had quite enough
to do with his woollen factory and his mines, so
he left Lawson in undisturbed possession.
Ic is not generally known that Henry
Parkes had, or thought he had, a second Christian
name—Faulconbridge, alleged to be his mothers
maiden name. In a letter to his sister, from
Sydney (Sept. 22, 1840), the future Premier signed
himself “Henry F. Parkes.” A family explana-
tion is that he only thought of adopting Faulcon-
bridge, and finally dissuaded himself. In that
letter he wrote :
I am now in a position which promises not only to pro-
vide me comfortable bread, but to enable me to save a
little money. You have doubtless heard of such gentle-
men as Customs House officers, whose business it is to
catch smugglers, seize contraband goods, etc. Your
humble servant is one of these. I spend most of my time
on board ships, where I have a good deal of time to write
poetry —I have enough already to fill a book, most of
which has been published in a Sydney newspaper. But I
intend to apply some of this waste time to bet. er pur-
pose—that of writing a series of letters to you and other
dear friends in England, descriptive of this country.
The spectacle of Parkes pursuing contraband and
poetry at the same time must have been thrilling.
“Henry Horsecollar ” writes:—
The statement that the deceased theatrical manager,
John Gunn, made his first stage appearance in a piece called
“ The Birthplace of Podgers ” awakens one of these vague
memories that are like the bright ideas we have w’hen we
are just waking up in the morning. There never was any
person named Podgers—such a thing is impossible, just
as it is impossible to be Bodgers and difficult to be Rogers
—but when an old Londoner hunts away back among the
hazy tombstones of memory he finds at last that it is
Mrs. Prodgers he is thinking of. It seems impossible to
be Prodgers, yet the deed was achieved. Mrs. Giacometti
(pronounced “ Jackometti ”) Prodgers was the terror and
misery of London cabmen in the days of my boyhood.
They fled from her as from a pestilence or a mad dog, and
she ran after them and had them hauled up for scattering
into the dim distance when she had signified, by holding
up her umbrella, that she required their services. The only
time the affluent Mrs. Prodgers ever paid the legal fare was
when cab 193,472 had fled from her signal, and she had con-
trived to hire cab 274,391 and go in pursuit. If the second
cab got close enough up to the first cab to enable Mrs.
Prodgers to identify it for purposes of prosecution, she
was so pleased that she sometimes paid cab 274,391 its
legal fare, but not a penny over. More often she paid
6d. or nothing, and then the police court proceedings
consisted of Mrs. Prodgers prosecuting one cab, and
another cab prosecuting Mrs. Prodgers. She was always
being prosecuted by cabs. Cabmen regarded her as
the Devil. She would drive four miles and worry the
driver on the way with 27 different and contradictory in-
structions. Then she would alight and, with a queenly
air, tender the jehti tid. or so. Language had not the
slightest effect on Mrs. Prodgers; besides the cabby
seldom got a chance to use any. The lady’s capacity for
alighting within 2ft. of a policeman was something
wonderful, and with the officer of the law on the
spot the cabby dared not say 1 per cent, of what
he wanted to for fear of prejudicing his case.
Even if there was no policeman on the spot
when Mrs. Prodgers got out of the cab, one was
sure to arrive in a minute or two ; a sorb of wireless tele-
graphy seemed to convey the news that Mrs. Prodgers
was getting out of a cab. The cabby never asked her
name and address when she tendered him the fid. (some-
times she didn’t tender anything); the man who didn’t
know Mrs. Prodgers was unfit to drive a cab in London.
His horse could have told him her name and habit-
ation. The lady herself never used language to
the cabby: she only looked at him like Moses
looking at pork. No rows were ever conducted with more
decorum than those between Mrs. Prodgers and the cab-
men ; she was cold and calm, and he was suffocated by his
feelings and by the knowledge that he was up against an
enemy who knew all the points of the game. In court
the cabby got his legal fare—it was astonishing how
small the legal fare was when Mrs. Prodgers had had
the distance measured and all the by-laws looked
up ; and other penurious clients made a note of the
points she elicited, and preserved the note for future
reference. Cabby also got the smallest possible amount
for lost time. The costs Mrs. Prodgers paid cheerfully.
Then she would pass a friendly compliment with the
magistrate and go forth to hire another cab. It is in-
teresting to learn that the late John Gunn made his
theatrical beginnings in “The Birthplace of Podgers.”
If the London cabbies could have found out the birth-
place of Mrs. Prodgers they would have gone there in
force and burned the place to the ground.

A venerable reminder of the early days
passed out at Bathurst (N.S.W.) last week, when
Alexander Septimus Piper died. He was son of
Captain John Piper, of the old Rum-selling Corps,
who became Naval Officer or Collector of Cus-
toms, and he was born at Point Piper (Sydney) in
1824. His mother was a Miss Sheares, whom his
father married at Norfolk Island. Captain Piper,
when he left the public service, settled at Alloway
Bank, near Bathurst, in 1830. He originally came
from Ayrshire. A brother, Henry Piper, still
lives at Bathurst. The name will not die out, as
Alexander Septimus Piper has left over half a
hundred Pipers behind him.
LADY DUDLEY SERVES TEA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The rector asked Lady Dudley to say a few words, after
she had taken her place as a volunteer to hand tea
f“ Never did tea taste better ,” said one of the Usuals, who
absorbed two cupfuls from the white hands of the
Government House chatelaine). —Australian Star.
The Usual swoons with delight—
Swoons helplessly, wholly away ;
The Universe glows with a light
Unearthly and weird, yet gay ;
And all things are shining and bright
As the midst of a midsummer day.
Aloft, in the shuddering trees,
The sparrows and other winged fry
Are dumb with astonishment. These
Are disorganised sorely ; they fly
With none of their old pristine ease
And a stunned sort of look in the eye.
On sidewalks, in streets and in trams
Men pause with their hands to their brows;
They're wild with amaze. Telegrams
Flash over the wires as the hows
And the whys of the ease, amid damns,
Are frenziedly canvassed. The cows
Desist from revolving the cud.
And lean, in a stupefied state.
On fences, their fainting forms stud,
Each paling and railing- and gate ;
Some even lie down in the mud,
Where they die at a terrible rate.
The restaurants echo with screams
Of protest and agonised rage ;
For mutton like fried bacon seems,
And fish tastes like onions and sage.
The waiters suggest folks in dreams
Or lunatics pent in a cage.
And ever the cause of it all—
The Usual—amid the remains
Of her tea-cup lies flat in the hall
Where she first knew the pleasures and pains—
The miraculous things that befall—
When mere Us-u-als meet Chatelaines.
N.S.W.

Eighteen months ago this bank started an officers’ provi-
dent or pension fund with a grant of £lO,OOO. As that
was a pretty large special vote, it is shown in the fore-
going table. In each of the last two half-years a vote of
£lOOO has gone to the same fund ; but these amounts
have been deducted from profits, being regarded, in fact,
as working expenses. The half-year hasn’t been as profit-
able as most half-years have been lately. Gross profits
have been slightly less, because by recent defalcations
the bank lost £3599 ; and current expenses have also been
considerably heavier. It may be that, to explain the
increase in expenses, the bank has been smitten in its
conscience and has given its clerks a rise all round ; it may
be that it has been spending a lot of money to find out
if there are any more deficiencies on the premises ; or it
may be that repairs or something of the sort have been
heavier than usual. Anyhow, calculated on capital and
reserves, net profits have lately moved this way :
Mar., 1906.. £7 7 11 p.c. Mar., 1908.. £8 7 2 p.c.
Sept., 1906.. 7 6 9 „ Sept., 1908.. 719 3 „
Mar., 1907.. 8 3 8,, Mar., 1909.. 8 0 7 „
Sept., 1907.. 7 2 4,, Sept., 1909.. 614 8 „
For the last three half-years £lOOO has been debited
against profits to cover what may be regarded as the
ordinary vote to the provident fund. Taking into
account the fact that for every £1 of its own money the bank
has the use Of £5 Bs. of the public’s money held on
deposit alone, and taking further into account that the
bank has less than 29 percent, of its liabilities held in coin
and liquid securities, the above figures relating to profits
are by no means high. In fact, they might reasonably be
expected to be higher. One can’t, however, tell
from looking at the outside why profits are small.
Assets may be over-valued, or there may be unpro-
ductive accounts in the books ; and a manager may be
using up undisclosed profits to write down over-vaiued
items. Or, on the other hand, a manager may come to
the conclusion that, just as bad debts have been made in
the past, so some must be made in the present; and he
may be providing for them beforehand—which is a very
wise thing to do, though there are some managers who
are not able to do it, being still too busy wiping out the
sins of their predecessors to attend to any that they may
be committing themselves.
The Colonial’s balance-sheets provide food for
these comparisons:—
Sept., Sept.,

As remarked above, the liquid assets represent less than
29 per cent, of the total liabilities ; which means that, on
the present resources, advances must be curtailed rather
than increased—presuming, of course, that the bank
intends to work on ordinarily safe lines.

Considering that it was born in 1888, just when a whole
lot of financial youngsters caught an epidemic which was
fatal to many of them a few years later, this bank got
through its troubles very well ; it certainly wrote off half
its paid capital, reducing it to £150,000, and knocked off
dividends for a few years. Then, with very good and
influential city backing, it proceeded to push out; and it
is still pushing. Little by little more capital has been got
in by the issue of another 150,000 shares, and handy-
sized premiums on some of these new shares have also
been passed to reserves. To-day, therefore, the capital
is the old £300,000 ; but nowadays it is in 300,000 shares
paid to £1 and liable for another £3. In running the eye
down the column headed profits, this increase of capital
must be kept in mind. As a matter of fact, things
haven’t been quite so good lately as they were. Cal-
culated on the basis of capital and reserves combined,
recent profits have been as follows:
Mar., 1906 .. £8 9 9 p.c. Mar., 1908 .. £9 9 9 p.c.
Sept., 1906 .. 10 3 7 „ Sept., 1908 ... 10 1 8 „
Mar., 1907 ..10 010 ~ Mar., 1909 .. 9 311 ,
Sept., 1907 .. 10 10 10 ~ Sept., 1909 .. 811 4 „
It is still a very satisfactory result; and it will look
more satisfactory still if nothing has to be written off in
the next few years in order to wipe out losses made
through advancing too liberally in these times of high
prices. This particular bank hasn’t, however, lost its
head and rushed its advances along at great speed.
Though it has £lOO,OOO more capital than it had 12
months ago it has less money out in advances. This
shows that movement and some others
Sept.,’oB. Sept.,’o9.

The very big increase in liquid assets has been partly due
to the new capital, but mainly to a big addition to the
interest-bearing deposits, this bank having snared about
£300,000 of the last State loan ; but that Won’t last long.
Probably the Royal didn't really want the money;
still, for one of the smaller banks that has
yet to do some struggling to reach the top, it
was a very good advt. Having all this money on hand
the cash position is very strong ; liquid assets are equal
to 54 per cent, of the total liabilities. It is a virtue, of
course ; but it is quite likely that the bank would very
willingly get rid of some of it if the management could
meet a few satisfactory borrowers.

The Queensland banks, all of them frightfully hard-hit 17
years ago, have battled their way through splendidly. Of
course, Queensland’s natural powers of picking itself up
are very great ; but, apart from that, all the banks up
that way had an almost complete clearing out after the
troubles ; and the new men didn’t think it necessary
to say that the old men had made merely small
mistakes of no consequence. Instead, they told the
shareholders that things were wretchedly bad, and that
the managements would have to keep their nose to the
grindstone for years in order to pull things through.
Starting off on that basis nobody was surprised when
divs. were not paid, and when they did start again share-
holders were grateful even for small ones. And mean-
time, knowing that a return of the public’s full confidence
was what they needed more than anything else, this and
other Queensland banks adopted 'a policy of great
caution. Even now, when it can say that it is
pretty well out of the wood, this bank has 48
per cent, of its liabilities represented by liquid
assets ; and it has not got even the full amount of
its deposits let out in shrinkable advances. If the
advances were only worth 12s. in the £ it could still pay
all its debts. This is a very strong position, and it ought
to secure the confidence which the bank evidently aims
at getting ; and it knows that when a bank gets that,
prosperity and fat dividends are within reach.
But with this policy one has to wait for the fat
dividends. So far the results have been rather poor.
Profits calculated on capital and reserves have lately
been :
Dec., 1905 .. £3 15 7 p.c. Dec., 1907 .. £4 12 7 p.c.
June, 1906 .. 316 4 „ Sept., 1908 .. 411 9 ~
Dec., 1906 ~ 4 211 ~ Mar., 1909 .. 4 9 6,,
June, 1907 .. 4 5 6,, Sept., 1909 .. 4 8 3 „
It is possible, of course, that more or less profit has been
made which has not been disclosed—profit which has
gone to write down over-valued assets ; in other words,
undisclosed profits may have been used to writeoff old un-
disclosed losses. How much more of this there is to do
isn’t made known ; but, accepting the accounts as merely
a general indication of the trend of things, it may appa-
rently be taken for granted that, in the opinion of the
management, things are steadily improving. Re-
cently either it has been much easier for the
bank to get deposits, or it has pursued them
more energetically. Anyway, it has largely increased
its holding. As for instance: —
Sept., ’OB. Sept., ’O9.
Bills and notes .. £52,597 £62,409 Inc. £9,812
Deposits, etc. ~ 1,103,927 1,221,387 Inc. 117,460
Liquid assets .. 443,195 609,043 Inc. 165,848
Advances .. .. 1,226,097 1,206,001 Dec. 20,096
Premises, etc. .. 63,458 63,139 Dec. 319
But the advances have actually shrunk—a rather remark-
able thing; in view of the progress Queensland is making.
It is pretty clear that the Royal of the North reckons it
has made enough losses for one short lifetime ; and it
sniffs a risk now at a very great distance. But that sort
of thing can be carried too far. It is all right to make
sure that you are not taking the wrong vehicle ; but there
is such a thing as missing the right 'bus through hesi-
tating too long on the sidewalk.

There are no flies in this beer. All that has any similarity
to a fly is an insect that floats on the top of the assets
side of the balance-sheet—a small insect labelled “Good-
will.” Nobody who only knows what the published
accounts tell him knows the exact size of this insect; but
the chances are that he isn’t at all prodigious. The
directors have, in fact, obviously done so much hard
writing down that it is almost inconceivable that they
have neglected this item; and it is probable that it is
kept there largely for exhibition purposes—just to sug-
gest to the Cold Tea campaigner that beer profits are not
so gaudy as some people think, and that it takes many,
many weary years for even a very successful beer co. to
wipe out the paper asset with which it started. At any
rate, even though the goodwill insect is still there, this
table shows how liquid assets have accumulated in the
past nine years :
Sept., 1900. Sept., 1909.
Liquid assets ..£127,453 £312,916 Inc. £185,463
Stock .. .. 51,582 138,444 Inc. 86,862
Book debts.. .. 210,037 190,009 Dec. 20,028
Total .. ..£389,072 £641,369 Inc. £252,297
Less debts to public 10,938 23,195 Inc. 12,257
£378,134 £618,174 Inc. £240,040
So, besides paying its dividend, and paying for any new
plant and properties that it may have acquired, the co.
in nine years has put by the comfortable little lot of
£240,040. Nominally, it is true, “properties, goodwill
and plant” don’t stand for as much as they did in 1900.
But that doesn’t necessarily mean that the co. owns less
properties and plant; it may only mean that it has used
profits to write them down to cover depreciation, etc.
Some of this writing-down is admitted ; the usual thing
is £5OOO a half-year, but that is only for plant and ma-
chinery. Various items have moved as follows this cen-
tury

Now, “ the necessity for extensions in order to cope with
the increasing demand for Australian beer has had the
consideration of the directors for some time past; they
therefore have acquired a large area of land in the close
vicinity of the Kent brewery, upon which new buildings
are in course of erection. These will be fitted with a
duplicate plant and utilised without delay.” Evidently
the directors don’t take a gloomy view of the beer out-
look. Still, though the beer outlook may not be gloomy,
it is highly probable that the number of licensed premises
will go on shrinking ; and that will mean more depre-
ciation and more writing-down. Apart from that general
brewery risk, this co.’s position is, to all appearances, re-
markably sound.

When they are not counting up hogsheads the people re-
sponsible for these accounts are evidently studying' the
Old Scripture, and they frame their subsequent remarks
on the model of the stories of Jonah’s fish and Lot’s lady,
which are not to be taken too literally. For many years
the alleged profits maintained a miraculous uniformity ;
but a few years ago they gave up that habit. Apparently
they are throughout to be taken merely as a hint. In
that case it seems (1) that there is a great deal of
writing off to be done ; (2) that in 1906 trade im-
proved very much, and was even better the next year,
but has now eased off considerably. How much more
writing off has to be done can’t be even guessed at. The
co., which began by paying far too much for goodwill,
subsequently paid far too much for properties ; but how
much goodwill stands for isn’t mentioned—these direc-
tors have the lumping disease pretty badly. Still in the
past nine years £24,000 has been written off out of pro-
fits ; and that ought to mean that £24,000 of cash or
substantial assets has taken the place of paper, or it may
be that £24,000 of debt has been paid off. In either
case the co. ought to be (say) £llOO a year better off than
it was 10 years ago. So far as they are itemised, the past
year’s accounts compare with some of their predecessors
like this:—
1901. 1908. 1909.
Liabilities to public.. .. £68,307 £57,259 £59,844
Properties, goodwill, plant,
etc 215,922 227,478 252,363
Freeholds (special account) 41,848 50,623 49,099
Sundry debtors .. .. 77,348 51,173 40,363
Bills, cash, etc 6,826 9,566 3,322
Stock 26,346 24,484 22,029
Receipts 61,716 54,371 50,156
Expenses (including pro-
vision for bad debts) .. 48,623 39,618 36,389
Considerable purchases of property or plant or both have
been made during the year ; but fortunately this has
been managed practically without increasing the debt
owing to the public, which isn’t now very large, though
it might be smaller. Ttie capital is £250,000 and the
resen es are supposed to be £49,332. The return of only
4 per cent, suggests that there not only are no reserves
at all, but that there is a big hole in the capital. If the
reserves were written off and the shares were written
down to 155., £111,832 could come off the assets; and
even that would allow for writing down all shrinkable
assets by one-third. If more than that has to be written
off, then the co. had better give up the idea of writing it
off out of profits, and instead proceed to clean things up
by writing off reserves and readjusting the capital
account.

The foregoing table shows the proceedings of the society
since the cleaning-up. At that time the assets were
worth £180,876, according to the books. According to a
new valuation, however, they were only worth £118,151.
The capital was therefore written down. To simplify
matters, two-fifths of it was wiped out, which, after writ-
ing down the assets, left £10,532 over to go to a con-
tingency fund to provide for repairs, further losses on
sales, etc. The valuators took a gloomy view of the situ-
ation, as it turns out; and, instead of there being further
losses, some of the written-off capital has been
coming back in the shape of profits on sales. This,
of course, has swollen the current profits, and has made
the history of the last few years look better than it really
has been—assuming that the full current profits from
other sources have been stated. The fact that the profit
on sales always nowadays comes out at round figures
suggests that the society is holding some sort of inner
reserve, upon which it is drawing ; but what the size of
this reserve may be there is no telling from the accounts.
As properties have been realised advances have been
made, as the following comparison shows :
1899. 1907. 1908. 1909.
Capital .. ..£98,150 £97,523 £96,387 £96,361
Deposits .. .. 2,997 21,936 22,427 22,644
Other liabilities .. 925 4,020 10,677 2,724
Foreclosures .. 72,683 38,535 37,917 30,414
Advances .. .. 40,510 127,952 138,271 138,408
Other assets .. 5,172 1,329 258 1,720
The rents from properties in possession totalled £3900 ;
and as that is equal to about llj per cent, on the average
book value of the properties held during the year, it is
pretty clear that the book value is exceedingly low ; and
further surpluses may be expected from sales. But those
surpluses can’t go on for ever; more than half the fore-
closures have already been got rid of. Further, as the pro-
perties are sold, more and more of the- beautiful 114 per
cent, return disappears. At the same time it is worth noting
that never since 1899 has the dividend absorbed the whole
of the profit from ordinary business, so that the whole of
the surpluses from sales, and more, has gone to reserves.
The advances are a profitable lot also, bringing in over
8 percent, as an average; but no accounts have yet been
invented which can tell what proportion of the securities
that are lodged against advances will fall into the mort-
gagees’ hands, and what losses will be made over them.
As the figures stand they are all very enticing ; it doesn’t
often happen that a co. can show such results unless it
takes on much heavier liabilities than this co. is doing.
For the total debts of all kinds are equal to only a shade
over one-half of the capital.

The original concern was the Newcastle Permanent In-
vestment and Building Society. In 1895 it viewed its
assets and found that they were mostly foreclosed proper-
ties, and that the building society part of the business had
dropped down an abandoned shaft somewhere. So the
next year this building and investment co. was formed.
The assets were re-valued, and on that re-valuation a large
proportion of the capital was turned over to a contingency
fund, which was designed to fill up the gap between the
book values of properties and the prices they would prob-
ably bring. Ten years ago the foreclosures (book value)
were £108,576; the contingency fund at that time was
£47,222. The foreclosures stand now at £61,090, and the
contingency fund is £40,413. So £47,486 of properties,
plus any that may have fallen in meantime, have been
got rid of at a loss of £6809, which is a long way better
than had been provided for, assuming; that what has been
got rid of is a fair sample of the whole. As the old pro-
perties have been realised others have been bought; and
the position now compares with the past like this :
1899. 1907. 1908. 1909.

Other debts .. 549 276 588 299

Foreclosures .. 108,576 66,785 64,729 61,090
Other property 5,682 52,684 53,732 54,057
Liquid assets .. 9,761 8,707 7,016 2,001
Lately, it will be noticed, the advance business has in-
creased considerably ; part of this is most likely in con-
nection with properties sold on terms. The properties,
including foreclosures, during the past year brought in
£6453, or a shade over 01 per cent., after paying rates
and repairs. Judging by that, one wouldn’t say
that the foreclosures in hand were very seriously
overvalued ; if they are, the other properties must be a
very profitable lot. The strength of the co. lies in the
fact that the capital of the old society was written down
very severely, and a great deal of it which was transferred
to the contingency fund was not lost, after all; so that
what appeared to be the exceedingly doubtful reserves
with which the co. started look now as if they were to a
great extent genuine reserves. There is an insect in the
ointment, no doubt; though it is a port, Newcastle is
also a mining centre, and the property and rent values in
even the best of mining centres are liable to severe bumps.

This co. owns the East Greta freehold and the Stanford
Merthyr leasehold collieries in the Maitland district,
along- with the East Greta railway ; and the railway is a
tidy little asset in itself. It will be noticed that the half-
year’s dividend and the amount written off absorb every
pound of the half-year’s profit, leaving nothing whatever
for reserves. Under ordinary circumstances that wouldn’t
look good. But there is some sort of suspicion that this
co.’s accounts might be made to look better if the
directors felt that way. Thus £1630 is shown as written
off-—£lsoo off plant and £l3O off house property. But
£3421 is already written off for depreciation of plant,
etc., and charged direct to profit and loss; and the
same thing has been going on for some years. Con-
sidering that the co. is maintaining a big output
of coal, and that the coal which has been dug out once
can’t be dug out twice, a solid allowance for depreciation
is no doubt called for. Probably thisco. doesn’t write oft
too much. But it is certain lhat a good many other cos.
write off much less. The co. now shows for the first time
an issue of £28,000 worth of debentures. Apparently
these have been issued to secure an overdraft; and the
small additions that have been made lately to the liabili-
ties have probably been due to the extension of the rail-
way. Additions to the mine plant, etc., have been mostly
paid for out of profits which have been written off against
depreciation. The financial position on Sept. 30 was that
the co. owed £44,963, while its liquid assets (including
stores and coal in transit) totalled £35,512. It is a case in
which the position is only seen indistinctly through a
heavy fog ; but there is very little doubt that, besides
having two of the best coal properties in the Maitland
district, the co. has a money-spinner in its railway, while
its surface rights are increasing in value.

This co. has had a lot worse luck than it deserved. It
started with too little capital—because it couldn’t get
more at the time—and when it simply had to get more
the money had to be bought. Still the issue of 20,844 £1
pref. shares has cleared away a lot of debt ; and on
September 30 the co. owed only £2500 on debentures,
plus £4964 on overdraft. When the coal trade was brisk
the co. had a fire on hand and had to seal up part of the
mine ; and that has always been more or less of a trouble
since. Now, when the mine could stand a good output,
the market is slack ; and that means broken time and
high costs. The co.’s neighbor works what is known as
the East Greta lower seam, a fine 12ft. body. To reach
this seam this co. has been boring off and on for 18
months, but can’t strike it; it got sandstone below the
top seam, but it can’t get the lower seam. Apart from
anything spent on development, and charged up to
capital account, recent resu ts have been :
Mar., 1906 .. Loss £132 Mar., 1908 .. Prof. £BB6
5ept.,1906.. Prof. 144 5ept.,1908.. ~ 2245
Mar., 1907 .. Loss 376 Mar., 1909 .. Loss 583
Sept., 1907.. Prof. 1867 Sept., 1909.. „ 1220
The position, therefore, is that after working about seven
years, and taking out about £160,000 worth of coal, the
co. has put £16,765 more into the mine, to meet working
expenses, than it has taken out of it; and nothing what-
ever has been written off for depreciation, notwithstanding
the extraction of all this mineral. Also £25,821 has been
put into plant, and nothing has been written off that for
depreciation either. If the co. could find a big lower seam
the development and plant might be worth the money ;
if the big lower seam can’t be found, and the life of the
mine is therefore much more limited, the cost of develop-
ment and plant ought to be written off much more
rapidly, and they are in that case already worth a good
deal less than they have cost the co. Coal shares are put
in the investment section on the stock lists ; but this is a
case where the future is as uncertain as that of almost
any copper mine.
* • * *
Just when metal people were saying that
Christmas would see lead at £l4, and that the worst of
the copper situation had been passed, the Bank of Eng-
land rate went up rapidly to 5 per cent., and the German
rate had previously firmed. That meant much dearer
money, and a bigger cost to finance stocks of metal or
anything else ; and the markets of all kinds promptly
sagged. The Broken Hill market had got the bit in its
teeth and was bolting ; and this steadier will give people
time to wonder what sort of fools they were beginning to
make of themselves. So far the zinc convention has
managed to keep up the price of spelter. If that market
should fail, there would be a frightful clatter.

The Callow Revolving Band Screen gives
promise of revolutionising the system of ore classification
in Australia, as it has already done in America, for all
classes and conditions of concentrating ores, with vastly
improved extraction in the mills. At Broken Hill it has
been especially successful. It is an unqualified success as a
fine wet screen, and this is what all the mining world has
been seeking; it is also equally effective on the coarse rafts
from Cornish Rolls. With it there is no excessive wear, no
clogging of the mesh ; it has an enormous capacity and a
small water consumption. The fundamental principle of
this machine is a travelling band or belt of screen cloth
stretched on a drum over which the ore and its carrying
water is spread by means of a distributing apron of
special design. The drum passes over rollers, and the
belt is caused to revolve continuously at a speed adapted
to the nature and quantity of the material to be screened.
The machine is fully protected by patent rights. For
further particulars apply to the Austral Otis Engineering
Co., of Hanna Street, South Melbourne. This firm are the
manufacturers and sole agents for Australasia.* [1

* * * *
There are only two States now which have more
than one Savings Bank —Tasmania and N. S. Wales. All
the other States have one apiece. During the last few
months a system of inter-State transfers was arranged
which enables depositors to pass money from one
State to another. Only Tasmania is barred from this
arrangement. In N. S. Wales it can be done through the
Government Savings Bank, but not through what is
known as the “ Barrack-street Bank ” —the Savings Bank
of N. S. Wales: the bank which the State ought to take
over, seeing that it would admittedly be expected to go
to its rescue if anything happened, just as it had to go m
George Dibbs’s davs. No doubt the fact that the other
N.S.W. bank—the'State Bank—has fixed up a treaty of
reciprocity with the savings banks of the other States
will turn depositors towards it; and that may presently
bring the “ Barrack-street Bank ” to its bearings.
* * * *
Silver keeps at a low price, and Australian lead
and copper mines help to supply a reason—a vast quan-
tity of the white metal is now produced as a by -product.
During 12 months, Chillagoe sent away 756,0000 z. silver,
contained in 6763 tons lead and 2966 tons copper, or
rather more than 80oz. per ton of base metal. Mount
Lyell in 12 months sends away, approximately, 640,0000 z.
silver, contained in 8400 tons copper. The last monthly
production was 655 tons copper, containing 53,8040 z.,
which gave the rather unusual return of 82oz. per ton.
Hitherto Broken Hill has only recovered about half the
silver in concentrating the crude ore to a payable grade
for the lead smelter to handle; but now the zinc works
are recovering much of that metal, which has been
accumulating on the tailings dumps. Looking at the
world’s production of silver, it seems probable that the
average silver contents in base metal in America is even
more than it is here.

Broken Hill Junction North had £6770 to credit
of profit and loss account six months ago, and the total is
now £16,002; so, after writing £7589 off plant to cover
depreciation, theco. is £9232 to the good on the half-year.
The amount written off in the 12 months is equal to 15
per cent., which looks a lot, but is not excessive. So far
as the mill plant is concerned, 20 per cent, isn’t too much
to wipe out every year ; for, especially when working on
stuff like the Junction North’s rhodonite ore, the life of
some of the parts only runs to months, instead of years.
If the co. could reach the end of its capital expenditure
it would promptly pay fairly solid divs. But the call for
new plant, or for the remodelling of the old plant, has
been incessant. Therefore, though a net profit of £9232
is shown for the past half-year, the co.’s finances are not
that much forrarder. The position on March 31 was :
Liquid assets. —Concentrates .. £7,604
Sundry debtors .. 5,113
Cash balances .. 2,763
Total .. £15,480
Liabilities.—Sundry creditors ~ 8,247
Surplus liquid assets .. £7,233
And on Sept. 30 the position was:—
Liquid assets. —Concentrates .. £3,654
Sundry debtors .. 11,950
Cash balances .. 9,121
Total .. .. £24,725
Liabilities.—Sundry creditors .. 8,891
Surplus liquid assets.. £15,834
That is a distinct improvement : after paying for plant
alterations or renewals and some additions, the co. is
thus £B6Ol to the good, whereas in the previous half-
year, though a net profit of £9752 was shown, the finances
went £2651 to the bad. As remarked just now, almost
everything depends on the amount which the co. feels
called on to spend on plant. If it can keep the total
down to about £7500 a half-year, then, on the last six
months’ experiences, and even with the past six months’
low prices, there is an easy shilling a share (£9000) per
half-year ; and every £1 over £l3 ior lead is worth another
9d. per share.
* * * *
The De Bavay zinc people have called in
Bewick, Moreing and Co. to report on their probable
profits. It is a curious position, because 8., M. and Co.
are general managers of the Zinc Corporation, the other
big Broken Hill zinc concern. However, neither of them
love the Sulphide Corporation, which is some sort of a
bond ; and on account of the European Zinc Convention
there isn’t much rivalry between zinc producers anyhow.
By about April next the De Bavay Co. expects to be
handling at the rate of 300,000 tons of zinc tailings per
annum ; and the output is expected to reach 100,000 tons
of spelter concentrates, worth at least 40per cent. Upon an
output of from 90,000 tons to 100,000 tons 8., M. and Co.
estimate a profit of from £109,000 to £120,000, taking
spelter at £2l. At £22 the profit should, they say, be
from £123,000 to £136,000.
If there is this profit in it for the De Bavay Co.,
the outlook is pleasant for the B. H. South Co. The
South is to supply the De Bavay Co. with most of its
tailings ; and the South must get the same amount as the
De Bavay makes in profit. First the South gets 3s. 6d.
per ton ; then the De Bavay Co. pays its costs out of the
proceeds ; then it pays itself 3s. 6d. per ton of tailings;
and the balance it whacks with the South Co. So each
co. puts precisely the same amount into its pouch, so far,
of course, as the South’s tailings are concerned.
Whether spelter can be kept at or past £2l is
another matter. In 1895 it was down as low as £l4 155.;
even last year it touched £l7 17s. 6d. The Zinc Conven-
tion may work wonders. But it would be a lot easier for
a combine to work wonders if there wasn’t this huge out-
put of new metal coming on to the market.
» * * *
The acid-oil flotation process, which is respon-
sible for all this new output of zinc, has hitherto been
applicable only to certain classes of ore ; but the experi-
ments of E. J. Horwood, the B. H. Prop.'s Broken Hill
manager, promise to sweep in much more material.
Hitherto his tests have been in the laboratory ; but bulk
parcels of Broken Hill slimes and complex Tasmanian
sulphides have been sent to Bendigo School of Mines for
treatment by the Horwood method. On Tuesday
word came from there that from both of these
materials a high-grade zinc concentrate was being
won, with a satisfactory extraction. If costs are
all right this will be very good news for the B. H. Prop,
(vvhich has the slimes nuisances on a large scale), and
probably better news still for the West Coast of Tas-
mania. And the silver-lead-zinc section of the West
Coast of Tasmania wants all the good news it can get
just now.

amounts are lumped together. The £113,653 “ applied
to the general purposes of the co.” are as mysterious as
the fate of the Waratah.
The second mortgage issue is a dear one too :
£260,000 of the £300,000 has been underwritten at 5J per
cent, commission, and the underwriters have a call on
deferred ordinaries at par to the extent of 30 per cent, of
their underwriting. So £16,500 goes in commissions
alone ; and as the new stock bears 6 per cent, interest,
the 0.0. C. cannot be too cheerfully regarded even by
the people who are so deeply in it that they must either
smile and part up or die.
* * • «(
There is a very restless feeling|among Newcastle
(N.S. W.) coal miners just now ; and anything may happen
before long. The miners always have enough grievances
to hang a strike on ; and, whether a strike happens or not
is therefore mainly a matter of the local feeling, or temper,
or whatever you like to call it. Just at present, the temper
is such that a very little would cause trouble ; but a
decision to maintain the present 11s. a ton selling rate
for another year will go a long way towards averting it.
The owners’ federation now has that matter under con-
sideration ; and a change from the present price isn’t
likely.

Vegetable Creek, the New England (N.S.W.)
deep-lead tin show in which Adelaide is interested, has
had a disappointment; the shaft didn’t bottom on wash.
They must now drive for the gutter, which of course
wouldn’t be a serious matter if the co. could .be quite sure
that its shaft is good enough to stand much racketing.
But it can’t be at all sure of that.

Main Ridge Deep Lead, Rockley (N.S.W.), is
developing a run of cement wash covered with basalt.
Drives into the hill have opened up a run of about 100 ft.
of washdirt. The cement is generally about 2ft. thick,
and dish prospects are said to show from Sdwt. to lOdwt.
to the load. The ridge should pay all right if it has 2ft.
of joz. dirt for any considerable distance.

J. E. Carne, assistant Government Geologist
(N.S.W.), has been out West looking at the Euriowie and
Waukaroo tinflelds ; but he doesn’t think much of them.
The shoots are too short and irregular. The one property
in whose favor he is inclined to make an exception is the
Kantappa.
The Harden (N.S.W.) will be worth watching .
for a few weeks. During the last day or two the lode has
been tapped in the crosscut from the bottom level
(600 ft.). So far nothing is known about it except that
the drill borings show it, where tapped, to be very poor,
worth only ss. per ton. (Holes were kept ahead of the
working face, because a heavy flow of water was ex-
pected.) On the data obtained from the upper levels
payable ore was not looked for at the point where
the crosscut was planned to strike the lode :
and a drive east will be put in at once in search of the
shoot of richer stone. The result of this driving will be
of much importance, because ore from this level will
before many months be relied upon to supply the new
mill, which has just been given a successful trial run. If
things don’t turn out as well as they should at 600 ft,,
another crosscut and drive will be put in at 720 ft.; the
shaft is already deep enough for that, purpose. As, hovv-
ever, the lode has been picked up within 18in. of where it
was expected at 600 ft., there is more reason than ever to
expect that the pay shoot will also be picked up by driv-
ing east at that level just as it has been at the levels above.
* * * •
Cheerful excerpt from Treasurer’s Moore’s
budget speech (VV.A.): “Developments have shown that
our mines live and retain high values at depth. Many of
the old districts which previously seemed abandoned
have shown renewed activity, and many new discoveries
have been made which considerably enlarge our known
auriferous belt. ... I have previously quoted a few of the
ore reserves of several of our mines, and I think I am
justified in again drawing attention to the wealth and the
prosperity of certain holdings, omitting for obvious
reasons the names of the companies. No. 1 has an ore
reserve of 731,426 tons, being seven years’ supply at the
present rate of output, and is estimated to contain
£2,500,000 worth of gold. No. 2 has a reserve of
510,000 tons, being four years’ supply, and estimated
to contain £1,055,409 worth of gold. No. 3 has
a reserve of 430,414 tons, being four years’ supply, and
estimated to contain 10‘21dwt. of gold per ton. No. 4
has a reserve of 1,055,409 tons, being four years’ supply,
and estimated to contain £2,500,000 worth of gold.
These figures, combined with the fact that, although
only a young mining country, lodes of value and width
have been located at over 2300 ft. deep, speak volumes.”
No doubt they do ; but why not go further and squarely
face the fact that without these four mines, which are
getting poorer and not richer, the industry would look
very sickly? Also, why throw so much limelight on the
alleged renewed activity in mining, and keep in the
pitch-dark background the fact that, despite the “ new
discoveries” and the enlargement of “the auriferous
belt,” etc., the gold yield is steadily decreasing? It is
this refusal to squarely face facts which has led to
Westralia having had six deficits in succession.

The options held by 8., M. and Co. over the
Frasers group of mines at Southern Cross (W.A.) are for
the London and Australian Exploration Co. Some dia-
mond drilling and other prospecting is to be done, and if
sufficient inducement offers, it is intended to equip and
work the properties on a big low-grade scale. In addition
to these and other options, the Exploration Co. holds
big interests in the Lancefield and Northern mines,
and it also has a big block of Great Fitzroy shares.
* * * *
The prospect of Boulder Main Reef (W.A.)
getting the extension of the Boulder shoot at 3000 ft. has
more than once been viewed with doubt on this page, and
now the local paper sorrowfully admits that, as the work
done in the present bottom level (2350 ft.) shows that the
shoot makes no attempt to bridge the 1000 ft. or so
between the present end of payable values and the
“Reef’s” boundary, the chance of the latter property
getting the shoot at the depth mentioned isn’t enhanced.
In view of this admission it looks as if an amalgamation
with Chaffers is the only thing that will save the co.,
as it won’t be an easy matter to coax shareholders to
respond to a further assessment sufficient to pay off Lionel
Robinson’s mortgage of £7OOO and provide enough work-
ing capital to go deeper.
* * * *
Lewis Aarons is in the West busy fixing up the
amalgamation of Chaffers and the Boulder Main Reef.
The idea is that the new Chaffers Co. should be in
750,000 shares, of which the main reef should take two
for every three now held, and the balance of 141,000
should be held in reserve, and passed out as money is
wanted. The cheerful feature about Aarons’ scheme is
that he doesn’t propose to assess the present shareholders.
“Pindan”: Out beyond Menzies and Davy-
hurst, in the North Coolgardie district (W.A.), several
big parcels of stone have been treated for 15, 17 and
18dwt. respectively. One lot of 400 tons, crushed at a
State battery, returned a clear profit of £750. This rep-
resented the work of four men for eight,weeks. Yet the
show was rejected after being under option for several
months. Of course, working miners have no guinea pigs
to support, neither have they a horde of experts, clerks
and typists on the premises. At Meekatharra, Sand-
stone, Magnet and all over the Westralian fields, pro-
spectors no longer chase after the company shark and the
promoter of syndicates with pocket-worn specimens, but
set to work with pick and gad. The State batteries
enable them to prove the actual value of their shows
without all the fanciful assays and other frillings of the
professional brass-plated crowd who control so many of
the big mines throughout Australia. For the courageous
prospector, with pluck and some experience of mulga and
spinifex life, there is no finer field than Westralia. Divi-
dends may fall off so far as the big mines are concerned,
but the actual workers will probably go on getting more
and more.
* •» * »
Stannary Hills (Q.), which reconstructed in
October, 1908, issues accounts for the period to the end
of July. The nine months have been spent mainly in
getting things ready for a fresh start. A new shaft had
to be sunk at the Ivanhoe, the mill had to be recon-
structed, and other big work done. Exclusive of £2165
spent on the new shaft, and other amounts charged up to
capital, the expenditure was £18,052 more than the
receipts. Interest is a heavy item ; and the company’s
Oohen looks in regularly, whether the mill is producing
or not. The bill for the nine months was £4453.
The trouble consists of £7470 of 10 per cent,
debenture stock, £63,850 of 6 per cent, ditto, and
£14,416 of loans; besides that the co. owed
£4870 on July 31. There are 650,000 shares, but they
have been written down to 2s. 6d., of which 6d. has not
yet been called up. Of the shares 100,000 are preference.
Even though the new mill should earn a profit of £2500
per month—and that is about the most sanguine estimate
yet made—there is a lot to do before shareholders, and
especially holders of ordinaries, can get a bit. They may,
no doubt, come in before the debentures are all paid off ;
but the 10 per cents, will surely be wiped out first, and
then a sinking fund will be called for on the balance. The
future certainly isn’t as black as it was once; but
whether the shareholders will ever get their money back
is still by no means certain. Besides the mine the co.
has an asset in the train way, which has been written down
to £50,529 ; but in the nine months there was only a
balance of £34 on the tramway account, after crediting it
with £607 for company’s own traffic, and without debit-
ing any interest to it.
The 0.K., in North Queensland, has now a
strike on hand ; or if it isn’t a strike it’s a lockout. The
men wanted a little more to come their way; and
the co. thereupon paid off all hands. Looked at from the
shareholders’ point of view, the smelter should have been
shut down long ago ; but it doesn’t suit anybody’s book
to have the mine closed down also.
* * * *
New Brilliant Freehold, Charters Towers, will
have its new air-compressor at work shortly, and develop-
ment work should then be quicker, and the output larger.
The extension of No. 2 east towards the Brilliant boundary
is a bit of interesting prospecting. There is a slide or
crosscourse somewhere ahead of No. 2 level; but it is
argued by the hopeful punter that, because the Brilliant
reef made again on the western side of the slide, the Day
Dawn has also a good chance of struggling to life again on
the eastern side, in the Brilliant P.C., or (at a deeper
level) in the Brilliant Block. Nevertheless, Charters
Towers is looking rather anxiously at the little quartz
vein called a Day Dawn as it comes along eastward. For
though it is quite true that the Brilliant reef did make
again after the slide came in, the Freehold was never able
to pay a dividend out of it.
« • * *
East Mexican, Charters Towers, has fixed up
its little difficulty about boundary rights with its neigh-
bor, the New Brilliant Freehold, and is by this time proba-
bly busy breaking down some of the good stone left at the
end of No. 6 level. Nobody knows how far this body of
stone will keep parallel to the boundary, which runs east
and west, because the reef has sometimes a slight trend
north of east. Still the prospect of getting another good
crushing has put more hope into this venture.
* * * *
Pfeiffer’s Day Dawn has had a good breaking
down at No. 9 footwall level, and hopes are held out that
the stone will extend upwards towards the winze from
No. 4, which also located good stone. Simultaneous dis-
coveries on the footwall reef gave Pfeiffer’s a good rise in
front of a 3d. call. Prospects have been indifferent on
both hanging wall and footwall sections of this reef ; but
another deep level is about to be explored, and as the
stone in both reefs carries mineral, there is certainly some-
thing to gamble on.
° * * * *
According to a report furnished by the Queens-
land Mines Department, 379,650 tons of ore were smelted
within the State last year, and only 21,004 tons were ex-
ported. The latter included 11,000 tons gold concen-
trates, 4500 tons copper ore, and 2000 tons lead ore.
Smelting and realisation charges eat a big hole in the
cake, even after the ore has been put on a railway truck
or planked down on the wharf. With total deductions
amounting to from £6 to £8 per ton, there is mighty little
chance of a big export trade in crude ore and concen-
trates, because the ore has to be phenomenally rich to
Stand such heavy costs. Results seem to be about the
same whether ore is sent to a local smelter or exported to
'N.S.W. or Europe; but deductions vary with the state
of the metal market and with the quantity of zinc or
arsenic in the stuff. One curious feature of Queensland’s
trade in minerals is that Stannary Hills sends its black
tjn concentrates to Singapore, in the Malay States. An
export tax on black tin from Singapore has helped the
lbcal tin smelter, so the Chow capitalist is able to import
Queensland tin ore, although right at Stannary’s back
door the Irvinebank tin smelter is in full blast, and the
quality of Australian tin is so good that it commands a
premium. # # *
Long Tunnel (Vic.) on September 30 stood like
this: Liquid assets, £17,476, apart from percentages held
back on mining contracts, etc. ; liabilities, £1489 ; sur-
plus, £15,987. Out of that a dividend of Is. was paid this
month—£l2oo. The half-year’s profits were £3441, of
which £3OO went as a final subsidy to the Moe-Walhalla
railway. The co. is already getting some benefit from
this work ; and the line will be finished right through by
the end of the year. Of the liquid assets, £14,000 is
bottled up in the reserve fund ; and in view of the look
of things in the bottom levels, the shareholders probably
feel pleased now that the directors had the pluck to hang
on to that much.
* • * *
The Vic. Government has decided to go straight
ahead with the Bill authorising it to do everything and
anything that may be necessary to enable it to work a
State coal mine on the new Powlett River field. The State
ishanging on to 20 square milesof thatcoalcountryforits
own purposes, and, unless the experts’ arithmetic is all
out, there are 30,000,000 tons in that little lot. And
there is room for S. Australia, too, the Victorian
Government remarks. S. Aus. had an idea of spend-
ing about £200,000 in opening up a mine on the Maitland
field (N.S.W.); the Victorian Government suggests that
it should come in at Powlett, where the sinking is, say,
only 400 ft., as against 1400 ft. at East Maitland, and the
train haulage only about 11 miles. The royalty men-
tioned is from 2d. to Is. per ton. The Victorian Govern-
ment wants about 400,000 tons a year for its railways,
etc. ; Si Aus. wants about 160,000 tons for its railways ;
and its tramways and other public services could take
another 150,000 tons or more.

From an ex-Australian in S’Africa: “J. C.
Watson is understood to have secured a dredging claim
on the Limpopo River, 100 miles from Petersburg, in a
wild and almost inaccessible part of the country. He
acquired this proposition from a syndicate in Durban.
This syndicate never saw the show itself, but sent an
honest man to work it with niggers; and after four
months’ trial and the recovery of 7oz. of gold at a cost of
about £2OO, the man got disgusted and threw the job up.
Some members of the syndicate tried to unload shares in
town (Durban), but the public sniffed, and passed. The
sluicing claim is known as the Thabina mynpacht. I
can’t imagine how Mr. Watson came to purchase this
show, for there are far better ones obtainable by anyone
who wishes to peg a mynpacht for 100 miles above this
ground on the Limpopo River.” Still, before the Aus-
tralian public will be asked to speak it will have a report
from P. N. Tewksbury; and he probably knows more
about dredging ground than even the honest gentleman
who worked it for the Jew syndicate.
* * * *
The Blythe River Iron Mines, Ltd., which owns
a splendid deposit of iron on the Tasmanian coast, is still
busy doing nothing. The idea originally was to put up
furnaces and other mill plant near Sydney, but the idea
has been hung up for nine years. “ There is very little
to say,” the directors report, “as we are still waiting
until a favorable opportunity arises for taking decisive
action.” The co., which is in 500,000 shares, nearly all of
them issued to the old syndicate which owned the pro-
perty, still has £5071 in hand ; and the interest on that
amount pays about half the expenses.

WHEN THE HAIR FALLS OUT—
You probably do not begin to worry about it at once.
It is only after you have noticed that it has o-rowri
appreciably thinner that you think it is high time that
something must be done to correct the trouble.
Have you tried many advertised remedies without food
results ? Nearly everybody has. Those so-called cure-all
remedies are useless, sometimes even injurious ; and you
need individual treatment, not necessarily expensive.
This is an extract from one patient’s letter :
“I have finished the preparations for my hair. Since
using them, two months only, my hair has improved
considerably in every way. It has grown two inches
longer, and has now a splendid gloss.' Also, it has got
much thicker.”—G. D. Denman.

Hello, deah! Surely you’re
not in such a hurry as not to
be able to spare a minute—of
course you’re going to-morrow
night—they say it’ll be awfully
jolly —but this weather, isn’t
it simply dreadful ? Can’t 1
persuade you to have a cup of
tea with me, I’m simply dying
to hear what you’re going to
wear! and its not good for you to
go so long without something,
you’ll have such a headache.
I’ve been rushing from
shop to shop all day and am
nearly dead, and for the last
half hour I’ve had one word
drumming itself upon my brain—
Robur! Robur!! Robur!!!
until I simply felt 1 must come
and get a cup. I always find it
brightens me up and makes me
feel ready to start right off
again, and it doesn’t affect my
nerves in the slightest. I’m sure
Robu r ’ s quite the best tea —
don’t you think so, deah? Which
do you use at home ? The No.
1 Grade ! so do we—mother
says its the most saving tea she’s
ever used and it must be if she
says so.
