A
WOMEN’S
LETTERS
Sydney, October 26, 1909.
My Dear Arint,—
“Lend us your aid,” said the women of Sydney
to visiting High Churchman Woolcombe, and he
replied in effect, “Lend me your ears.” And
behold the Women Society, political and working,
went forth to the Sydney Town Hall and lent
all its ears as commanded. It hasted in mul-
titudes to hear the counsel of the prophet;
with its enchantments and the sorceries that it had
practised from its youth, it hurried, saying, “I
am, and none else beside me.” Many were dis-
mayed, especially the mothers of the more youthful
virgins, for word had gone forth from the pub-
lishers to the populace that no man save he that
was to speak should pass within lest he be turned
back greatly ashamed. As the Biblical style is a
little trying to one used to plain Australian, I will
now drop it and proceed to assurances that men
and youths might have been present at the
address without the faintest embarrassment to
themselves or their feminines.
Lady Chelmsford introduced "My friend, Mr.
Woolcombe,” in a few well-chosen cooings, and
then sank into a velvet chair facing the 3000-
odd audience. Behind, or flanking her, were
the Simpson lady, Mrs. Allen Taylor, Mrs.
H. H. Dangar, Mrs. Monty Severn, and
about a hundred other women of more
or less importance. If anyone really expected
an address beginning “My dear Sisters, let us
first consider the evils of tight-lacing, the doubt-
fulness of the decollete Direotoire, and the danger
of the moonlit, sequestered place,” she was dis-
appointed. Mr. Woolcombe has a voice that
annihilates distance—the distance inside the
Town Hall. He spoke a common-sense,
popular form of counsel—spoke it fervently
and with emphasis. The engaged girl might
induce her affianced one to turn his eyes
from the scratching board to eternity instead of
joining him in senseless frivolity. The enormous
influence of the woman . . . he quoted historical
instances. But the major portion of his exordium
was directed to mothers in relation to their sons.
The pathetic anecdote was frequent; so were the
tears it called forth. Ninety per cent, of the
audience was worker manual worker at
that, if appearance counted—so it took com-
placently the speaker’s entreaty not to continue
to lead “a butterfly existence.” The meal un-
shared was food unblest; they hoard in vain what
love should spend ; self-ease was pain ; their only
rest was labor for a worthy end. A toil that gains
with what it yields, and scatters to its own in-
crease, and hears while sowing outward fields the
harvest song of inward peace.
That’s the gist of what he said, only he didn’t
rhyme it. Then damsels took up a collection
amounting, in (mainly) threepenny bits, to
£55 15s. sd. Several yims were sung. Lady
Chelmsford drove off with her attendant lancer
clattering behind, Mrs. Mayoress Taylor honked
away in a dark-bodied car with red-running gear,
and the other “ butterflies ” flew their many ways.
I have heard a good many sermons in my time.
Most of them, I am bound to own, conveyed little
to me. They didn’t so much leave me cold as
puzzled. The normal cleric has a mystifying way
of flooding his subject with words and words and
then more words till the waters of obscurity rise
around it and hide it from the view of all who are
not inspired. This is the sort of spiritual good
cheer I have had handed out to me from a thousand
pulpits:—
Ah ! brethren, but the Lesson to be learned, the car-
dinal, heaven-sent Lesson, is that tiie Spirit is not so
much of the Word as the Word of the Spirit. How many
of us is accorded the grace to go away into the silences,
the lone, vast places of earth, and brood upon that mighty
thought? How many of us have what I may venture to
call the moral intrepidity to emulate the Forerunner,
and retire to the desert where the flesh-pots are not and
naught but locusts and frogs abide, there to cope unaided
with the legions of the Lost? Alas ! not many, I fear me
much, in these materialistic times. No, brethren, we
prefer the gay lights of the tea-shops, the tram-rides, the
visits to waxworks and picture shows ; and as we flutter
our little hour, like wayward moths around these the
candles of our desire, the great basic truths of the Gospel
are relegated to oblivion, and the Spirit which is of the
Word and yet is not the Word nor in any way connected
with the Word is suffered to mingle and commingle.
And so on. I have listened dazedly to reams and
reams of this sort of thing. I well-nigh burst my
intellect trying to resolve what it was all about,
and failed every time. Yet in pews all round me
were folk, no cleverer-looking than myself, who
raised their eyes and hands admiringly at the
finish and murmured “lovely,” “sweet” and
other words of praise.
Mr. Woolcombe is staying with the Chelmsfords
at Cranbrook, and is saving souls in bundles.
Mrs. T. H. Kelly organised a garden f§te last
week in aid of All Saints’ Church, Woollahra, and
it transpired on Wednesday, on the grounds of the
Glenyarrah home. On the same afternoon another
of these entertainments was raging at Llandilo,
Strathfield, the abiding-place of the Sydney
Joneses. It is assumed that the recrudescence of
the garden or lawn f6te idea had its origin in the
“ Belle of New York” revival, wherein it will be
remembered all hands finish up at one of these
revels. For hours Double Bay trams and Strath-
field trains bore away citizenesses (and here and
there a citizen) who looked more or less like
Queens of comic opera, Blinky Bills, Mamie
Clancys, and others whom we have come to re-
gard as typical lawn filters, and with them were
small, stout, exasperated children, and in the
children’s hands were clasped trumpets and toy
bears and so forth, the products of the stalls.
The Glenyarrah show realised over £lOO, and the
Llandilo one (it was in aid of the Queen Yic.
Home for Consumptives) a few pounds less.
What a horror the young bride of the present
day has for whiskerings ! King Alfonso of Spain,
while in Madrid during the recent troubles, grew
a luxuriant set of “side-levers ” —or, as Mr. Frank
Richardson, the prominent English whisker-
expert, has it, “ let-us-prays.” When thev had
bourgeoned to the full, he hastened to San
Sebastian and displayed his new possessions to
Queen Ena. She immediately covered her baby’s
eyes, rang for the infant’s attendant and had it re-
moved, and then opened outon her disfigured spouse
in such fashion that he tore in disorder to the bath-
room and mowed off every vestige of the herbage.
A few generations ago the humble little wife—the
one who sat fashioning samplers in anticipation of
the return of her lord—would have well-nigh
swooned with joy at the thought that he was to
bring home with him a full rich crop of whiskers.
Who does not recall the continual references of
heroines of fiction, about Thackeray’s time, to So
and So’s “divine,” “delicious” and “superb”
whiskers? Now any trace of face-grass renders
the young husband a by-word. Who shall explain
these things? Alfonso wanted to argue that it
tended to make him look “like an Admiral.”
“Like a butler,” said the Queen, acidly. It was
at this stage of the discussion that the King left
for the bathroom.
Dear smiling George Reid attends kirk not
quite so often as he might, but he is known to be
a son of, and sympathetic with, the Manse. So the
Moderator of the Presbyterian Assembly, noticing
him among the spectators the other day, cordially
invited him to step up and speak a piece. George
at once complied, but modestly, and as though
stung by conscience-pangs, declared himself
unworthy of the honor and half sobbed out-
artful old comedian that he is—something about
the return of the Prqdigal Son. But, ye ken, it
possibly wasna the Prodigal Son at all of whom
the rev. Moderator bethought himself on espying
amongst his audience the plenteous adiposity of
George. It may only have been the fatted calf.
Still, as usual, the plausible’George made the very
best of the situation.
“Never miss a meal, rqy friends,” counselled
George aforesaid at a recent meeting of the
N.S.W. Optimists’ League. Seeing which, a pes-
simistic Broken Bay fisherman sent him a gigantic
fat schnapper. The deceased fish, explained the
sender, had been a member of the Broken Bay
Optimists’ League, and its fine condition bore wit-
ness that it had acted upon George’s ad vice ; in
fact, not missing its last meal (squid bait and a
big hook) had been its undoing. In the circum-
stances reverent burial was besought of Mr. Reid
for the corpse of his dpluded scaly disciple.
George wrote back saying that the remains had
had “a most enjoyable funeral.”
Curious to what an extent the fiction about Mr.
Dan O’Connor’s marriage to a foreign countess is
accepted as fact by a lot of Sydneysiders. Old
Father Time is still the unmarried widower he
was when he left us. Some of his friends in
London must have invented the countess for
Australian consumption. And she has been ex-
tensively consumed.
Last week the Horticultural Society held a
rose show at St. James’ Hall. Roses, masses
of lilac, lily of the valley and sweet pea—
the visitors were like to die of them in
aromatic pain, like one of Poet Pope’s ladies.
But in spite of the show being labelled a Rose
Meeting a bench of the ne>v amaryllis dominated
the hall. This gorgeous, bold-looking flower,
blood-red or streaked with yellow, is a hybrid,
and is at home to its adorers under the generic
title of hippeastrum.
Miss Kate Hay, a step-sister of the late Dr.
John Hay of Coolangatta* was the prettiest ivory
satin charmeuse bride of last week, or, for that
matter, of last year. She wedded Dr. T. G.
Crowley, of Melbourne, at St. Stephen’s Church,
and, after the latest thing in receptions had been
held by her mother at the Australia, the bride got
herself into a prune, braided tailor-made, and the
happy twain (she and her bridegroom, not she and
the tailor-made) went off to the Mountains.
The Crowley-Hay nuptials occurred quietly at
the Phillip-street place of worship of the bride,
last week. The groom is an R.C. and the bride a
Presbyterian. The lofty attitude of the Church
which Dill lives to assail was responsible for a
priest losing the wedding-fee, in this case. In de-
ference to the rigid rules pf her future spouse’s
faith, the lady had agreed to be married in his
church, and so on. Straightway uprose a cleric
who said that that wouldn’t suffice 5 she must
also turn Catholic. It was more than any normal
man of spirit could endure, and in firm though
courteous terms the prelate was urged to go away
and play. This attitude of the R.C. Church in
regard to mixed marriages seems to be getting
distinctly over the odds, and is well calculated
to drive even the ultra-devotional to Primitive
Baptistry or some other weird faith.
Afterwards the extensive Hay clan and its
friends gathered at the Australia and congratu-
lated the newlyweds with all their might. Still
later a chosen few turned up in boxes and stalls at
“The Belle of New York,” the young women of
the party looking festive with bouquets and such-
like matrimonial emblems. The happy pair
left during the afternoon for the Port Hack-
ing residence of the Arthur All—, that is
to say they motored for their honeymoon to the
Blue Mountains. The punt man at Tom Ugly’s
Point, the Rockdale policeman who streaks after
scorching motorists on a bicycle with his red hair
streaming in the breeze, the centenarian parrot
that curses dodderingly in his pub by the sea, and
other old-established landmarks familiar to
society brides and ’grooms are aghast at the
almost revolutionary originality of the pair in
giving the National Park site the go-by, and the
Mayor and aldermen of Warumbul are talking
heatedly of demanding back the district’s Dread-
nought money by way of protest.
Captain Verney, the prospective bridegroom of
the hour, leaves for Melbourne on Friday with
Lord Chelmsford, and there proposes to abide for
a space and to participate in ceremonies not un-
connected with the worship of the fetich Horse or
Prad. He is to be back in Sydney on the 10th
November, for on the 11th he will enter into an
honorable undertaking to pay for a lady’s clothes
all the days of her life. Lord Dick Nevill, an old
friend of the Walker family, will accompany the
young man on his pilgrimage Sydneywards.
Cuppers who have gone to the Southern capital
from Sydney include Mrs, Gordon Wesehe, Mrs,
Edwin Ryrie, Mrs, P. Morton, Mrs. Ewan
Fraser, Mrs. Donald Maclntyre, the Littles of
Craignish, Mrs. A. Weston, the J. C. William-
sons (who have now a house at Aspendale) and the
fighting Tommy Burnses. Your Akenehi goes
down on the coming Saturday.
Bird-man Colin de Fries, brought hither by the
Taits to fly a Wright machine, is mentioned as
due in Sydney early next month by the Orontes.
He was at the Australia a few months ago
answering questions about flying, music and
waltzing, and few knew he had dashed back
Londonward. He is .to make his first skyward
movement in Sydney, and his friends hope be
won’t be nicknamed, as was Latham, the “ May-
fly.”
Contralto Kate Rooney pauses awhile for a rest
in this cit}' of the waters. She has been leading
the complex life while on tour, as witness her
hand: —“ We’ve been riding camels at Bourke,
eating delicious oranges at Pera Bore, investigat-
ing copper ore down in the earth’s innards at
Cobar, saying ‘ How marvellous ! ’ and * Good
gracious ! ’ at sight of the rich gold at Canbelego,
assisting at the final drilling of the new artesian
bore (2270 ft.) at Coonamble, shearing sheep (2|d.
a sheep) at Sunnyridge, and I’m making a gieat
collection of sketches of Western types.” For the
rest the returned warbler has been guest of honor
at many stations, engaged in snowballing at Man-
durama, celebrated the somethingth anniversary
of her marriage with a banquet sprung on her by
her company, and gave successful concerts all the
way. These songsters do seem to crowd their'
hours with play and labor.
The Hat, like the eternal feminine with which
it is associated, is of perennial interest. A girl
who wishes she had ten heads so that she could wear
ten hats, all masterpieces, tells me she has been
to “The Belle of New York,” and that the only
interesting circumstances in the production are
the hats of some of the principals. Miss
Lottie Sargent’s potais (potai is Maori for hat)
are (1) a violet one—a mass of lilac, with
pink, mauve and cream roses, and a spray of
mauve wheat; (2) a white straw with cream net
on the crown and two big, extra-crushed pink
roses and a blue bow. Miss Stirling, the Cora
Angelique of “ The Belle,” has a large white bat
with white feathers, gold tissue ribbon and a
frisky gold tassel. A pot-shaped black hat with
three ostrich plumes and a wide, flat bow of old
rose and mole velvet also falls to Miss Stirling’s
share.
At a recent big first night, five ex-actresses who
came here as strangers, and presently annexed
affluent Australians in matrimony, were seated in
a front row of the stalls. To the local girl they
were terrible examples of the danger of harboring
the attractive alien.
Miss Nora Long, the Maoriland soprano men-
tioned Jin my last letter as having taken a two
years’ engagement as first singer at the Grand
Opera House at Nice, will make her debut there as
Juliet next month. She has taken the name
of D’Argiel. So when you read of the triumphs
of Nora D’Argiel you’ll know her for the tall thin
girl who used to lift her high soprano in Sydney.
Report says she has developed as well physically
as songfully. She will sing three nights and a
matinee weekly. Her operatic work leaves her
free to accept private engagements, but she has
had to sign a contract not to sing under 20
guineas ah engagement. For the rest, her frocks
are dreams and their prices are nightmares.
I threw up my lucky ha’penny to decide whether
I should go to Melbourne to see Henley on the
Yarra or stop for the “Julius Caesar ” recital at
the Town Hall. The coin, after rising to a height
of 400 ft., fell for Shakspeare, and now, after the
recital, I am infinitely obliged to it. I did not
previously know that the Romans shook arms,
not hands. With a fine, wide, generous move-
ment of the right fore limb the meeting
senators grasp each other between the elbow
and the wrist (the arm is, of course, bare under
the toga), give a perceptible shake and release the
clasp slowly, almost reluctantly. The weediest
man amongst the senators looked majestic in his
scarlet-bordered cream toga. One thought the
garment white until Miss Lily Brayton, as
Portia Brutus, appeared in her snow-white nightie
and proved the contrast. The modernity, or rather
sameness, of human nature, too, is scally waggishly
brought home to us when the Roman populace,
the “rude mechanicals,” cheek the senators. The
cobbler barracks the clean-shaven cream and
crimson soldier-lawmakers just as “Ginger ” from
Leichhardt or Redfern might guy Senator Walker
and ask him where he got that hat. And the
Roman senator gets angry very much after the
manner of a dignified Australian senator, only to
the.former the barracker is “Thou saucy varlet.”
Mark Antony is the champion Yes-No of his-
tory. That speech of his over the imperial-purpled
dead body of Caesar proves it.
What singular forethought on Oscar Asche’s
part to include so many Roman-nosed men ! I
never saw such a collection of aristocratic
Caesarian noses, conks or beaks. It was re-
marked on the first night of the Asehe co.’s
appearance here, but the aquilines show up
against the blue-black velvet of the forum
with greater significance in the Roman play.
Arid they “go well” with the ennobling
toga. It may be that Asche selected so many
eagle beaks as contrasts to his own short, strong,
bull-dog organ. In spite of that and with his
locomotive-fender jaw he, as Brutus, really looked
what Antony called him—the noblest Roman of
them all. And his tread was planet-shaking.
The co, isthereby invited to do it again.
Mosman, that water-roundaged suburb that
lies opposite Sydney, has aspirations. The
chiefest (Sbaksperian term) of these is to play
the part of Buda to the Pesth of Sydney. The day
is not far distant when Dr. Arthur, its politi-
cal representative (provided he still holds that
qualification), will arise in Council to move that
the twin cities’ names be connected by a hyphen,
or a bridge—Mosman-Sydnev. Half, the Sydney-
siders who get married go to live at Mosman
(worse luck !). Every place where, five years ago,
flourished the flannel flower is now disfigured with
the jerry-builder’s red cottages; story-writers are
laying the scenes of their tales at Mosman, and
every night in the week there’s a something of Itn*
portance on at its town hall—either a hall, or bazaar
or an operatic performance. Last Saturday this
ambitious suburb held its inaugural flower and
Vegetable show. All the produce came from the
gardens of local residents, There was a cabbage
that Would have brought tears of envy to the eyes
of a vegetable John. There was also a flower
hat that Flora herself might wear when
a-visiting amongst the other goddesses. The
crown was fashioned of white carnations,
and the remainder of the surface was
mostly pale yellow rosebuds. A cluster of
pale pink buds nodded at the left side, but the
sweetest, cutest part of the fragrant confection
was the under-side of the brim. Ivy leaves, sewn
■overlapping-wise, it was. And only the possible
danger of intrusive acquaintances corning up to
smell my hat prevents me making and wearing
its like. Think of being able to wear a fresh hat
every morning—seven hats a week.
You can’t be born respectably in Sydney
unless you are born in the Herald at half a
crown. To get married in Sydney without a
two-and-sixpenny hand in the game being taken
bv the Herald is merely to live without benefit
of clergy ; and to die in Sydney without dying in
the Herald is practically to remain unburied.
No merely ordinary citizen can reasonably hope
to have a respectable funeral unless it’s adver-
tised in the Herald about eight times. On two
succeeding days recently I saw in the Fairfax
paper 16 advts. relating to one funeral and 14
referring to another. For a long while the
management apparently put its foot down on
matrimonial advts., but they are again coming
along beautifully. Saturday before last two
young ladies advertised for “ two elderly gents, of
means, view mat.” On the other hand, the Tele-
graph seems to specialise a good deal in houses,
wooden and qtherwise. Thus, you advertise your
wooden house in the Telegraph as a matter of
course, and your wooden overcoat in the other
paper.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

ON KAMBALA HEIGHTS.
The most select and exclusive residential suburb in Sydney is Bellevue
Hill. And until now building allotments there have been practically unobtain-
able. But all that is to be changed. On NOVEMBER 13 we will offer at auction
some hundred home sites on KAMBALA HEIGHTS ESTATE— a picked
spot in Bellevue Hill district.
This choice property fronts Bellevue Road between Double Bay and
Bellevue Hill. It is close to “ Cranbrook,” the residence of the State Governor,
and many other of Sydney’s finest homes. The estate is high up above the city
and enjoys glorious views. From some parts are to be had delightful glimpses
of the harbor.
The Last Chance.
This auction offers the last chance to secure a home site in Sydney’s best
suburb. The number of allotments is limited—only a few can make their homes
there. Will we send you a plan ?
Lots on this estate will be splendid investments. No matter where you
live, you can have one. Send for a plan, select the lot you want (or let us
select you a good one), and instruct us to buy it for you at auction.
Terms are easy —5 per cent, deposit, balance in 60 equal monthly
payments, at 5 per cent, interest.

AS A WOMAN TREATETH HER COMPLEXION, SO IT IS
THE ADVICE OF A WELL-KNOWN STAGE BEADTY.
THIS is the story of a girl from Bendigo. She had
ambition, talent and beauty. Wider horizons
lured her on. It seems only a very short while
since she joined London’s multitudinous ranks
of the aspiring, but already this statuesque Bendigo
girl, Miss Alice Crawford, thanks to her charming per-
sonality, which makes her a splendid subject for the
artist’s fancy, has been winning increasing fame with
each passing theatrical season. As by her admirable
and successful acting she has gained plaudits and
undeniable appreciation, so she now bids fair to gain
the thanks and gratitude of many Englishwomen for
having pointed out to them the infallible means for the
preservation of Complexion-Beauty. In a letter repro-
duced on this page, she says of Valaze Skin-Food and
Beautifier :
“ Valaze has done my skin such a lot of good. No
one knoivs better than yourself how much an actress’s
complexion must sufter through the exacting require-
ments of her calling. Your Skin-food, Valaze, does
precisely what the skin requires ; it preserves its tone,
whiteness and clearness as no other preparation has
done.”
Miss Crawford then continues :
“ When I have said that I have only vaguely hinted
at the true pleasure, satisfaction and benefit which I
derive from it. All actresses should use it daily.”
These words, coming from an actress blessed with
such charm as is Miss Crawford’s happy possession, are
well worth pondering, because there are none quicker
to realise than stage-folk that while talent is a great
thing, mental equipment and true artistic Came iff
are most valuable possessions, physical ‘nkth'active'ness'
frequently finds them wanting when weighed with them
in the balance. .
To command success you must look it.
Miss Crawford’s letter is of further interest, because
it illustrates so well.the satisfaction one feels when one
has obtained the best in any one thing.
Valaze ACTS on the skin, seeking out the deepest
layers, the deepest tissues, the deepest cells, and there
works like Nature works, thoroughly and surely, for
skin health, purity, color, freshness, for skin beauty in
every sense.
The skin that feeds on Valaze can bear the brunt of
all climate condifiohs, of all moods of weather, of heat
and of cold, of atmosphere dry and damp.
The Valaze? complexion knows no speck or freckle,
as the Valaze skin knows no line or crowsfoot.
Valaze-nourished tissues are never depressed. A
Valaze face never looks tired. Valaze is, indeed, the
apex in complexion requisites.

The price of Valaze is 3s. 6d., 6s. 6d. a jar, 6d.
postage.
My book, “Beauty in the Making,” which forms
the most scientific discussion on the subject of the care
and treatment of the complexion, can be obtained free
on application, enclosing threepence for postage. It
furnishes details of other complexion preparations
which I have brought with me from my home-town,
Vienna, and from Russia. These are all intended for
home treatment, and serve for every imaginable re-
quirement of the complexion.
These specialties have been tested under every clime.
I have studied their action on the skin in the tropical
heat of Australasia and India, in the moderate zones of
Central Europe, in the cold latitudes of Canada and St.
Petersburg, and under the uncertain and variable con-
ditions of England.
The names and prices of some of them are :

The MOST DESIRABLE TYRE
Reliability, durability and efficiency may be considered
as going hand in hand as the first considerations in the
manufacture of the most desirable Motor Tyres—and
in no tyres will this trinity of features be found more
pronounced than in the ever-popular Continental variety.
The Continental Co.’s interpretation of the word
“reliability” is “ability to get there,” and not only
to get there, but to get there well; to be certain at the
commencement of a journey that the end is in sight.
CONTINENTAL TYRES
MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, October 25, 1909.
My Dear Yarrik,—
Henley is growing into a wonderful affair. It
develops characteristics individual enough to give
distinction to an unborrowed name, despite the
wild endeavors of the hawty committee in the
direction of purely English lines, don’t you
know. Saturday was a brilliant blue day,
and the crowds —seething, good-tempered, holi-
day-making crowds—drifted in a peculiar free
atmosphere of their own—not only in the
extensive, expensive enclosures, but' over the
bridge and round the avenues and into every nook
and cranny for quite a mile in many directions.
The lawns along the banks at the back of the
river—if a river has a back—and the shrubby
grass plots of Alexandra Avenue that curves
beyond, made holiday places for holiday-frocked
people. Motors by the hundred had much ado to
toot and honk their way to the “Reserve En-
trance.” The houseboats have been pushed
farther still down stream until they touch
Blunder’s Ferry—a pretty spot on the much-
maligned Yarra, at the foot of the Botanical
Gardens. The two-and one-shilling entrance fees
charged at the enclosure go to the improvement
of the Yarra banks, as do the rents charged for
houseboat room. There is little to mock at now
in the Yarra banks, once so bare and ugly.
Yet Henley has only existed for five
seasons. Such a change, niv countrymen !
Australian Henley No. 1 showed a few excited
races, a goodly crowd, and a smattering of mangy
houseboats (i.e., coal barges half disguised with
flowers and “art” muslin) huddled in a bend of
the river. Now the houseboats are a blaze of
competitive splendor. Even little dinky rowing
boats are deliberate schemes of color, and there
are more motor launches than Melbourne thought
of a year or two back. Nowadays, too, the crowd
on the banks makes it a Cup day. Every feminine,
whether she thinks in hundreds of pounds or in
shillings, studies out a Henley dress and hat.
Everything was so much larger than even last
year’s largeness that tile committee got hot and
red-faced and lost its temper before the show got
going. Even when the Excellencies were arriving
at their own particular dog-box things were in
such a fluster that the Union Jack got hoisted
wrong side up, and the poor Lion, standing on
his head, kicked indignantly in the north wind
until he was turned right side up. There were
some special and altogether swagger lunches on
the houseboats, engineered for the Very Few.
The gaud-Mayor was so pleased at his re-election
that his boat flamed in a spree-like red—or
perhaps the scarlet betokened his military rank.
Anyhow it was a hideous battle-cry of color in the
heat of the day, but it blazed with good red
electric effect in the night. The Commercial
Travellers were also stridently red. The Aus-
tralian Club lay low in pale modest blue,
with a shady bungalow roof that matched
the sky. The Alexandra Puss Club waved glory
green pennants, but the boat was unhappy under
its bilious greenery-yallery paint. The Athenaeum
Club had, as usual, the largest boat—a big, solid
Great Eastern, with winch decks turned into
ferny corners, and its beaming solid comfortable
hosts gave all the pretty girls the Henley of their
lives. Then this club was thoughtful enough to
spread crimson felt for the showing up of dainty
frocks and shoes. Besides companion ladders
fore and aft, there were gangways to port and
starboard—one to the bank, t’other to the river—
and the clubbers had engaged motor launches so
that they could show their guests the rest of the
Yarra. Incidentally, the master-man of the
launches cursed deeply and softly and continuously
as he came backwards and forwards among the
moths and butterflies of small craft that hung to
the moorings. This club had much of the fat of
the entertaining. Masked singers came aboard
and warbled to the accompaniment of an
orchestra. The pretty Winter Garden girls en-
gineered the pink ice and the strawberry.
The slick white Gadfly—the swiftest motor-
launch here—tabooed womankind and ran only
flannelled masculine creatures. A Peter Pan set
of pirates flung skull and crossbones to the wind.
Theatrical folk were restricted to day-time
parade. Melba motored down early and left very
early to be rested for her concert. In spite of the
Henley crush there were still people left in Mel-
bourne, and they rushed, in the heat and dust of
the afternoon, to secure front seats that night. A
huge multitude stood outside the Exhibition for
hours, each with a heated two-and-sixpence held
in a feverish hand. Mrs. Bland Holt ap-
peared ac the King’s on Saturday night. She
took her Henley (with Blan’olt himself) at
lunch-time on the Athenaeum Club craft. Dainty
Fan Dango, in white with a juvenile blue sash,
put in the afternoon there. Pantomime Boy
Meredro, in the cutest thing in linen, showed her
dimples all day.
The Savage Club stands frigidly aloof from the
line of boats. It has its own special bit of lawn
and puts up its canvas mia-mia barricaded and
bristling with awful weapons and gruesome
scalps.
To the veriest few is Henley a race. An occa-
sional shouting and a glimpse of fleeting colored
rowing togs distract the girl on the bank for an
odd moment or so. But the oarsmen themselves
say this year was a racing year. The rule of the
river was so stringent that they even had room to
row. Some artistic gazers murmured that things
were more picturesque in the days when any sort
of old craft rambled any old where on the course.
Until the new rule was made as one of the
statutes of the Medes and Persians, oarsmen
bucked at training. Aforetime the racers were
half their time swimming out of the river, drag-
ing their boats behind them and cursing because
they had to dock for repairs, while lunatics in a
flower barge, who knew no more of rowing than
they did of the Boots of Perdition, guffawed and
giggled at their mishap. This time there was a
straight course, good racing and not one upset.
From this time on there is likely to be a zest in
the training—a prospect of a good strong pull and
fairplay.
A beautiful sight at Henley was blase Guy
Madden, girl-less, reposing like Nero in the
cushioned bows of a boat and looking gloomily on
the fields of femininity; while Willy Kelly
perched like a sparrow on the stern, showing a
glimpse of the power and the glory of his scarlet
socks. Between the two beauties was one fat
man doing all the rowing and perspiring like
Hotness.
To-day, in Collins-street, I saw a motor-car
chock-a-block with chimney-pot hats—new shining
tunnel-hats with men under them. It was an open
motor, and the frock-coated men sat on each other,
so great was the company of these shining
angelic beings. They were Governors, swarming
into the city even as the bookie swarms—for the
races. It was a beautiful sight. Some of the
company may have been gentlemen helps, but
their hats shone with even more radiance than the
Governors’. Really, the hat of an aide-de-camp is
as the sun in its zenith, or like broken bottles in a
summer noon. In fact, were it not a busy day,
poetry would well up at the very thought of these
heavenly tiles. An A.D.C.’s hat is his badge
—more so even than blue or purple lapels.
He is never without it. A mere Gover-
nor may occasionally relapse into a squashy
lid when there are not too many people looking,
but the aide — never. There is a belief suddenly
borne in on me that the crush hat—that
swaggah opera concern—originated as a nightcap
for gentlemen helps. The poor things were not
allowed to sleep without their tall hats in case of
fire or earthquake. It would not be the thing
for the Governor to escape unless “accompanied
by Secretary Inkpot or Captain Horsemar.ne.”
Probably they used their second-best hats for
bedtime, and one night, in the sleeplessness of
horrible discomfort, a brilliant A.D.C. evolved the
happy soup-plate crush hat. Ever after aides
slept in opera hats, except (by law) on moonlight
nights because then the dull shineless surface
would be apparent. Dear Yarrie, do you ever
contemplate the ghastly horror of living for ever in
a high hat? When the Governor stalks abroad,
the earliest up must be the A.D.C., to precede
his Excellent steps. That same aide has
sat up far into the night, poring over
time-tables and mind-racking figures. Then
during the day if hitches occur (horrible animals,
hitches), the aide must pacify the brutes. Also
he has to guide the troubled footsteps of the
besieged Gov. on to the next oyster to be opened.
He has to be at his elbow with his speech notes.
What a catastrophe if the Governor began to
speech his Norman Lindsay show talk to the
Neglected Children’s Aid Society, or his remarks
for the Epileptics to the Alexandra Club.
Poor aides !—first up, last to bed, working all
the time, ever stepping lightly upon eggs, and
trying not to break the bruised reed of conversa-
tion. Here and now I start a society for the Aid
of Aides.
The Caf4 Chantant in aid of the Melbourne
District Nursing Society that gathered at St.
Kilda Town Hall on Thursday last, might have
been a select Government House shivoo for the
multitudinousness of the Somebodies who all
rolled up at 2s. a head. Governors and people
were scattered about like ordinary mortals. The
quiet little Miss Bosanquet palled all the evening
with Miss Stuart-Monteith, the red-haired visitor
at Stonington. The Wrixon dame and the
Madden throng and the gaud Mayoress all bol-
stered up the seats nearest to the Excellent Lady.
Railway Tait and Mrs. Tait beamed on the
tableaux which held their Winnie-girl—a very
pretty picture, by the way. Mrs. Justice Isaacs
and Mrs. Tatham, who had worked like niggers to
secretary the show, gazed overall with the anxious
eyes of generals. Marjorie Isaacs led a Gotten-
berg crowd in Berlin-on-the-Spree, and blushed
every time she tossed her trousered leg over a
cane. The other trousered, blue-jumpered crew
were the two Robb girls, Miss Atherton, a
McEvoy maiden and Nancy Grice. “ Trally ”
Foster, that beauteous singing man, duetted with
the Emmerton daughter in a “Girl Behind the
Counter ” encounter. The Maddens made things
hum, and put a finger into ’most everything.
Ruby of the Dimples posed in a lover-like picture
with Keith Anthony, who was able to give his
profile its due in the limelight. Ptg 0 y Hammond.-
Clegg and Miss de Grut had practised for weeks
at the Greenaway girl dance, with Tom Payne of
the big head coaching them. They were quite
pleased with the result—as well as the
practice. Another Miss Ham-and-Clegg posed
as a pretty Merry Widow (tableau), with Norman
Hodges bending unhappily to the picture, as if
Danilo were suddenly attacked with colic,
Beatrice Madden sang a sweet little song while
the light was resting. In one living picture
Murie Huon hung rather large nude feet out of a
moon hammock and turned a pretty profile to the
house. Mrs. Harry Cornish, in a certain amount of
raiment, sat on a bough in the limelight. They had
set husband Henry at the door taking tickets, so
he couldn’t rush the platform with a glare
and an overcoat. The pretty O’Hara colleens and
Winnie Tait and half a hundred other damsels
sold the expensive strawberry and the sly-faced
ice and every other marketable thing they could
carry on ribboned trays. An occasional make-
believe maid tilted her coffee tray over unhappy
Chantant listeners under stress of ardent admira-
tion from johnnies struggling in the crowd. Then
she pouted because people who got their cafe on
the neck objected to pay half a crown for the
honor.
The Naval and Military Club is gathering
ammunition and making every preparation for a
ball in the Town Hall that is to knock things sky-
high. Fireworks and a burning kerosene stove
would be tame to the blaze of this gorgeous ’op
which is to prance on Friday—the night before
Derby day.
The Carmichaels invite a rehearsal of race
frocks—otherwise a garden party—on Monday,
November 1, at Stonington, where is a beautiful
garden, all roses and terraced lawns and faint
fragrances.
Australians are a big-breathing crowd—they
like space. Girls have just as free-handed a way
of lumping luggage, grabbing a suit-case and
dashing abroad as have the men. A flutter of a
handkerchief, and a girl is-off for a year’s spin
round the globe. The younger of the Holme
Rennie girls is back after tossing about in
a White Star liner on the homeward track
of the ill-fated Waratah. The weather was curious
and the sea surging and angry. Several times the
crowd was battened down. One lady carefully
dressed herself for the expected drowning, and
remarked that she would like to “meet her
Maker in white.” She said it in the same solemn
voice that she might use in discussing her Cup
dress. Another dame put on her oldest petticoat
in order to save her good silk one. Are men
built like that, I wonder? Is there any record of
a male having put on his best frock-coat and his
tallest hat as the ship went down ?
Gold-gilt Sternberg, of Bendigo and of the
’Ouse, gave his one ewe lamb away in marriage
last week to Mr. Cohen, who is also gilt-edged.
Miss Sternberg was one of the smartest of the
smart Bendigo maidens who trip to this town
from the golden city. She has also tripped over
plenty of the stepping-stones of the globe,
and has not long returned from the land
of the Rising- Sun, where the Monkey shines
in the East. The wedding was at the Grand Hotel,
Melbourne, and was quite a small family affair
of a double dozen guests. Dorothy Sternberg, a
young cousin, came to hold the gloves and other
necessary superfluities of the bride while the ring
was rung. Everybody partied at Her Majesty’s
in the evening for “The Catch of the Season,”
and returned for an extra special supper in the
private supper-room of the Grand. The hilarious
noise of departing motors and fleeing guests died
away at 3 a. rn.
The Collins-street Smiths —£.£., Mrs. £.£. and
Miss £ouise—landed back at the grey ancestral
humpy last week after a long and elaborate earth-
journey with a few sea trips between. The Parisian
dresses that will appear in Race week are just
jumping from their oilcloth travelling boxes in
excitement.
Picture shows are becoming a plague—like
rabbits and grasshoppers and starlings. People
are sending invitations from The Studio at Otway
Forest (turn to the south, and it’s the last gum tree
on the left), or planted in One Tree Gully. These
places are comparatively get-at-able. Bu t one genius
has the gall to invite people to Orrong-road,
Malvern—the place where the new electric tram
does not go, and which still boasts ’buses and
other prehistoric animals in its wilds.
From “ Rosna,” of Adelaide town :
It was a white-satined Excellency, with diamond tiara
surmounting her silvei’-grey locks, who beckoned the
world in and shook it by the hand at the Residence, the
other evening. The ostensible purpose of the revel was
to be kind to the Commerce delegates ; and, though they
had been rushed about the harbor all day, they bore up
man- and womanfully. The one available vice-regal
daughter was also white-and-silver clad ; and Mrs. Albion
Tolley, an inveterate Parisian frocker, wore a white-satin
Directoire robe. The Way dame wore pale-pink satin and
rose point; Mrs. Mayfield was in black sequined chiffon
and silk; and Mrs. Attorney-General Mitchell in silver
tissue with a black lace over-dress.
On Saturday, with Jupiter Pumphandle toiling away
again for all he was worth, Kensington Gardens were
opened by the Governor. We are proud of our gardens
in Adelaide, and this one is going to make us prouder. It
consists of 40 acres of what is known as Pile’s Paddock, a
picturesque patch sprinkled with great old gums, cud-
dled together at the foot of the hills. It has been a
favorite picnic ground for over 40 years, and now it has
been handed to the Tramways Trust to be made
into a public park, with an electric car service
running into the middle of it. The authorities
had an idea of making a kind of garden (or paddock)
party of the function ; but, as heretofore mentioned,
Jupiter Tankhandle had other plans. The flags which
marked the park boundaries drooped like depressed wash-
ing in the rain ; the people followed suit; and the only
solace was a marquee which protected the afternoon tea
and the gilded bun. And while the rain rained his Ex.
planted a tree and bade the crowd go home and do
likewise.
The Melbourne express the other day deposited here
14 trunks of frocks and sweet Marie Narelle. But she was
also a fleet Marie, and rushed on to Westralia the same
day, after making promise to burst into an Adelaide
season in a month or two.
Those entertaining Frenchwomen, Mrs. Mouchette and
Mrs. Lion, are having an exhibition of pictures, minia-
tures, old tapestries, engravings and china, at their
studio this week, and their Excellencies and society gene-
rally are flocking along to admire and buy. The studio is
an artistic oasis on the top of Steamship Build-
ings, managed by charming and interesting dames
who have a dovecote up in the middle of the hills, with a
beauteous garden where all the birds of the air are taught
to regard themselves as landed proprietors and have
crumbs distributed among them three times a day. The
studio in town is to be given up at the end of this year,
and Mrs. Mouchette and her sister are going to stay in
the garden to see that their bird friends are polite at their
meals and don’t eat their peas with the gridiron.
A special train took his Excellence and daughter Alicia
to Mount Gambier last week. The other twin, whose
name is Beatrice (they were called A and B instead of
M and N), has been wearing her prettiest chiffons and
smiles for the benefit of the eastern States. But on
Tuesday she came back to us to help her mother cope
with the rush of rose shows, garden revels and other
floricultural items.
Rose shows were littered all over last week. On Tues-
day one happened in the Unley Town Hall, where her
Ex. praised its complexion, and asked it to consider itself
opened. On Wednesday there was another at Norwood,
which defied the world to prove that it wasn’t the finest
rose-show on earth. Lady Bosanquet made more sooth-
ing remarks here ; the available daughter had gone over
in the morning to decide sundry weighty matters about
prize dinner-tables and bouquets. But that didn’t end
it. On Thursday the Adelaide roses met in the Town
Hall. Her Excellence this time was helped by the Way
dame to bear the responsibility of judging. And the
matter didn’t finish even there. On Saturday St. Peters
caught the prevailing fever, and showed off at the local
hall all the afternoon and evening.
All this floriculture reminds me that an Adelaide scribe,
Mr. J. M. Black, has just published a book on “The
Naturalised Flora of South Australia.” He is a front
lamp of the Field Naturalists, Botanical Society, and all
the other bodies that are privileged to call the Gompho-
carpus arborescentt, and the Barnyardius marigoldium
by their unchristian names. And he has illustrated the
book with 300 drawings. Mr. Black mixes botany and
languages, but is a delightful man nevertheless. His
sister is the widow of the late d’Oyley Carte.
The Commerce delegates, or rather their three avail-
able wives—Mrs. Ayrton, Mrs. Ives and Mrs. Barrow —
conspired together last week at the South Australian.
The result' was an impromptu reception to those people
who have been showing them the country with so much
vigor and vim. They stuck 40,000 roses in the hair of the
reception room, ordered in ices, and Adelaide sent some
of its best frocks to complete the picture. Mrs. Barrow
had some Limerick lace' on a navy frock for which I
would have become a burglar but for the sake of
the family. Mrs. Ives wore a pretty garment of a
pineapple tint, and Mrs. Ayrton a bright blue
shimmer)' frock and rose-wreathed hat. There were
two Mueeke girls, one nut-brown and the other grey-
clad. Mrs. Arnold Davey, in Directoire embroidered
chiffons and blue-plumed hat, the George Deans’ daugh-
ter in navy shantung and with a big black hat on her fair
locks, the G. F. Clelands, John Hills, Prossers and Chas.
Goodes all assisted in the distribution of tea and compli-
ments.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.
Interesting to know how many of the finest
complexions that will brave the elements and
charm the eye at Flemington, this Cup time, owe
a touch of supernatural charm to Valaze. The
Valaze establishment in Melbourne is said to be
the Mecca of all smart women from the country
who come up for the races. That it is the favorite
place of worship for local feminine loveliness
seems to go without saying. Everybody
who is anybody must be either “finished off”
at the temple of toilet mysteries itself or
supplied with the essentials for home treat-
ment. The fact is admitted, but of direct
evidence in support thereof there is nothing
to swear by. Valaze never gives itself away, so
to speak. What complexion could have been
more irreproachable than that of Helena Rubin-
stein (now Mrs. Titus), who introduced the “skin-
food” to Australia and is at present presiding over
her Valaze Salon in London ? The same note of
interrogation applies to the serenely pale face of
Cesca Rubinstein, her sister, manageress of the
rooms in Melbourne. One looks for signs of
make-up in the beauty of Miss Cesca, but finds no
suggestion of art. Her assistants —the sweet girl
graduates— are equally innocent of embellish-
ment, as far as the inquiring eye can discover.
Yet it is assumed to be there. The sceptic who
makes these remarks has come to the conclusion
that in the matter of Valaze use is second nature.
Bob Hodge, the new member for Burnett,
used to be member for Rosewood. Five years ago
he was defeated, went to the Kilkivan district,
became a publican, sold out lately for £2OOO and
£6 a week for 10 years, contested the seat and
won. Go into a pub, young man—but go behind
the bar.

There is only this to tell people who are pale,
weak and bloodless. You are pale and weak
because you haven’t enough blood, and you won’t
he better until your blood supply is increased.
You shouldn’t lose time in increasing your blood
snpplv, for people who neglect anaemia are often
the ones who slip into a decline. Further when
you have increased your blood supply you can
reasonably expect to have a good color, to have
lost that tired, weary, breathless feeling, to have
a. good appetite and to get good nourishment from
your food. Probably you know all this.
Now to get more hlood. If you have ever heard
of anything that will make new blood and bring
buoyant happy health quicker than Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills, take it. We have never ; we even
haven’t heard of anything half so good. They are
scientifically compounded for the very purpose,
and how well they do their work the hundreds of
cures of anaemia in Australia bear out. The
following case is a fair sample of what they do.
“ I was always inclined to delicacy as a young
girl, and after marriage I became anaemic,” said
Mrs. Arthur Whitmore, 70 Sewell Street, East
Fremantle. “ I couldn’t get my strength up after
my little boy was born. My appetite was very
poor and I dare not take meat. I lived mostly on
milk foods. I lost all rnv color. My face got
sallow. I only fancied pickles, lemons and vine-
gar; as to raw rice, I’d eat handfuls of it. It was
no use saying these things were bad for me ; I
fairly craved for them. I fell away in flesh and
got pitiably thin. My feet and hands were
always cold, and if I stood long on my feet my
ankles would swell up. Often I’d get up in the
morning and feel so heavy and dizzy that I knew
a violent, splitting headache was coming on
and would last all day, so I would have to go and
lie down, not bearing anyone to speak or make a
noise. I never felt well, especially in the fore part
of the day. I’d wonder how I was going to get
through my housework. It was as much as I could
do sometimes to lift a broom. My limbs seemed
to have no strength in them. It was as if they
were bending under me. I had so little strength
that I’d often faint straight off and go down flat.
I went to the doctor and he said that I was run
down. I took some bottles of medicine, but got
no relief. I was ailing about eight months when
I started Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. From the
first rny health began to return. I gradually lost
the craving for vinegar and starch, and regained
the desire for wholesome food, and soon I was
eating quite heartily. I felt my blood getting
much richer and the color returned to my face.
The difference two boxes made was wonderful.
The price is 2s. 9d. per box, (> boxes 15s. 3d.,
and if you have trouble in getting them, send a
postal note for the amount to the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co. of Australasia, Ltd., Sydney, and
they will be sent post free by return mail.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
SPITE.
Don’t think that your tax will burst up the big estates;
the chances are that once the tax is imposed the owners
will hang on to their land tighter than ever, if only to
spite those who put on the ta x.—Carlisle in his speech
against the proposed Vic. land-tax.
The most gigantic trust or combine ever
heard of is said to be forming in the U.S., the idea
being to secure a monopoly of water-power over
the whole Uncle Sam territory. The Foreign-
trader always says that trusts are caused wholly
by Protection, so presumably his remedy in this
case will be to take the duty off water and import
it freely in kegs.
Even the potato is crying aloud for unifica-
tion and reconstruction. Though the different
State Ministers lately came to ap agreement
regarding Irish blight and the transportation of
spuds from suspected districts in other States, the
compact has not been kept, for Ma State sjf ill refuses
admission to clean potatoes from her neighbors,
though lots of her own tubers are blighted. Now
Victoria threatens to retaliate by prohibiting the
importation of tomatoes from Ma if they come
from blighted districts—and it is pretty certain
that they mostly will come from blighted districts.
So long as affected districts are quarantined, it is
difficult to see what a State boundary has to do
with the matter. It was to do away with these
absurd discriminations that Australia federated ;
but a swarm of State insects are busy trying to
block progress in that direction, and—worse still
—Australia lets them do it.
Sydney Telegraph barks with ferocity at
the revived N. : .W. Freetrade League. Itshould,
if it had any r ipect for principle, be beside the
League, or somewhere near, taking its share of
the eggs of public derision ; but that’s not its
way. Instead it calls the League bogus, a sham,
a soi-disant Freetrade body, and all the other
offensive names which perverts sling at their old
comrades. N.S.W. has gone ahead so vigorously
under Protection that the Freetrade banner
becomes very heavy to carry these days—it feels
as if it had a corpse on the top of the staff, or
something like that—and only a very hardened
enthusiast would drag the discredited old luggage
around.
Lands Minister McKenzie (Vic.) sent some
of his constituents home, the other day, feeling as
though iced frogs were skating down their spines.
Said McKenzie:—
He had noticed that municipal councils were “ instruct-
ing-” their members to vote against the Bill. But many
of the councillors were large landholders, and if his
Government were defeated over this matter it would go
out. Then they would probably get a Labor Government
with a far more drastic measure. Country land-holder
and city property-owner were both squealing ; but land
taxation had to come no matter who was in power.
The biggest development that British in-
dustries have experienced of late has arisen through
high-handed Protection. A little over a year ago
an Act was passed which set forth that every
foreigner who patented a new invention in the
United Kingdom must manufacture the patented
article in the United Kingdom to meet the local
demand, or else have his patent revoked. So,
under compulsion, 24 great new industries have
been established in England alone during a single
year. It is good business, but it is mighty bad
Freetrade.
“S.R.” discourses oil guns :
After accepting a tender for an up-to-date small-arms
plant, the Australian Defence Department has made what
looks like a bad mistake : it is having the machinery
adapted to the Enfield standard ; and the Enfield is not
the best rifle to be had these days. There is no comfort
in the statement that the new weapons will be sounder
and better finished than those at present supplied. The
type does not compare favorably with most of the well-
known makes. The Mark 111. Lee-Enfield short rifle—
the baptismal name of the new slayer—is not even equal
to the old pattern Lee-Enfield. For long range shooting,
the rifleman has no hesitation in choosing the older gun.
After the last meeting at Bisley the British War Office
received an awful roasting over the Mark 111., and the
only retort it could make was to point to the two lonely
successes scored by the gun. Unfortunately for the
W. 0., both the wins were scored over short distances, so
they weren’t much support to the argument. It was also
alleged at the time against the Mark 111. that the breech
mechanism was too weak for a military weapon, and a
long controversy in the papers dug up the interesting
fact that the cordite charge for the regulation ball car-
tridge had been reduced. Before that cat rushed out of
the official sack, a lot of shooters had been complaining
of inferior ammunition. The Canadian Ross gun, at the
same meeting, was praised by an enormous proportion of
the shots present. The standardisation of weapons isn’t
going to be of much use to the Australian forces if
Britain is to set an inferior standard. It would be a
darned sight better for this country to invent something
for itself, like the gentle Kanuck, or even to slavishly
imitate one of the really first-class shooting irons. I
don’t see any advantage in standing shoulder to shoulder
with Ma, if Ma is going to totter along in the rear of the
procession.
Any old rags ? Britain, the boss shoddy
shop of the world, is a steady buyer. In the
last three years Australia has sent J. Bull £51,485
worth of its cast-off socks and pensioned under-
clothes and other such trifles. It sends Bull 20
times more rags than it does rum ; in fact, in most
years it sends him more rags than dried fruit, of
which this country ough t to be producing thousands
of tons; and for every £1 he pays Australia for
jams and jellies the rag bill is just over £5O.
But an even more amazing thing is that,
while J. Bull is a steady importer of Aus-
tralia’s old clo’es, Australia now and again
imports a few sacks full from Bull—pro-
bably the same old clo’es, worn a few
weeks in a London slum, and then stuffed
into another bag and sent back this way
again. Still, compared with the rags that
Australia sends father Bull, Australia’s im-
ports are very small, never amounting to more
than a few hundred pounds’ worth a year.
Almost everywhere the miner is the re-
liable democrat. In the Queensland elections
every gold-mining district voted Labor Straight-
Charters Towers, Gympie, Mount Morgan, Croy-
don, Clermont, Thornborough and Georgetown.
Andy Dawson, Queensland's first Labor Premier,
was a goldfields man. Earle, Tasmania’s first
Labor Premier, is a miner. Jack Verran, S.
Australia’s Labor party leader, represents the
Moonta men. Bach, of Westralia, represents
Brown Hill, another mining camp. And still, in
spite of all that and a great deal more, the first
line of this par must have its “almost ” ; for the
Lithgow (N.S.W.) coalfield for years sent Joe
Cook to the House, and is now represented by the
Foreign-trader Bowden, apparently on the prin-
eiple that the less coal the Australian miner has to
supply to Australian factories the better.
Wilks, M.H.R., of Dailey (N.S.W.), used
to claim that he was an enthusiastic Foreign-
trader and Shoddyist, and on the strength of his
more-or-less gospel oath to that effect got the im-
porters’ vote at last election. But Balmain,
which is part of Wilks’ electorate, is the
home of certain industries, and when it came
to applying the glorious doctrine of Cobden
to those particular industries Wilks heaved the
glorious doctrine into Darling Harbor, and
reached out for all the Protection he could get.
Naturally the Cobdenites said that sort of thing
wasn’t Freetrade, and now the Sydney Freetrade
League proposes to put up a candidate against
Freetectionist Wilks. So between Laborist
Howe, who pushed him hard at last election and
has been on his trail ever since, and the Freetrade
League’s candidate, and a possible selected
“ Liberal ” candidate (because Wilks hasn’t been
too nice to the Fusion of late), William of Derry
should have a busy time ahead.
Jabez Coon, M.H.R. (Vic.), is acquiring
some importance as a temperance advocate. Jabe
now proposes the insertion of a clause in the
Defence Bill to provide that “no intoxicating or
spirituous liquors shall be used or sold at any
naval or military canteen, camp, fort or post.”
Evidently he has been basking in the shade of
Sammy Mauger, and wants the Commonwealth
army to fight and “swear terribly ”on water. Is
there any record of a teetotal army doing great
things except when it was drunk with fanaticism?
Or a vegetarian army ? Ora fish-eating army ?
IN ADELAIDE.
THE HUMORIST : “ Why all this fuss about wood-
blocking our streets? Why, if our Legisla-
tive Councillors would put their heads to-
gether the thing would be done."
Representative Bamford proposes a couple
of quaint amendments to the Defence Bill—one
that qualified male electors under 50 years of age
who fail to vote shall he liable to serve in the
Citizen Forces without pay ; and the other that
healthy males between 28 and 55, marriageable
but unmarried, shall also serve gratis for l(i days
per annum. They are cheerful propositions, and
both aim the brick where something hard
and sudden is called for; but they have this
objection, that they make the Defence Force a
means of punishment. And that is about the last
idea that this country ought to encourage.
Hutchison (S.A.) made a commendable
effort, t’other day, to deprive the Commonwealth
citizen forces of a bit of frill. During the con-
sideration of the Defence Bill in committee he
moved :
(a) The citizen forces shall be instructed only in those
duties that are required of them in war ; (6) there shall
be no military funerals in uniform except on active ser-
vice or in the case of those who have been on active ser-
vice ; (c) military uniforms shall be of one character only
as prescribed, and shall be worn only on parade or other
military duty ; uniforms shall be supplied free of charge
to all ranks, including officers ; (d) the system of military
salutes shall be reduced to the simplest form for military
discipline.
He, didn’t carry his point, but the proposal was a
monument of common-sense all the same.
N.S.W. Treasurer Twaddell told the
National Rifle Association banqueters the other
night that he was really anxious about the stra-
tegical position of Australia: —
One of the weakest spots in Australia was the Northern
Territory. It was so far away, and it had so few people.
Railway connection with the north was imperative. A
line should be constructed from Bourke to Cunnainulla,
and thence via Lougreach, Winton and Cloncurry to Pine
Creek, the southern terminus of the railway to Palmer-
ston. This was a great national work. Until this was
done Australia was in a weak strategical position.
Yet let no bad-minded person insinuate that
Twaddell is barracking for the round-about,
expensive, semi-coastal, largely-desert railway,
instead of the straight, all-Federal line from
Port Augusta to Port Darwin, in order to drag
the Northern Territory’s trade to Sydney. ’Twere
an unworthy thought! Twaddell wasn’t thinking
of his Congestion Policy at all. He was only
worried about the large interests of Australia.

—Daily news items for months past.
HIS EMPLOYER: “Hi, Deakin! Who th ’ L are
you paid t’ work for? ”
“Jean Sibi ” gives back-talk to the legal
profession :
Costs had an invigorating experience one day lately.
There was some small discussion as to the distribution of
a large estate, and eight ordinary barristers and three
K.C.’s, instructed by about a borough of solicitors, col-
lected to guard the various interests concerned. It is not
often that the Vic. Practice Court beholds so many Gents
doing nothin#- in particular with such success, and it is
less often that the old place displays such an air of queer
activity. It had that curious, animated appearance that
a street might have if a blind man dropped a ha’penny
and a herd of Armenians helped him to look for it.
The Federal Home Affairs Department
recently gave a local industry a chance of showing
what it could do, and it responded promptly y
doing the imported article brown. Heretoror ,
the padlocks for ballot-boxes have been importe
at a cost of 4s. (id. each, but a Tasmanian nrm
lately offered to turn out a similar article tor
3s. lid., and scooped the order for 6000 locks.
Yet Protection, according to the Freetrade
fanatics, “bolsters up local monopolies at public
expense.”
Melbourne Age lately published some
figures demonstrating how the Victorian
country shires batten on the city tax-
payer. Taking a bunch of shires, it
showed that their municipal valuations
are, on an average, 48 per cent., and in
some particularly horrible cases 80 to JO
per cent, below those of the Victorian
Water Supply Commission. Hence, when
they levy a feeble nominal rate of about Is.
in the £, as they generally do, they are
actually levying a much feebler rate of
a very few coppers in the £ on the
proper valuation. Of course they are
unable to support themselves by their
own taxation, and have to come cadging
to the Government whenever they want
money. They are always wanting and
always cadging, and a complacent Gov-
ernment is always ready to oblige. This
year the Weary Ministry has earmarked
something over £300,000 to give them,
and talks about it as if it were a right
honorable deed instead of a prodigious
public larceny.
A dead set is being made in Adelaide
against the train running down King William-
street, and the Labor members are seeking to
oust it in favor of the electric tram. The on-
slaught casts a bright blue light upon the anomalous
fact that Railway-Commissioner Moncrieff is a,lso
Chairman of the Tramway Trust. He is fighting
himself vigorously, and giving himself much
anxiety in both capacities over this matter.
THE SLOWGRESSIVES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Interest this week has spread itself over the precincts,
finding no particularly important, point on which to con-
centrate itself in the House.— Melbourne Arus (16I10I’09).
They sit in tiie seats of the mighty still, with their feet
on the Guv’ment floor ;
They’ve lounged on the Guv’ment benches, now, for a
hundred days and more ;
They’ve worked for months on their cheerful job of draw-
ing the Country’s cash,
And they never do anything ill-advised, and they never
do anything rash ; '
They find that Prudence (ahem !) is best, and it’s easy to
snail along
So long as the Country will stand for it, and the
Treasurer’s hand is strong ;
They never do anything foolish —nay, nor anything
hasty-like,
And they’ll probably bumble along like this till the
Country goes on strike.
They browse all day in the sun, their hoofs in the good
green Guv’ment grass,
And they pay small heed to the flight of time as the
beautiful moments pass;
They bask in the salary sunshine still, and they munch
green stalks at will,
For life is short, though sweet, and it’s well to take one’s
Guv’ment fill.
They’ve nibbled for months on the lotus flats, where the
fodder is sweet and long,
And the loud, wild cry of the Country comes to their ears
like a midge’s song.
"Vet who in the world will blame them now for taking
their Guv’ment whacks,
If the Country’s getting theirdoom prepared in the shape
of a sharpened axe?
They live their lives in a waking dream, and their days
go by like smoke ;
The Guv’ment lolls on the sward and loafs like a common
or garden bloke ;
There’s little to chain the wandering thought, these
warm kind dreamy days
When the Guv’ment senses the Truth of Things through a
blithering blissful haze,
And the call o’ the country’s hardly heard at the Com-
monwealth duty-works,
Where the Guv’ment'boss in the grateful shade of his
Guv’ment caucus lurks...
The drowsy day and the lazy day—they pass, and the day
draws nigh,
When down to the slaying the Land will come, red wrath
in its terrible eye!
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.
