SPORTING
NOTIONS
Now that a single line of railway has been laid
from South Kensington to the Melbourne City Abattoirs,
which are close to the southern boundary of theFleming-
t.on racecourse, a new way of conveying the people to the
Cup and other V.R.C. race meetings comes into sight.
Thousands of flatites could be carried by this new route,
if only there were a double line, and this would relieve
the frightfully congested traffic by the ordinary route on
the days of the big horse gambles.
The “ open ” appearance of the Melbourne Cup
at time of writing is indicated by the unlawful betting-
lists quotations which are not 'published in the daily
press. Bookmakers offer a price “bar five,” the five
oarred ones being Trafalgar, Alawa, Pendil, Post Town
and Prince Foote. The temptation to back one’s fancy
before the V.R.C. meeting has started is not very
strong. It were better, perhaps, to wait until
the day, and then risk an investment in the
light of Derby Day revelations and latest whispers
from the blessed stables. Probably the aspect of the
situation will be greatly changed on Saturday. The Mel-
bourne Stakes, the Derby, and even the Hotham Handi-
cap may have luminous results. Meanwhile the Cup
looks as open as it was last year, when Lord Nolan, the
winner, went out at 16 to 1, and the average starting
price of the three placed horses was 27 to 1.
How about the three-year-olds of this season ?
They have made no show at all in the big handicaps
thus far. Compared with their elders, the youngsters—
bar one—are cutting a great lack of ice in public estima-
tion. The one exception, of course, is Prince Foote, the
little bay with three white feet and a singularly piebald
nozzle, who won th.e Sydney Derby in brilliant style, after
being frequently blocked. The exact measure of Prince
Foote’s excellence could be more easily gauged if the other
three-year-olds had done anything worth mentioning.
They certainly haven’t. Patronatus, who ran second to
the little champion in the Derby at Randwick, disgraced
himself in subsequent races. Danilo (third in the Derby)
was nowhere in the Metropolitan. The Racing Calendar
shows no reason why By Play should not be Prince Foote’s'
most formidable opponent among the youngsters, and
should the favorite get invalided by bis cracked hoof the
present writer'would as soon back By Play for the Derby
oil Saturday as any of the others. 'However, assuming
that the flaw in the favorite’s understanding 1 is not so
very serious after all, the tip for the V.R.C. Derby is
Prince Foote, By Play and Danilo, in that order. If
the race be fast and the placed horses finish
fairly close together, all three of them should have
a chance in the Cup. If this writer were bound to select
two likely Derby horses for the Cup a week prior to the
event, he would take Prince Foote and By Play. The
former is already handicapped with 7st. 91b., a Derby
winner’s weight; the latter stands at 6st. illb. Con-
sidering the difference in the Cup weights of the three-
year-olds, together with the fact that a Derby winner has
to carry 7st. 91b., it is absolutely impossible to say which
of them will be the most promising chance next Tuesday'.
Patronatus, for instance, is in the Cup at 6st. 71b. Should
he run Prince Foote to a head in the Derby, he will in the
Cup have a stone the best of the weights, after allowing
21b. for the head defeat. Punters are bound to remember
that it was the light weight which accounted for Lord
Nolan’s victory last year.
In considering the Cup, the least prosy plan is
to set up a number of names as skittles, ere proceeding
to knock most of them over, and to do this without
stating any reasons why the names of other probable
starters are not set up at all. The Bulletin field includes
Trafalgar, Alawa, Lord Nolan, Post Town, Pendil, King’s
Cross, Artillerie, Ungarie, Blue Book, Prince Foote,
Flavinius, Flavel, D'unolly, Knox, Didus, Delaware,
Trelo Vouni, By Play, and the three horses placed
in the Derby', if two of them be not already named.
Lord Nolan is pitted in general reasoning against Pendil.
Both are small horses with heavy burdens. The former
could make no impression on Trafalgar in their two races
at Randwiek recently 7. On paper he has no chance to
beat the big chestnut at even weights (9st. lib.), whereas
Pendil, with Sst. 101 b., has a chance. Over goes Lord
Nolan therefore. Pendil is one of the few approved
stayers in the field. He would have won the Caulfield
Cup had the distance been 40yds. longer. He will meet
Blue Book on better terms over a course half a mile longer
than Caulfield Cup. Blue Book doesn’t look equal to a
two-mile journey, nor did he run like a genuine stayer the
other Saturday. Over goes Blue Book. Probably the
fragile Artillerie will outlast Mr. Williamson’s gigantic
steed at Flemington. Ungarie and Didus are a somewhat
difficult pair to discuss rationally'. The latter was not
suspected of stamina until he ran third in last Sydney
Cup to Trafalgar and Ungarie. He has been specially'
reserved for the Melbourne Cup and heavily backed.
His friends have great hopes; his enemies sum him up as
a leggy brute who is bound to be beaten by Ungarie and
others. Seeing that Ungarie is a better-looking horse,
‘•with a lot of the Trenton about him,” and is, in fact,
the best handicapped one in the race on his form with
Trafalgar last year this scribe favors the idea that he will
beat Didus again. So over goes Didus, along with
Flavinius, Flavel and Knox, who are doubtful stayers.
Knox, with his light weight, is bound to run very
prominently most of the time, all the same.
Of the rank outsiders that might turn up trumps,
Dunolly, half brother to Aurum and Auraria, is bred to
stay ; likewise Trelo Vouni, the good-looking 6-year-old
that ran fourth in Caulfield Cup and second in Moonee
Valley Cup last Saturday. Delaware’s claims to
consideration are a light weight and a capacity to
stay. He ran third to Lord Nolan and Tulkeroo
last year; but he is a bit more aged and not quite
so lightly weighted this year. Over goes Delaware. The
writer prefers King’s Cross, though it is not yet clear that
the son of Grafton and Crossfire can go a full two miles.
But King’s Cross stayed well enough to win the
V.R.C. Handicap (If miles) last November, and on that
form he is well handicapped at Bst. 31b. He should be
taken in any small lot to beat the field.
Now, without knocking down all the other
names the writer will reduce his fancies to six, viz.,
Trafalgar, Alawa, Pendil, King’s Cross, Ungarie,
and Prince Foote (or any three-year-old that looks better
than he on paper after the Derby). Of the top weights,
Alawa should beat Trafalgar if he can stay the full two
miles, but even if he beats him in the Melbourne Stakes
on Saturday the stamina of Alawa will not be
proved. On the other hand, should Trafalgar win the
Stakes, he will look certain to beat Alawa and Pendil in
the Cup. But to swear by Trafalgar without having “a
bit ”on Ungarie were absurd. The latter has run but one
race over two miles, and that was the best race of his life
thus far. Let,the tip then be —
Trafalgar .. .. ]
Ungarie .. .. .. ..2
Pendil 3
King’s Cross 4
* * * *
Sydney Telegraph found another grievance
last week, and wailed thusly : “Queensland newspapers,
circulating in N.S.W., contain the ruling odds on the
Melbourne Cup. It is an offence for the papers pub-
lished in this State to print them.” Well, it was the work
of the Telegraph’s own pet Government; and it is in-
deed pleasing to find that its support to Jeremiah Wade
has rebounded and banged it on its owrf bullion.
T§e fees that the jockey receives paled into
insignificance when the racing chauffeur appeared on the
scene, but the motor man who bounds along the earth at
the rate of 100 or so miles per hour has in his turn sur-
rendered pride of costliness to the aeroplaner who soars
over it. The flying man demands and receives anything
from £lOOO to £2OOO per week for the actual aviation, and
then scoops in fancy fees for delivering lectures on how it
feels to fly. At Blackpool (England) this week, Lieu-
tenant Latham staggered along at the rate of five miles
an hour against a gale blowing at the rate of 40; and
then with a sudden swerve he mounted the blast and
flashed along at the rate of 90 miles an hour. When he
comes to tell how he felt during that amazing soar—that
cannon-ball flight—people will climb over each other to
get seats ;mnd probably come away disappointed when
they hear that, unless he looked at the earth below 7 , he
felt as though he w'ere quite still. It is doubtful even if
a glance at where he came from gave him much idea of
speed. He w'ould probably still feel that he was standing
still and that the earth had broken from its moorings and
bolted. The appearance of the Baroness de la Roche
at Chalons (France), w'here she flew over the big military
camp, suggests that the aeroplanist’s high fees will not
last long, \yherever the woman can butt in, the rate of
remuneration soon falls.
* * • *
If there is one thing that the Automobile Asso-
ciation of Australia has set its face against it is the
road hog—the reckless criminal u'ho dashes headlong-
over country roads regardless of his own and everyone
else’s life. The other day a periodical entitled The
Motor in Australia made a bad blunder by publishing a
map of Sydney and its environs, showing the exact
locality of the 17 police traps which exist in and around
Sydney to catch road hogs. This sort of thing is
most deplorable. It is either a tacit accusation
that all the Sydney motorists are criminals or an
admission that The Motor in Australia is not in
sympathy with the action of the police in trying
to make the roads safe. Anyhow, it is calculated
to do motorists harm by bringing them into evil repute.
According to a recent decision in England it is illegal to
publish warnings to motorists as to where they may ex-
pect to find police traps. The traps are for the detection
of the guilty, and to publish their whereabouts is held
in England to be interfering with a police officer in the
execution of his duty. The Motor in Australia w ill be
well advised if it drops the publication of maps to assist
road hogs to escape detection.
* * * *
Unless the übiquitous George Whatmore comes
along with one of those much-talked-of flyers he is always
threatening to produce, it looks as though the next race
in Sydney waters for the Australasian Motor Boat
Championship will be a walk over for Arthur Davies in
the Fairbanks. Victoria has frankly admitted that it has
nothing to hurl against the cedar boat, and Maoriland
will not compete even if it has anything that has a show.
So by all appearances the thing is a foregone conclusion.
There is a whisper that Whatniore is building a dark
horse—something unique that will astonish the natives ;
but ever since that alleged hydroplane the public is some-
what inclined to shake its leg when Whatmore talks
darkly in hidden meanings. The reason why it shakes
its leg is that it wants to see if Whatmore is hanging on
to the end of it.
* » * *
Long and McLoughlin, the invading Yankees
who are after the Davis Cup, will arrive in Sydney by the
Aorangi on or about November 2. They' will streak on to
see Melbourne Gup, as their countrymen did on the last
occasion, after which they will settle down to three weeks’
hard practice, and on Nov. 26 commence the struggle for
the Cup at Sydney.
* * * * ■*
Apparently Maoriland has gone mad on scull-
ing. Auckland, Wanganui and Foxton all talk of
arranging big handicaps, with prizes which will make it
worth the while of any good sculler to trip along. If
the boom that is apparently coming but redeems half the
promise of its making, there will be a mighty exodus
from Australia very shortly.
But while Maoriland is howling of what it is
going to do, Perth is quietly’ negotiating for a race be-
tween Pearce and Day on the Swan River. Pearce’s re-
sponse is modest enough (a purse of £2OO and £25 for ex-
penses), and Day is hardly likely' to say’ anything more
exorbitant. So that match can be considered almost a
certainty.
* * « *
The team sent to Brisbane by the N.S.W.
Cyclists’ Union to compete at the championships meeting
is a fairly strong one, consisting of P. Mutton, Cutliffe
and P. Shepherd. On form they should come back
loaded up with pewter and household furniture.
•* * * *
Munroe, who put up the fastest time in the
Warrnambool (Vic.) ride is a mere slip, weighing 81st.
He is only 21 years of age, and has already put up’an
amazing record of 20 fastest times, three firsts, 15 seconds
and nine thirds since 1906, when he started racing. He
was born at Warrnambool, within 20yds. of the starting
point of the great W.-M. ride.
• * * *
N.S.W. League of Swimmers is bobbing up in
a style which is very disconcerting to the amateur body’.
Seven clubs are launched, also three ladies’ clubs are
affiliated ; and as each club records a good membership,
the struggles for the enclosures during the season pro-
mise to be interesting. The League opens with a gala on
November 10, when an inter-club handicap with a prize
of £5 and a gold medal will be dangled. The League is
promising its patrons big prize-money; and the Amateur
Swimming Ass. is consequently squatting around with its
head in vinegar.
* * * *
The Oodnadatta to Adelaide homing pigeon-
race—subsidised (mildly) by the Commonwealth Govern-
ment, with a view to possible helpfulness in war-time—
produced what is said to be a world’s record. There
were 281 starters, and the first bird was in his loft in
Southwark (an Adelaide suburb) in 7hrs. 49min. 46sec.
This means nearly’ a mile and a quarter, a minute for the
whole 644 miles.
* * * «
A. B. Postle, the speedy Australian profes-
sional footrunner, met an unexpected reverse at
Wigan (Britain) this week. He was racing a Yankee
named llolway over 120yds., and bad the ’Murkan appa-
rently dead to the world. Apparently the Yankee ex-
ploded a tin of Chicago sausages under his heels, or some-
thing ; anyhow, with a sudden bound and spurt that, in
his apparent condition, seemed impossible, he passed
Postle and beat him by 18in. This ability to suddenly
sprint is the feature of the Yankee-manufactured sports-
man, and suggests that the Yankee methods of training
are superior. As the Yankee shows no objection to steal-
ing our patents, there seems to be no valid reason why
the Australian should not steal the Yankee’s training
methods. It isn’t much of a revenge, but still it is better
than nothing.
• * * *
A feature of Melbourne Pennant cricket on
Saturday was the batting of Warne, of Carlton. Warne,
who smacked up 133 against University in the previous
match, this time knocked up 113 not-out against East
Melbourne, with the result that Carlton’s tally now
shows 246 for three wickets. Richmond compiled 158
against Fitzroy’, mostly by the unexpected scintillation of
a generally tired tail, and ’Roy responded softly with two
for 13. P’ran dismissed South Melbourne for 221, and then
occupied the crease long enough to wallop up six without
the loss of a single stick. Melbourne is graciously excused
for allowing University to* total 239, for it is evex*ywhere
admitted that the present Melbourne team is made up of
flannelled fools from assorted epochs. North Melbourne
is also pardoned for whacking up 257 for eight wickets
against such an amiable team as Northcote ; while Essen-
don’s generosity to Collingwood is cordially admired. It
permitted ’Wood to make 217, and as it has replied with
two wickets for four runs as a start, it evidently intends
that ’Wood shall win the match.
* * * *
Saturday saw the commencement of the third
round of Sydney first-grade cricket. It was the day of
the bat, and the posters of evening announced heavy-
scoring. Barnes, of Redfern, made 105 against Middle
Harbor; Johnston, of Gordon, totalled 103 ; but this was
easily surpassed by his club mate, Maclean, who carried
his bat against Petersham for 120. The boss batsman of
the day, however, was Pite, of Sydney, who smashed and
drove and slashed and banged till the figures stood at
137, and then Norman Deane clean bowled him. The
same bowler also got Pite’s colleague, Collins,
who had made 101 against North Sydney. Sydney’s
effort was a mighty one—3B6 runs for four wickets—and
was easily the best of the day. Gordon made 324 for six
wickets, Redfern totted up 305 for the whole innings,
Burwood had 203, and Paddington 174. Practically the
only bowler who illuminated the gloom was Connolly’, of
Central Cumberland, who took six Burwood wickets for
59 runs, although Norman Deane also did things. Only
four Sydney wickets fell. Two of them were caught by
Clarke off Deane, one was bowled directly by Deane, and
the other was caught by Deane off Proctor. So it will be
seen that Deane was in evidence.
* * * *
Victor Trumpet, who seems to be developing a
habit of opening his head, howls that the trip to Britain
was a frost. Gross receipts, he says, will reach £13,500,
of which the Board of Control will take £1238, general
exes. £6OOO, .with a balance to each pla.ver of, roughly’,
about £392, out of which each man will have to pay his
laundry, etc. If What Trumper say’s of the exes, is true,
some inquiry should be held. In 1896, with 14 players,
the bills reached only 7 £3229, and the players drew about
£6OO each. Anyhow, what is wrong with the £392 ? How
many of the XI. would have made that sum in the same
time had they stayed in Australia and toiled?
**• * *
The Victorian Cricket Ass. has once again
elected Peter McAlister as sole selector of the State team.
Mac., who doubtless does his best, has only one pair of
eyes, and therefore fails to note many things. The Ass.
yvould have been better advised to make the committee a
band of three or more.
* * * *
C. G. Macartney, returning from his Austra-
lian XI. ramblings, goes to Otago (M.L.) as coach to the
local Cricket Association. There is more horse-sense in
the choice than in persevering with the foolish idea of
importing a player from England. C. Wordsworth (of
Sydney) also took up the “coach” position at Otago
recently.

■» * * *
Champion pug Jack Johnson is indulging in a
scrap in the law courts. When he was at Salt Lake City,
recently, one of his white minions engaged rooms at a
high-class hotel for “ Mr. and Mrs. Johnson,” while the
champion and his spouse went along to where the cullud
population gave him a royal welcome. When the last
beaker was empty and dry and the night was far
advanced, Johnson and his missus tooted up to the door
of the pub, where the landlord barred the way. He had
understood that the rooms had been taken for white
people, and his hotel never catered for colored pussons
under any circumstances. To admit them meant that
every white person would march out in the morning, and
Johnson must go elsewhere. Johnson did. He drove to
the police station, but the Mormon John Dunns declined
to interfere, and where Johnson put in the night is not
known. Next day he started an action, claiming $25,000
damages.
• * * *
Young Tom Turner, a recent graduate from the
novice ranks, proved his mettle against Bob Greenshields
in a 20-round contest at Ballarat the other night. The
public brought much grist to the “ mill,” and the plugging
apprentice waded in to remove the scalp of his experi-
enced opponent. But Greenshields is a recognised expert
who has dealt, more or less successfully, with the best
Australia has made in his class, and he succeeded in keep-
ing out of the way of Turner’s vigorous belts and biffs,
although he quite failed to make the youngster look cheap
and easy at any stage of the game. Greenshields won on
points. The fact that he did not succeed in stopping
Turner is a badge of honor for the boy who was nominally
defeated.
* « * »
The coming month will complete the cycle of 21
years since the advent of the Dunlop pneumatic tyre.
And what changes it has wrought 1 Writer can remember
roaming round Maoriland on a solid-tyred high machine,
and being acclaimed as something a little less than a god,
only to have jeers flung at him six months later when he
visited the same locality on what was then termed a
“ safety ” —the thing was regarded as “ effeminate ”
also on solid tyres. In those days a machine had to
weigh at least 351 b. to stand the strain and shock the
solid tyres were unable to absorb. Then one day came a
stranger on a weird-looking contraption that seemed to
have rims as large as a buggy wheel; and he bounded as
no other bikist ever bounded before. He explained it
was a pneumatic tyre, and it was called the “Boothroyd
Scorcher,” but he said it had a habit of coming off,
and he was going in for one called “the Dunlop”
which was fastened to the rim of the machineby cemented
cloth. We were interested. Then came another strang r,
fresh from Britain, and his machine had the Clincher
tyre. He was booking orders, and he went away from
the' country town with a full book. Then the Dunlop
arrived at last. It is still here. Boothroyd Scorchers,
Clinchers, Raleighs and the rest have all gone. No one
hears of them now—they are like Nineveh and Tyre, and
also John Brown.
MORNING GALLOPS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The middle grass track was rather holding this morn-
ing, though several fast gallops were recorded.—Train-
ing note.
Oh 1 the gallops !
Morning gallops !
Those fast, press-recorded gallops 1
Oh ! what does the punter profit from the gallops in the
dawn ?
When the fav’rites and outsiders
With their flogging, spurring riders
Thunder past the staring grandstand and the packed and
anxious lawn ;
And a thing that couldn’t gallop worth a tinker’s in the
morning
Is seen coming up like blazes on the outside of the field,
Casting dirt on odds-on chances and their jockeys’ silk
adorning
With the flying clod of insult which the flying hoof doth
yield.
Oh 1 the gallops 1
Early gallops !
Those exciting, misty gallops !
You remember them with curses when you see the
fav’rite flag,
And the rank outsider flying
By his side while he is trying
Like a plucky, beaten equine to break from the rival nag ;
’Twas the fav’rite did the gallops in the dark and frostt'
morning,
And he split the precious seconds into fragments with his
heels ;
But that blanky Cold Molasses that was frozen with his
scorning,
Melts away in front and beats him to the Ring’s trium-
phant squeals.
Oh ! the gallops !
Fatal gallops!
Oh 1 put not thy trust in gallops,
I 1 or the stop-watch is a mocker and the training track a
snare !
’Tis the race, O gentle backer,
Where the pace is made a cracker—
'Tis the race that tells the story when the dust clouds fill
the air!
Then the silken jackets streaming past the batteries of
glasses
Show the morning stop-watch winner toiling madly in
the ruck,
And he’s still right in amongst it when the storm of
horseflesh passes
Where the boxed-in judge is waiting. Then you mutter,
"Just my luck t"
Oh 1 the gallops !
Morning gallops !
Do you read about the gallops?
Tell me, friend, are you a student of the cinders and the
tan?
Do you carefully remember
In October and November,
When Disaster and Delusion were amongst the “ also
ran ” ?
Do you strew their hopeless furlongs with the cash for
which you sweated ?
Do you cry aloud in anger when they’re given too much
weight ?
If you do, O simple Punter, it is much to be regretted,
Eor you’re doing your fast gallop towards the Old-Age
Pension fate.
N.s. w.
E. O’F.

Sydney Daily Whiskeks bristled with
surprise the other morning regarding “Alderman
Meagher’s peculiar statements of the effects of
‘golden ointment’ in ‘ chloroforming ’ municipal
representatives of the city in relation to certain
contracts,” and, when it had closed the window
on its beard to keep it out of the ink,
it said that the whole thing was “an
interesting mystery anyhow, and ought to be
cleared up.” But why does the Telegraph itself
not findjout the answers to the string of questions
it emptied into the four winds of King-street as to
who was chloroformed and gold-filled, and why,
and wherefore, and whaffor? Does it ex-
pect the ointment-smeared aldermen to leave
their cards, or that Richard Denis will de-
posit the heads of the grafters on the office-
counter ? Probably Richard wondered what
had fallen on him when he saw his golden grease
statement in the Stak, because he had made his
references at what was supposed to he a private,
meeting of the King division of the P.L.L. The
advertisement that Richard sought was from
quite a different medium than the newspapers,
but it so happened that a pressman mistook the
door of the hall for the back entrance to a pub,
and walked in to purchase a hot pie. When he;
recovered from the shock of his mistake, he found
.the noble Roman making his oration. Next day
the noble Roman was the shocked person, and
after that the learned Dr. Ward of the Telegraph
was shocked, and the public was shocked, and
things in general were shocked.

Continental Square Tread Tyres
The new season’s Model de Course Square Tread Tyres are now here, and in the
quality of fabric, quality of rubber, and quality of workmanship are ahead of any
other tyre in the world.
The new “ Square Tread ” is the ideal tyre for touring purposes, and not
only contains a higher grade of rubber, but is heavier in weight than any similar
tyre known to the motoring world.
Continental Three-Rib Tyres
The new Continental three-rib Tyres mark a new standard of excellence in non-
skids, and in a series of severe tests on Australian roads have given the fullest
satisfaction.
The quality of rubber, the workmanship, the hard wearing qualities of these
tyres are giving satisfaction to every motorist who is using them.
These tyres carry the most liberal of all guarantees —the guarantee of
complete satisfaction.
Depots : Melbourne, Sydney, Adelaide, Brisbane,
Launceston. Perth, Christchurch, Auckland.

Business. Robbery, etc.
A Portarlington (Vic.) fisherman netted ]os.
for a week’s work in catching and marketing: over 20
baskets of fish. It was such a herring-gutted return that
the fisherman decided to trawl the mystery on his own
account. He hadn’t grot far before he learnt that one
day, when fish was abundant, only 10 baskets were put up
for auction out of 185 that came in. The rest were put in
the cool stores, and at a later favorable opportunity were
unloaded at from 10s. to 14s. apiece. As his fish were
amongst these stored lots, the Portarlington man natu-
rally expected a decent return; but this is where the
auctioneer came in. He was not only an auctioneer, but
a dealer. He had bought the whole consignment at an
average of 25., and of course he collared the proceeds
when they realised 10s. or 14s. ; so he merely paid the
fisherman' 2s. per basket, less commission, freight,
cartage, dues, etc. The fisherman dropped his inquiries
just there and had a conversation with a lawyer ; but the
lawyer assured him that the Melbourne councillors and
the seven aldermen had already investigated the mat-
ter and found that the market methods were the very
acme of excellence, and it really wasn’t any use talking
Law on the subject.
• ■* • *
“Anti Melbourne has now an “ice manu-
facturers’ association,” which, if it isn’t a ring, or trust,
or combine, or other swindle of the sort, is at any rate
one of those “horrible understandings” which squeeze
the public just as dry. It’s a wonderful business, this ice-
making, if its “properly handled”—that is to say, if the
public is parcelled out like so many lambs to the various
butchers. A ton of water is worth 3d., yet the lowest
that the ring permits ice to be sold at is 255. net per ton, at
the works, and only at that price to a few individuals called
“ vendors.” Other people pay 355. to 40s. net. Car-
riage is extra. Now, the'value of the plant in an ice
wbrks is not more than in a saw mill, flour mill, engi-
neers’shop, foundry, or many another industry capable
of turning over the same amount of money annually:
also the wages paid are not so good, and the number of
employees is much smaller. Yet look at the relative cost
of the raw material to the manufactured product. Then
bear in mind that the ice factory runs seven days a
week and 24 hours a day, just like the interest on a
mortgage. It is a matter of common knowledge that in
Melbourne all the ice works are extending their plants,
although they had a “bad summer.” But 1 wouldn’t
mind that if, with very few exceptions, they didn’t im-
port nearly all their machines, although there are works
in Australia turning out plant quite as good, and for the
same purpose. It may be a shade dearer ; but who can
wonder at that when the workmen’s cost of living is put
up by such “ horrible understandings ” as this ? And, of
murse, it isn’t the only one. In fact, the “ Ice Manufac-
turers’Association ” has a twin-brother who looks after
the frozen meat trade, and keeps our own beef and
mutton prices high by glutting the foreign markets.
Bowen (Queensland) fruit growers are out after
Bowen shipper, as a test, split his weekly consignment
into two lots of 33 cases each. And although th fru
each lot wag exactly the same, one agent returned -s. 6d.
a case, and the other Is. 3d. The agent who sent ■ g
the low-priced
agent complained that the consignment was over-npe and
wet. The complaint was accompanied by a fervent op
W
ripeness aiid wetness. The Bowen Fruit-growers’ Asso-
ciation now reckons that if other North Bananaland fruit-
growers combine with them in sending a trustworthy
checktnan to watch the slippery agent, there 1
good margin out of the savings, even after paying i/iie
checkinan’s hotel and cigar expenses.

‘ Ivin man s Man : lin going to be rather open
to the chaff bestowed on beermaker Nick Fitzgerald,
when he said that Australia’s great need was a nice light
ale. The problem now is to get beef to Europe. It is
Australia’s misfortune that we cannot do it unless, we
freeze it. We are just a. little outside the zone or radius,
The Argentine sends chilled beef to London. The United
States can send any quantity. We cannot send a single
cargo. An Australian in London goes into ecstasies over
the grand English beef, and is coolly told it is from
Chicago or La Plata. The best is, of course, sent alive. A
Briton never sings “ Oh, the Old English Roast Mutton!”
Beef, beef, all the time. Get Australian beef there, and
it is one step towards solving the Northern Territory
problem. That territory can carry two million bullocks
and ten million sheep, in addition to its more powerful
virtues in cotton, coffee, sugar, rice, etc. Get beef to
England, and Australia can be bulloclced to its utmost
capacity. The beef man’s mouth waters as he sees the
careless way in which sheep carcases are tossed about at
Sydney or Melbourne wharves and London docks. Beef
is the delicate article—not to be frozen like mutton, only
chilled. I have not the slightest doubt that the problem
will be solved. Lookback to the troubles and triumphs
of Mort, and take heart to try and try again.
RUNNING THE CUTTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The position of the Dominion is entirely different.—
Maoriland press item.
We are different and very much superior—
Oh, the spuds in our yard grow green—
The prospect that allures is much cheerier
As long as J. Ward can raise a bean.
We’re not drunk yet, though getting beerier,
While Cohen fills ’em up and isn’t mean.
Yes, decidedly prosperity will come again—
Oh, the spuds in our yard grow high—
And whenever our affairs are looking rum again
We’ll face them with a Dreadnought eye,
Send Ward to the British pub to bum again
When the credit and the cash run dry.
He will always run the cutter to the Jew again—
Oh, our spuds in the dew are wet! —
He is always very ready to renew again
Any pleasant little circumstance of debt.
We will borrow, borrow, borrow till all’s blue again ;
We’re superior and different, you bet !
MAORILAND.
D.M.W.

The Commissioners are prepared to pay interest on deposits up to £5OO.
ADVANCE DEPARTMENT.
THIS Department offers most advantageous terms to settlers or intending settlers on the land, a few of
the important features being :
(a) Advances made from £5O to £2OOO.
(b) Low AND FIXED rate of interest during:
currency of loan.
(c) Money may be lent for any term up to
31 years.
(d) Loan instalments (interest and principal)
are payable half-yearly.
(e) Borrower has the right to repay the loan
on any instalment date; interest to that
date only being charged.
The tenures upon which the Advance Department may lend are(l) Freeholds, (2) Certificated Con-
ditional Purchases (with or without associated Conditional Leases), (3) Uncertificated Conditional
Purchases (with or without associated Conditional Leases), (4) Homestead Selections (before or after
issue of the Homestead Grant), (5) Settlement Leases, (6) Settlement Purchases, (7) Conditional
Purchase Leases.
SAVINGS BANK DEPARTMENT.
THIS Department makes advances up to £25,000 upon city and suburban freeholds and on country lands well
situated and of good quality at moderate rates Of interest.
Detailed information on application to Branch Managers, or to the Secretary, at Moore Street, Sydney.

IN BANANA LAND
On the Downs.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ril HE grass grows green on the Plains of
Peace, and the breeze goes gently by,
And the boss o’ the plains goes drowsing
around with content in his full, fat eye.
The sheep says “ Ma-a-a ! ” on the Plains of Pence,
in a manner serene and kind,
For the tucker is plentiful all around, and there’s
nought to distract its mind ;
And the boss sits down, when the evening comes,
to a pipe in his old armchair.
And his heart rejoiceth, his soul is glad, and he
knoweth no want nor care ;
lor the sunshine blesses the owner’s toil, and the
profits come sure and soon
On the Plains of Peace, where the sheep and the
boss are the lords of the golden noon.
The grass grows green on the Plains of Peace—
and there ought to be men there, too ;
There ought to be men, and the sounds of toil and
a township or two on view,
And there ought to be women and kids about—
these certainly should abound
In the land of promises unperformed, in the places
of peace profound ;
Not peace and sheep, but men and noise, and
ploughs on the Plains of Peace,
And a fair, square deal for the cheap, plain hand
and his cheap, plain elbow-grease ;
Vnd the farmer must some day plant his hoofs
where at present the farmer’s not,
On the Plains of Peace where the sheep is god.
“To Hell with the farmers ! ” What .?
The boss of his own sheep-universe is great on
the Plains of Peace,
And he spends his days in a slow, calm, sure pur-
suit of the golden fleece ;
The boss of a hundred thousand sheep, and of
laborers nine or ten,
He suffers from plain, swelled head, and feels a
prince among mere paid men ;
And night after night far jumbucks bleat, and the
noise of them fills the earth,
And the boss lies warm in his lavendered sheets
and calculates what they’re worth ;
And there’s hard, cheap toil for the few that toil,
and gold for the few that don’t,
On the Plains of Peace where the big boss might
do wonderful things—but won’t !
F. H.
Some Absurd Matrimonial Complications.
lhe case of Mrs. Jones, who might really be
Payne, or who might be partly Jones and partly
ij yne ’ arose laist w «ek in Queensland. It was an
old, old trouble, or' the tail-end of one. Thirty-
six years ago Catherine Kiley married Edward
Payne in N.S.W, Twenty-eight years ago she
got a decree nisi— which is usually half a divorce
—against him in Queensland ; but she never
applied for the decree absolute, which is usually
Hie other half of the divorce.
Tn *s seems to have left her
half-Payne. Twenty-four
years ago, the lady who was
apparently half-Payne mar-
ried Reuben Jones. Appar-
ently something happened
to Jones, for one year ago he
applied in N.S.W. to have
his marriage declared null
and void, on the ground
that Mrs. Jones was really
Mrs. Payne, and had been
so. all along. He won his
case, and the lady became
Mrs. Payne again—in
N.S.W. anyhow. Then she
fled to Queensland, and
asked for a decree absolute
to finish her unfinished
divorce of 28 years ago,
and she got that. It would
seem that in Queensland
the decree absolute isn’t so
necessary as in some other
places, and the Queensland
Court was willing to regard
it as the mere formal completion
of a matter which had, even 28
years ago, been complete enough to
answer all the reasonable requirements
of Jones. Having thus, in 1909, got her
Queensland divorce of 1881 made com-
plete and regular, the lady is nowin a
position to he legally married —or to have
been legally married—to Reuben Jones
in N.S.W. in 1885, but how far that
affects the circumstance that her marriage
with Jones was annulled in N.S.W. in
1908 it is hard to say, Apparently in
N.S.W. the lady isn't married at all, the
N.S.W. view being that her marriage with
P ly.rie was annul <1 inßnsbanein 1909,, and thatshe
was never really married to Jones at any time. In
Queensland the law holds that she is married to
.tones; ,that she was sufficiently divorced from
Payne in 1881 to enable her to legally wed Jones
in 1885 ; and that the cancellation of the Jones
marriage in N.S.W. is null and void. Being Mrs.
Jones in one State and not in the other, her
status, if she sat on the allegedly rabbit-proof
border fence, is a difficult question. Her status
in Victoria or Westralia is also a difficult ques-
tion. Plainly it is time the Commonwealth took
over the whole marriage and divorce business, so
that a person will either be wholly Jones or not
Jones at all. Meanwhile any correspondence
intended for this lady had better be addressed to
“Mrs. Partly-Jones.”

The three Anti-Sosh candidates at the next
Senate elections will probably be Cowley, Joe
Millican and Whittingham. Two of them have
been passed out of the Assembly, so Anti-Sosh
naturally picks them up. Cowley is an engineer
bv trade but a politician by profession, Millican is
a Charters Towers mining man, and Whittingham
is a Western squatter who can talk for nearly
five minutes without repeating himself.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, October 23, 1909.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
There seems some chance of our getting a full-
fledged Governor to ourselves again. Sir William
Macgregor and Lady Mac and certain members of
the family are afloat upon the Orsova, which is
due to tie up at Pinkenba on December 2. As his
Ex. has a well-developed taste for Papuan heat
he will probably feel good here, and wear his sun
hat with a rakish air. Lieut, the Hon. H. R.
Scarlet (whose name suggests a Past, but who is
probably merely a member of the Baron Abinger
brood) has been selected to wear a white-fronted
suit and be bored in general.
What a terrible tale is that which was hurled at
the Pioneer Club women by the Courier this
week. According to that biased publication, the
Pioneers’ hilarity completely upset, and other-
wise interfered with, the enjoyment of some 30
plumbers who were holding a smoke gathering on
the lower floor of the Eschenhagen Cafe. Now to
my personal knowledge there are hundreds of
otherwise peaceable citizens all over the
civilised world who simply ache to dis-
concert even one plumber, having
suffered untold misery at the hands
of the whole plumb tribe ; so to
get nigh two-score of them in
one room and make them all
miserable for two hours is
a feat. Bricklayers, car-
penter s, stonemasons,
blacksmiths and the like
are brethren ; but the
plumber, the pawnbroker
and the undertaker are
outside the pale, or ought
to be. Never yet did I
have a water-pipe repaired
by a plumber who didn’t
leave a worse leak behind
Inin. Now I get any unavoidable plumbing done
by a shoemaker and a broken-down actor, and the
results are satisfactory.
After all this exordium I learn with grief that
the Pioneers didn’t harass any plumbers. First
of all, the Pioneers hadn’t any music to annoy
plumbers, with ; second, there were no plumbers
there to be annoyed. The gathering was a birth-
day party.
A closer acquaintance with the Widow Beilairs
brings out strongly the fine work of Miss Eosslyn
Vane ;is the weepful Julia, and Mr. Conway
Wingfield as O’Hara. The former further im-
proved this pleasant impression in “Sweet Nell ”
as the Lady Olivia. King Charles 11., in the
person of Mr. Claude King, fell off his horse, or.
as he modestly put it during a brief explanation
in the first interval, he dismounted hurriedly on
top of a stone. The result was a strained ankle, a
limp, and a pain in the high-heeled shoe.
Touching the stage, the Lang-Meason lady
writes me from the wilds of Bundaberg to say
that Walter Bentley’s northern barnstorm has
been successful, and that “Hamlet” has got
through without a scratch. The co. strikes this
city for exactly two hours on Thursday on its
tumultuous way south.
The Day and Knight co. also drops a line from
a town called Timaru, in the Lord’s Own Place.
Its members seem impressed by the talkative-
ness of the legislators, and by the character of the
tea supplied by that verbose body on the day when
the laundry opened for soiled” political finery.
Beatrice was there drinking with Mrs. Ward.
There has been what the small bov describes as
a bonser scramble for that vacant portfolio in Hie
Kid-Philp Cabinet. Philp himself was offered it
PERTINENT.
LADY: “Is this a Jit book for mg daughter to
read! ”
BOOKSELLER: “ Well, madame. I’m not acquainted
with your daughter, so I cannot pass an
opinion .”
and declined. The money was strong upon Cribb,
of the coal-mining Bundamba electorate, while
Petrie, Forsyth, the recovered Tolmie, and Mac-
artney were all howling. To give the thing away
was to make at least half a dozen enemies, so the
Kid has decided to leave well alone, and the work
is to be divided between Barnes and Appel. Some
people say that the reason the chief Kid is steer-
ing so clear of making appointments is that he is
out on a little matter of the kind for himself in
London town, though it seems an unreasoning sort
of a reason.
Yet another misguided bachelor is to be roped
in to domesticity. The other conspirator is
named Miss Bellfield, from Dumaresque.
The Robertson couple will play skitties on
Saturday. That is to say they will be at home at
the Booroodabin bowling green, and their guests,
after having filled up to the required bowling
figure on tea and cake, will be invited to smite
the jack.
The Mater Miserieordi® Bazaar is still going
strong, and the number of bachelors whom I have
come across burdened with teacloths, dolls, rock-
ing horses, and goodness knows what, that a
properly brought up bach, has no use for, would
scandalise you. I hear that the financial result is
a trifle disappointing.
Charlie Tom and Miss Doris Gill said “ Yes” a
couple of times to the accompmiment of the
Voice on Wednesday, and are now seeking seclu-
sion and cool breezes in the Blue Mountains.
Another sojourner in your midst is John Whit-
tingham and his bride, who used to be Miss
Dalziell. They are on the first lap of a round-the-
globe trot.
liulloo electorate (Queensland) has no church
in all its rust area. One was erected in the '9o’s,
hut the cattle ate most of it during the drought.
These are the remains. It is through, this district
that some people propose to deviate the transcon-
tinental railway.
The sad news comes from the Great Fog that
Mrs. George Warry, who, as Miss Lizzie Heal,
was so well known in social circles here, lies ill in
a London hospital, with very little hopes of ulti-
mate recovery. The Warrys have been honey-
mooning for the last five months.
The Milk Institute is in the mud as regards
funds, and the worried committee finds itself con-
fronted with a monthly deficit of something like
£2O. Now the Institute, the last legacy of Lady
Chelmsford, is doing good work. According to a
prominent physician, it is saving more lives than
any hospital. Yet the municipal body is evidently
satisfied to see it break down rather than supply
the £240 to set it on its legs again. In Melbourne
the municipal folk shell out twice as much.
The rush of immigrants to this State is becoming
awful, and very soon the place will be as the turret
of Babel for dialects. To date there is a dash of
German, Spanish, Swedish, Danish, Russian and
Italian, and the accumulated aroma of sourkrout,
garlic, green soup, vodka and macaroni beats the
band.-
The city fathers have passed the sketch plans
of the Town Hall after much vituperation, and
unless there is another row inside of seven weeks
there is imminent danger of the foundation stone
being laid with the name of a defeated candidate,
Thos. Wilson, thereon.
Sincerely yours,
MARIO.
The other day the newly elected member
for Fassifern (Q.), Arnold Wienholt, staggered his
constituents by presenting one year’s Parlia-
mentary salary, £3OO, to a local hospital. A wild
ptean of jubilation rang through the Queensland
press. For many years the Weinholt Estates Co.
held the Marvvale and Fassifern stations back
from settlement, and thereby retarded the pro-
gress of two most important districts for the best
part of a generation. Recently a paternal Govern-
ment purchased Mary vale at a generous figure, and
that estate was immediately bought up at prices
ranging from £3 to £l3 per acre. Recently also
Fassifern was cut up and went off like hot cakes.
It is thus quite understandable how Wienholt can
afford to give £3OO to a local hospital. The piti-
ful part of the business is that the local farmers
imagine they’ve struck a Peabody in the politician
who has just handed over the paltriest fraction of
the wealth the Wienholt Co. levied as a toll
on the industry of Southern Queensland. It is
alleged that the donor above mentioned does not
believe in payment of members —hence the gift of
£3OO, But surely the man who buys popularity
with £3OO of his Parliamentary salary is using the
money of the State for his own advantage just as
much as the man who spends it on beef and clothes
and bread and butter. If a candidate for Parlia-
mentary honors made a donation of £3OO to some
institution in his constituency a week before the
election a very harsh term might be applied to
the proceeding, and yet the donating of the sum a
week after the last election and three years before
the next one may be done with the same intention
and may have the very same effect.
When Lennon won the Herbert seat from
Cowley, three years ago, it was put down as an
accident; and, partly because he has dodged about
a good deal, it was reckoned that he wouldn’t stay
long in Herbert. Yet he put up a good fight this
time, and hung on. Thirty years or so back young
Lennon was handing out groceries in a Stawell
(Vie.) store. Then he wanted to sample Mel-
bourne, and got a billet in the Mines Department,
Brough Smyth was the fierce old tyrant there—
Smyth, whose bitter tongue was known to make
grown men weep, and who drove even a
Government department to such fierce revolt at
last that he had to be suspended in order to avert,
a general stoppage—and when the historiealinquiry
came along, Lennon was one of the witnesses who
damned him. After that he joined the
Bank of Australasia. E. S. Parkes was
superintendent then —just about the ablest
banker and the greatest autocrat who ever
sat in a general-manager’s chair in Australia.
Parkes, who was killed afterwards in the Windsor
railway accident, had most of his officers crouch-
ing to him, and despised them when they did it.
Lennon didn’t, so the “old man” picked him
out to open up business at Townsville. From the
bank he passed over to Burns, Philp, but he
quitted that, of course, before he burst forth as a
Laborite. When his party ‘‘ arrives ” the finan-
cial experience of Lennon, ex-bank manager and
ex-bank director, ought to come in handy.


MODEL FARMS AND SUGAR.
A wonderful sugarcane district— Marburg—exists
within a few miles of Brisbane.
There the hill tops are covered with flourishing
cane, above the level of the frosts.
'The forest giants are being displaced by the thick
rattoon growths of the sugarcane.
Young cane is seen growing amongst the stumps
of the newly-cut trees.
tn this district the traveller will find a flourishing
settlement of thrifty Germans, who are
fortunate in having chosen one of the
FATTEST DISTRICTS OF QUEENSLAND.
But millions of acres of similar country are obtain-
able—so the immigrant need not despair.
Farther north the men who are already on the way
to competence and fortune hold out the hand of
friendship to the world. Their ivords are
“ We invite intending settlers in Queens-
land to come along and throw in their
lot with us.
“ We will share and share alike
THE GLORIOUS INHERITANCE
which the pioneers of the district
discovered for us. ”
Queensland To-day:
the Queen State
of the Common-
wealth.
No. XLIII.
By J. D. FITZGERALD,
Barrister-at-LAW.
•rOKTUNES IN SUGAR.—V.
A. GLANCE at the kind
of country available in
Queensland, and the inducements to cane plant-
ers. Take Rosewood and Fassifern, in Southern
Queensland. At Marburg, near the main line
connecting Brisbane and Sydney, a sugar mill has
been in existence for 15 years. Started as a
private concern, it is now owned by the Marburg
Sugar Company, and it has been fitted with the
latest machinery and appliances. It has a capacity
for crushing and treating 1200 tons of cane per
week. The district around Rosewood is devoted
to mixed farming. Coffee flourishes there.
Dairying is the backbone of the place, and cane
growing can be advantageously combined with
dairying, as the tops of the cane make good fodder
for cattle. On this piece of Queensland’s territory,
which a few years ago was covered by the primeval
jungle, the land is employed all the year round in
raising alternate crops—grapes, maize, lucerne,
oats, potatoes, cotton, sugarcane ; and these are
, combined with bee culture, grazing and a timber
- industry. The material and productive side is
, the one which naturally appeals to the prospective
I emigrant. But much could be written upon tne
tbeautiful scenery of this district, its rolling hills
. and distant mountains, its patches of forest, left
: standing picturesquely when the scrub was
‘“banished” More, too, could be written about
tthe climate, the beautiful mildness of the
winter, which makes all the sugar regions, from
smith to north, health resorts for Sydney, Mel-
bourne and Tasmanian
folk in the bleak south-
ern days.
I was through this
district in June last,
having been taken
through by Mr. Denis
Keogh, member for the
district in the Queens-
land Parliament. After
leaving Ipswich we soon
found ourselves among
the picturesque farms
of the German settlers
of Marburg. The day
was clear with soft
sunshine over every-
thing — and we were
snugly wrapped up in thick overcoats for the pur-
pose of accommodating ourselves to the “ unen-
durable heat ” of Queensland. A lovely cold
•breeze blew in our faces. Its effect was perceptible
,jn the nice complexions of the children and the
-women. On either side of the road were beautiful
ifarms, showing maize and lucerne side by side,
with occasionally a pumpkin patch, the deep
yellow of the ripened rinds of the pumpkin gourds
(contrasting with the light yellow of the sugar
rat toons and the green stalks of the young corn.
Hear the dam which was constructed to supply
Ithe surrounding district we found the Public
School, and at our request the teacher made the
scholars troop out into the sunshine to have their
photographs taken. A fine, sturdy, happy-looking
lot of children they were—boys and girls; and if
the future of Australia is dependent upon its
Ibirth rate, it can be safely said that so far as the
Marburg district is concerned that future is
perfectly safe.
All round us were undulating hills, and as we
looked -westward were the dark-blue peaks of the
Main Range, with Cunningham’s Gap clearly dis-
tinguishable from the neighboring gorges. In this
district the sugar is grown on the hill-sides to
.escape the frost. In the very centre of things is
the mill, and above on the crest of the hill is
the residence of Mr. Gibson, the manager of the
anill. From the verandah of the Gibsons’ house a
•magnificent view of the surrounding country may
Hje obtained. Hot far from this point a branch
a-ailway will run to- tap this fertile district, and
&ringit into.communication 'by,rail with Brisbane

and the ships. One of the striking scenic features
of the district is Tallegalla Hill, on the summit of
which two fig trees have been left standing.
These constitute a landmark for miles and miles
around. The soil is rich, and is capable of grow-
ing anything. So far as the intrinsic beauty of
the landscape is concerned, I have never seen
anything to excel it in the landscapes of Britain,
France, Germany or Austria, Passing along the
toad we met the pastor and his wife in a good,
substantial buggy ; and, further on, the typical
German waggons, with their ribbed sides, aniid
the other surroundings, reminded me of certain
parts of Westphalia or the Valley of the Drave in
Austria.
We find another typical sugar region in the
Mackay District, at the other extreme of the
great State—in North Queensland. This area
also offers a hearty welcome to the man or the
family who come along and take up sugar-growing.
The anxiety of the present settler is to have his
district developed. The more new-comers of the
right sort, the better for him ; and he knows this,
and is prepared to do everything to make the
emigrant comfortable, not, as he avows, from any
sentimental or philanthropic motives, but
out of pure business reasons. The spirit of
the dwellers in this district is expressed in these
words :
Our people have a splendid
district, which they wish to
see developed to its utmost
capacity; and they invite
intending settlers in Queens-
land to come along and
throw in their lot with
them and share and share
alike the glorious inherit-
ance which the pioneers of
the district discovered for
them.
There are in this dis-
trict 142 square miles
of Crown lands open
to selection at an aver-
age price of 15s. per
acre. There is the
chance for the man of
small capital; and when
this quantum is taken up, more will be
available. “ This,” says an official pub-
lication, “is all on the coast side of the
range, and varies in distance from town from
27 to 45 miles. Much of the land is within easy
access of the tramlines belonging to the various
sugar mills in the district. A new road, recently
constructed to the Eungella Tableland, has made
accessible large quantities of merchantable tim-
bers and acres of rich Crown land.” In this
region, too, dairying could be made to flourish
exceedingly; but King Butter gives way to
King Sugar in this royaume, for the latter
is monarch; and it says much for the
attractiveness and for the lucrative nature
of cane-planting that dairying is neglected.
Later on, doubtless, these two, with other
sources of wealth, will be developed side by
side.
Another phase of the ad vantages of this district
is pointed out in these words :
There are 10 large sugar factories actively operating,
with capacities from 4000 to 8000 tons sugar, most of
them controlling steam tramway systems totalling 200
miles in length. It is worthy of note that of the
11 mills built in various parts of (Queensland bv Gov-
ernment loan money, one of the Mack ay Central
Mills—the Racecourse—was the first, to repay in
full, out of its profits, the amount borrowed for
its construction.
In 1907 this district
produced 41.430 tons
of sugar from a thou-
sand farms, and upon
this tVie bounty from
the Federal Govern-
ment amounted to
£122,590 in that year.
To produce this
amount of sugar
350.432 tons of cane
were raised, and the
average price paid
the farmer for the
cane was 12s. fld. per
ton, equal to £219,020.
The value of the
sugar produced in this one district alone in
the year was £580,738.' The official guide-book
makes this significant statement :
The half-million pounds’ worth of sugar, equalling a pro-
ducing value of £5OO per farm, was the product of a
thousand farmers, fully three-fourths of whom came to
the district as agricultural laborers.
And not only is the immense area of Crown
land referred to above available, but private
estates which have been used for grazing and
other purposes, or have lain idle, have been
cut up, and can be had by the small
capitalist at from 15s. to as high as £l2
per acre. Enough has been said to outline
the prospects of the settler in this, which
will some day be one of the richest of the staple
industries of Queensland. If wool is in universal
demand to-day, so is sugar. If dairying can make
the fortune of a nation as well as the individual,
so can sugar. Not only is it the primary pro-
ducer who is benefited by the growth of a great
industry like this, suited to the climate and the
soil of a vast territory. Here again in this, as in
the other industries described, the workers, the
tradespeople, the hundred and one enter-
prises which depend upon the establish-
ment and successful maintenance of a great
national industry, are benefited, and through
all the veins of the nation’s industrial
life the wealth created in the sugar in-
dustry pulsates, and the results are woven in
the web and woof of the material and com-
mercial development of the State and Common-
wealth.

Melba . Theatrical posters are often unconsciously
humorous. A case in point is the old familiar one,
“See Charles Warner in Drink." A well-known Ander-
sonian melodrama is “The Price of Sin.” The bills
usually read : “ The Price of Sin—3s., 2s. and Is.” Some-'
what similar was the poster of another Andersonian pro-
duction which the writer saw in Perth, with Walter
Dagleish as juvenile lead. The hoarding's were gay with
the legend “ Why Woman Sins—See Walter Dagleish
at Her Majesty’s Theatre.” Way back in the ’eighties an
American actress named Louise Pomeroy was starring
in Melbourne, the late Herbert Flemming being in her com-
pany. Louise was a heavy (in every sense) tragedienne—
a leg’lllar Dreadnought of the boards. One of her stock
pieces was “ Led Astray,” and the posters thereof were
the standing joke of Melbourne in those days. And no
wonder, for they ran—“ Louise Pomeroy—Led Astray
for One Night only." J
“P.Q.”: Asa frequenter of biograph shows, I derive
almost as much interest from the comments of my fellow
regulars as from the pictures. The other night, at
West’s, I recognised among the suitors for a millionaire’s
daughter a tall, gaily-caparisoned youth who had the
previous week appeared as the villain in a case of
attempted suicide, supervening on seduction under
aggravated circumstances. I wasn’t the only one who
recognised him. “See that feller,” observed a factory-
gul behind me, ‘ that’s the same one ! I’d swear to him
out of er dozen 1 Ido ’ope ’e don’t marry ’er ! It was ’im
that treated that girl somethin’shockin’. You remember!
larst week. Nice girl she seemed, too.” Her escort con-
the identification, and gradually others did.
Ihat s nil 1 ” came from the rear. “ 1 know ’im ! ’E’s
a . , . <!( , )W ’ a , n ’ 110 '"‘stake ! ” The story was gradually
unfolded, apd the erstwhile scoundrel played the hero’s
strenuous part, knocking down presumptuous persons
saving the heroine’s life, rescuing little children from
beneath motor cars, and paying the rent for needy
washerwomen. In the end, he carried off the millionaire’s
daughter by a masterpiece df gallantry. But the audience
didnt enthuse to any extent ; the psychology of the
traducers sudden conversion was too much for it.
“A.D.”: “Harrison O.’s” item (19/S/’O9) recalls to
mind an hilarious incident in the tour of that wild
and woolly tragedian, Bandmann, who bounded across
the Australian theatrical landscape a few decades ago.
A Danish Ha.mlet was right enough, but a Danish Macbeth,
who said, Leadt on, Mactuff, undt tamdt pe he who
hrst cries holdt, enuff," and rent his kjlts in the effort
wouldn t gee at all. Hence the tour was a record at
ghastly failure. The Herr and his wife comprised the
whole outfit when he reached the little town of Glad-
stone (Queensland), and the memory of his first glad
night is still treasured by old-timers. The Town Hall
with its packing case stage, was the one show place of the
town that should have been the capital of North Australia,
but isri t. There were no dressing-rooms attached, and
mummers had to hire the reading-room of the equally
miniature School of Arts. But cash was short with the
Herr, so he improvised a dres-ing-room right in the fore-
front of the OP. wing. It was a very midget changing-
place, and a sheet screened the Herr from the audience
In one of lus quick-change impersonations Bandmann,had
to haul off a pair of silken tights. But he was large and
bullc.i, and the lights were very tight indeed. With his
back to the audience, he was hopping frantically on one i
leg, and wrestling like a Viking with the obdurate cos-
tume when he collided.with the sheet. Alas ! that sheet,
was not a fixture, and a perfect hurricane of ribald
applause greeted the appearance of a large, unclothed
perspiring rear section of the Herr before the footlights’
The show ran for three nights, and every cowboy for
miles tolled up thinking this particular scene was part of
the business. That was the most successful night in the
.Whole of.Bandmann’s disSstrous tour.

“Melb.” : Argus correspondents have of late been ex-
panding upon the history of local playhouse building
and, of course, there is some mixing up/The first theatre
was a bunk named the Victoria, in Bourke-street, near
h e Bull and Mouth. Next came a little playhouse called
the Adelplu, I‘linders-lane, between Elizabeth and Queen
streets. The Queen’s Theatre, owned by J. T Smith
seven times Mayor, opened in 1845, Tinder Nesbitt.’
Three months afterwards Coppin’s company came there
from Launceston. A star tragedian of the Queen’s was
Morton King subsequently auctioneer, magistrate and
legislator. His real name was Mark Last. He had been
a Sydney timber merchant, but took to the boards in the
other way. No new theatres came till 1855, when there
were three—the Royal, Olympic and Astley’s. The Royal
was built by John Black, a carried. The Olympic of iron
was brought by Coppin from England in the steamer
Argo. Brooke followed, in the steamer Pacific.
He opened at the Queen’s. The Olympic opened
with wizard Jacobs. G. B. VV. Lewis lost £30,000
over Astleys. It opened as a circus. There had
already been two large circuses. Noble’s opened
on the north-west corner of Bourke and Sprinv
streets. It was a canvas place, afterwards turned into
wood. Rowe’s circus came from San Francisco, and
opened on the corner of Lonsdale and Stephen streets
Coppin erected the Olympic on the same site. Astlev’s
was turned into the Royal Amphitheatre, for drama.
Next, Alex. Henderson, from Bendigo, named it the
Princess Theatre. It was all wood. Rowe came back
from V/aliforma and built a new theatre in 1858 the
American Hippodrome, extending from the back of the
Theatre Ro\al to the hospital. Afterwards it was called
the Prince of Wales Theatre and the Lyceum. The next
new theatre was Coppin’s little Pantheon, Oremorne
Gardens. He also built the Havmarket, 1862. Then came
K OP A? a . HO j Se ’ 18 1 2 ’ nevv Royal (after the fire) ; Bijou,
1875 ; Alexandra and new Princess’s, 1885 ; King’s, 1908.
When tlie Australian pro. is too dead to be a star any
longer he ought to have acquired velocity enough to be a
veij fair comet. Take a recent Williamson rush for a
sample. After its usual turn at Sydney Royal on Thurs-
day evening, the “Jack and Jill” panto, c'o. shot itself
and its acres of scenery, props., etc., aboard a special
train anu whirled to Albury; then shot itself into
another special to Melbourne ; had a 30 minutes’ stop
sa ” dwich i rushed to Adelaide and
reached the Ho.y Patch at 6'30 on Saturday evening-
jumped down to the Kanowna which was waiting with
steam up ; slid aboard like a greased telegram ; and then
jumped away across the Bight, the intention bein<r to
make Fremantle about daylight the following Thursday
morning. Should the effort succeed the co. waf to shovv in
J? remantle the same evening, and thus establish the
record of appearing atone side of the continent on one
Thursday and at the other end the next Thursday.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The frowning actor, tall and lean,
With tumbled hair and piercing eye,
Put on his striking Hamlet mien,
And told us how, in days gone by,
He shook the world, as never since,
With his astounding Danish Prince.
“ I took my audience,” he cried,
** And lifted it with force intense.”
“ That’s true,” the low comedian sighed.
“ I saw him lift his audience—
'Twas no great matter. Truth to own,
It didn’t weigh eleven stone !”

Government Notices.
CLOSER SETTLEMENT.
Coreen 8 Back Paddock
(Parts of Gunambil) Estate.
t..
COROW A LAND DISTRICT. -
fIIHE Coreen and Back Paddock portions of the
Gunambil Estate, recently acquired by the State
Government, have been subdivided.
61 FARMS
will become
AVAILABLE FOR APPLICATION
on
TUESDAY, 16th NOVEMBER, 1909,
under the provisions of the Closer Settlement Acts.
The Farms range in area from 448 acres to 998 acres,
and in value from £lBO6 to £2931, the average area being
about 615 acres, and average value about £2456.
Full particulars and directions for applying, together
with lithographs and descriptions of the various farms,
may be obtained free of cost on application by post, or
otherwise, to any Crown Land Agent in New South
AVales, or the Information Bureau, Department of Lands,
Sydney.
The Estate is situated in the Corowa Land District,
and distant from 18 miles to 40 miles from Corowa. The
authorised railway line from Lockhart to Clear Hills —in
course of construction—will approach to within two miles
of the northernmost point, and about ,18 miles from the
south boundary, of the Settlement Purchase Area.
Balldale, the nearest station on the Culcairn-Corowa
line (14 ; miles from the southernmost portion of the
Estate), is distant 395 miles from Sydney. Oaklands,
(Clear Hills), the terminus of the Lockhart to Clear Hills
railway, is distant about 406 miles from Sydney.
The average rainfall in the locality' is 18 inches per
annum. No natural service supply', but water may be con-
served in tanks ; 23 of the farms already contain tanks,
An abundant supply of water has been obtained in parts
of the Estate by sinking and boring. The Estate is well
within the Murray River Irrigation Scheme, and is
irrigable.
The climate is good and very healthy.
The Estate is generally' level to undulating, eminently
suited for wheat, oats and mixed farming. The bulk of
the land may be classed as good agricultural forest land ;
the whole area is good grazing land.
Terms: —5 per cent, deposit, balance (including prin-
cipal and interest at 4 per cent.) in annual payments of 5
per cent., spread over a period of 38 years.
Persons who lodge applications for any' of the farms
referred to herein will be allowed a refund of half the
railway fare paid b,v them on their visit of inspection, and
on any' journeys they may require to make to appear
before the Land Board in connection with their applica-
tion. Successful applicants, their families, and such
belongings, including live stock, as were in their
possession immediately prior to the making of the appli-
cation for land will be ca tied at half rates, provided that
in the case of live stock it. be not more than sufficient for
the land required. Persons desirous of obtaining con-
cessions in respect of railway' fares may obtain a form
from any Crown Land Agent in the State, which must be
given to the Railway' Stationmaster when obtaining a
ticket, or, if unable to obtain a form, must ask the Rail-
way Stationmaster to take their name and address,
amount paid, and number of ticket.

A Maorilandeb has been cursing that country
before he quits it for Queensland
Everything- is exported, to the cost of those who are in
poor circumstances, and their name is legion. One can
buy Maoriland mutton in London cheaper than her.e. .
Well, the Maoriland Government alone has bor-
rowed £69,000,000, and the local governing bodies
have borrowed £12,000,600, and everybody else
has borrowed just about every penny he has been
able to raise ; and the bulk of all the borrowings
has been from Bull Cohen. All this debt carries
interest, and Maoriland has to export things
to pay the interest. It doesn’t matter what the
market may be like on the other side. Even if
Maoriland mutton only fetched Id. per lb. in
London, Maoriland would still have to send it
away and sell it for Id. All the difference the
low price would make would be that M.L. would
have to send away more mutton, and that would
make the price lower in London. And through
sending away more mutton to London there
would be less mutton left in Maoriland, and that
would make the price higher in Maoriland. It is
only a question of getting deep enough into debt,
and Maoriland will have to toil 16 hours out of
the 24 at raising and exporting mutton and selling
it for Id. per lb. in,London, while the Maorilander
himself, because all his decent mutton has gone
to London’s overstocked market,, pays lOd. per lb.
for his o'wn scarce old chop in Wellington or
Auckland.

Steam (obit 1912?). An
Epitaph.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
W W HY stand the dockyards silent !
f/jfy/' What ails the yards of Tyne ?
r r Is Sunderland a graveyard? Where's
Greenock of lang-syne,
And all the railway depdts and all the cars in
line ?■”
And heard ye not the story ; or is it that ye dream,
Ye Engineers and Stokers who thus astounded
seem—
In nineteen hundred (sometime) the world had
done with steam !
They tried it down in Egypt, three thousand
years ago,
That kettle-boiling method, but found it crude
and slow;
Just ear marked its invention, and left the matter
so;
Until a Gaelic person, once-glorified, named
Watts,
Sate mooning o’er his parritch and contemplating
pots:
And so achieved distinction and burned himself
in spots.
And Stephenson and Fulton, with engineers a
score,
Perfected water boilers, till all the wide world
o’er
The potent white steam clouded, red glowed the
furnace door.
And so, a passing epoch, it reigned, this New
World Power ;
A tyrant and a. master, a rank, exotic flower
On weeds of Exploitation, that flourished for an
hour.
It ’slaved the toiling legions, it held the Earth in
thrall ;
It narrowed down the Distance; yea, by its
mighty call,
The Fashion of the Ages was changed for good
and all.
It brought the hosts to battle, it drove the fleets
to fight;
On steel and iron altars, year-long, by day and
night,
Pale, sooty priests of Progress, its pale fires kept
alight;
That all the world might worship with Body,
Brains and Soul,
In use and constant service ; give fealty and toll
Unto this new-born Offspring, of Water—wed to
Coal.
And Lo ! in nineteen-sometime, one Smith, a
hermit-hatter,
The Universal-Boiler Age to Hades swift did
scatter,
By finding how to draw direct its energy from
matter!
Thus motors now, on rail-less roads, and airships
overhead,
From storage cells of Native Force at intervals are
fed—
And Smith in half a decade slew the Great Steam
Fetich dead ;
Turned turbines into ancient freaks, made rail-
roads seem a jest;
Converted boilers into junk, and simply left, the
rest
As potent as a catapult, compared to Armstrong's
best.
“Six-penn’orth of atomic force, please sir,” the
youthful Turk
Or Mongol at the counter cries these days with
easy smirk —
“Our airship came uncharged last night, and
father’s late for work.”
One generator to a shire, one station to a town,
Whence light and energy with ease, on wires are
measured down,
To run a mill or light a church a month for half-a-
crown;
Of course it changed the world at once—a Cheap
and Noiseless Force
That did away with smoke and smell, and waste
and clamor hoarse
Of engines that to-day appear so clumsy, old
and coarse.
VICTORIA, 1950, A.D.
E. J. BRADY.
A House of Insults.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A DOOR banged like the brazen Gates
of Doom, and a loud, indignant wailing
floated up the stairs to where three hag-
gard men played cards by moonlight.
“ Never hare I been so insulted in my life before !
Never!” It rang out like the howl of a muezzin
calling the faithful to prayer, and even the far-off
sound of the cat leaping on the kitchen-table in
search of nourishment went unheeded in the
shuddering silence that tiptoed in the
footprints of the last word.
“ And in my own house, too /” Again the dismal
voice below rang out, calling the attention of
whatever gods were on duty to the outrage.
“Your deal,” said the man without a collar,
throwing down the greasy cards.
“ Never have I been so insulted before! Never ! ”
The little man with sideboards stopped suddenly
in his dealing and said, fervently, “ I wish to G-d
I hadn’t asked her for that candle ! ” Then he
finished dealing, and all three took up their cards
and examined them.
“ Pass ! ” said the man with the benevolent grey
beard.
“ Pass ! ”
“ Pass ! ”
The man without a collar put his knife in the
kitty to mark the double-header, and took up the
cards for another deal.
“ Never have I been so insulted in my life /”
“ I’ll go three.”
“ Four,” muttered the dealer.
“ I pass ! ”
“ And in my own house, too! ”
The little man with sideboards dashed down his
cards with sheeplike fury. “I won't stand it!”
he shouted. “ I'll go down and tell her I won’t
stand it! ” He rose, and edging past the other
two went down the stairs into the utter darkness.
The pair of players, listening intently, could hear
the shuffle of his slippers on each step and his
slithering progress along the tattered linoleum of
the hall. Then there was a sound of timid knock-
ing and his lost voice crying, *'■* Now ! now ! Mrs.
Fadstitch ! ”
Never have I been so insulted in my life!”
From behind the distant door the lament of the
landlady sounded defiantly.
‘‘Now, now, Mrs. Fadstitch ! This won’t do
at all ! ”
“ And — in — my — own—house !”
“Mrs. Fadstitch! I won’t put up with this
sort of thing !”
Silence.
‘‘Now, now, Mrs. Fadstitch !”
Then the two listeners upstairs heard the ex-
plosive sound of a door opened suddenly from
within, and a loud angry voice trumpeted, “So
it’s uou, is it?”
“Yes,” replied the voice of the courageous
sheep. “I’ve come down to tell you I won’t
stand it ! ”
“ HOW—DARE—YOU ! ”
“The mere fact of a person asking for a
candle ”
“ What! Another candle ! ”
“ asking for a candle, Mrs. Fadstitch ”
“ Do you mean to tell me you have the imperti-
nence to stand there and ask me— ME ! —for
another candle?”
“Calm yourself, my dear madam. I didn’t
ask ”
“Never have I been so insulted in my
LIFE ! ”
“Now, now, Mrs. Fadstitch !”
“ And—in—my—own— house ! ”
“ Tut— tut, now ! ” In.a soothing tone.
“ How dare you say that to me ! ’’
“Mrs. Fadstitch, you must control yourself !”
“ How dare you tell me I must control
myself!”
“ Let us be reasonable, Mrs. Fadstitch ! ”
“ It is like your impertinence to say so ! ”
“Now, my dear madam ”
“Never have I been so insulted in my
LIFE ! ”
“Oh! Damn it all !” shouted the small man
hopelessly. The two above heard him slither a
step or two towards the stair when, with renewed
energy and Asiatic enthusiasm, the landlady’s
voice chanted, “Cursing—and—swearing—and—
demanding—candles—right—and—left ? ”
“ I only asked for one! ” shouted the sheep.
“Never have I been”
The little man pattered furiously upstairs and
burst in upon the awe-stricken pair.
“ I won’t stand it! ” he yelled. “I won’t stand
any more of it ! ” He sat on the edge of the bed
and mopped his brow.
“Cursing and candles and card-playing for
MONEY WHILE I SIT DOWN HERE AND STARVE ! ”
added the Voice triumphantly.
The man with the benevolent beard stumbled
out to the stair-rail and shouted down “Nobody
here has got any money ! ”
“Don’t I know it!” intoned the dark land-
lady. “ Haven’t I been robbed and insulted all my
life!”
“ Well, nobody here’s got a d d penny ! ”
“For three blessed weeks you idle men have
been playing cards for money in my house ! ”
“ Well, nobody’s got any money ! ”
“ And burning my candles”
“ You will be paid in due ”
“When are you three idle men going to stop
playing cards for money?”
“I tell you we’re not playing cards for money
at all ! We’re playing in a quiet gentlemanly way
to pass the hours until such time as we can obtain
remunerative employment.”
Answered the Voice, “I’ve been robbed and in-
sulted and trampled on by boarders all my life,
and nobody that ever lived under my roof ever
had a penny, or did any work, or wanted to ! ”
Hoarse with suppressed fury, the man with the
benevolent beard shouted back, “You will be
paid in full in due course!” With which
characteristic remark he resigned his share in the
discussion, and, returning from the lobby, sat
down and stroked his flowing whiskers in gloomy
silence.
The man without a collar, who had been aim-
lessly shuffling the cards from one hand to the
other, said miserably, “ Now you’ve offended her !
What’s the good of being insulting to the woman?”
The man with the benevolent beard turned on
him angrily. “Well,” he sneered “why don’t
you go down and pacify her, instead of skulking
up here in the dark? Why don’t you go down
and talk to her in an easy, gentlemanly fashion,
eh?”
The man with the cards shuffled his feet and
muttered angrily to himself.
“ Because you’re afraid, my dear sir ! Afraid /”
“No,” sighed tlie other, “I’m only ashamed.
I wouldn’t be, though, if I had any money.”
“Bah !” hissed the man with the benevolent
beard. “Who would be afraid or ashamed if he
had money ? But why be a skulking coward
because you don’t happen to have it?”
“ I’m not a skulking coward ! ”
“ You are, my dear sir—you arc!”
“Why? Because I haven’t got the face to go
down and ask the wretched woman for a candle ! ”
“You were ready enough to sit by the candle
if Flidger or I could get it, though.”
“ I know I was. But ”
"Well, what do you call that ? ”
“I call it pride, d——n you—proper pride.”
“Bah !”
The little man burst passionately into the argu-
ment. “ PRIDE !” he shouted. “Do you think
you’ve got all the pride in the house? Hasn’t
anybody else got any? Now I see why I’ve
always got to go and ask for candles. I thought
it was only your d d laziness, when all the
time it was just to save your rotten pride ! What
about MY pride?”
“Yes,” boomed the man with the beard,
fiercely, “and what about mine, confound you?
Three times within the last week, when it" was
your turn to go down and ask for candles, have I
gone and humiliated myself to that lodging-house
woman, and been insulted with my poverty to
save the dignity—God help us !—of a d d
barnstormer ! ”
The actor flung down the cards at the insult and
waved his clenched fist in the air. “By Heaven! ”
he hissed, “ this is too much ! ”
“No, by the lord Harry, it’s not! I’ve had
enough of your mountebank tricks, and if I were
a younger man I’d knock you down. You’re a
scoundrel, sir! ” He thrust his fiercely-wagging
beard within a foot of the furious actor’s face, and
the actor, with his open hand, pushed furiously at
the mass of whiskers and knocked the man they
were attached to over on the floor.
