“You villain ! Help. Flidger ! Help me up!
Old man as I am, I’ll thrash him ! ”
Little Flidger rushed to the assistance of the
man who called himself old but was only middle-
aged and lazy, and the terrified actor, with a back-
ward hunted look, ran out through the doorwav,
scrambled madly up the stairs, and, falling into
his room, banged and locked the door.
'•Where’s the d d scoundrel, Flidger?
Where is he ? I’ll flog him for this. He sha’n’t
escape me—-old man as I am!”
“No, no, Mr. Bodger ! You mustn’t! He
will apologise—he must apologise ! ” The little
man danced about in an ecstasy of fear.
"Apologies won’t do me, Flidger! I’ll drag
him out and thrash him within an inch of his life!
111 flog him ! Where is the scoundrel?”
“Hes in his room, Mr. Bodger. But I beg
you ”
“111 have him out, Flidger? I’ll drag him out
and flog him ! Come with me.” He stumped
heavily out with a great show of determination
and ferocity, and the little man followed, pluck-
ing at his arm and begging him not to do unvthing
rash.
They stamped up the stairs—or Bodger stamped
and Flidger crept—to the actor’s room, and there
bodger pounded on the door, and informed the
shivering inmate in a loud voice that he intended
to thrash him.
Up the stairs floated one of the landlady’s
public soliloquies. “Gaming and cursing and
candles to no end won’t satisfy them. Now
they’re going to insult me in tnv own house
by murdering each other. Oh ! they ought to be
ashamed of,themselves !.”
The magnificent and awe-inspiring Bodger
waved his whiskers over the stair-rail and shouted,
“Not murder, ma’am. I’m only just about to
thrash a scoundrel.”
“ Are you going to insult me in my own house
by thrashing scoundrels in it? ”
“He struck me, Mrs. Fadstitch ! He knocked
me down like a ninepin, and I’m going to flog the
-ooundrel ! ’’
“Do you think my house is fit for nothing but
flogging scoundrels in ?”
“ But he struck me, ma’am! He knocked me
down like a blackguard !”
“ And what else are you fit for but to be knocked
down like a blackguard ? I’ve been in this house
thirty years next week, and nkveu have I been so
insulted as I have been to-night! Never! And
in my own house, too /”
Before the amazed Bodger could think of a
suitable reply, there came a most furious bang-
ing at the street door. It continued until the
distraught landlady went and opened. Then
there was a whispered conversation and two
shrieks and an indignant roar from Bodger of,
“God bless my soul, if the woman isn’t kissing a
coal-heaver ! ”
The landlady in the midst of her transports,
looked up from the shoulder of the mean-looking,
weedy and dirty man she was embracing, and
sobbed, “It’s me beautiful son come back from
the sea with a fortune, and I’m going to throw up
all me boarders and live like a perfect lady for
ever—you see if I don’t ! ”
Bodger, staring and enraged, came down a step
or two and held up one hand like a bishop pro-
nouncing a jjenediction. “Woman!” he roared,
“ this is not a play! Release that man and let
him bring in his coal !”
But the hysterical landlady only clung closer to
the mean-looking, under-sized man and wailed.
It’s me beautiful son ! It’s me beautiful son ! ”
Bodger turned furiously on Flidger, who was
cowering behind, and cried passionately, “Are
we a parcel of d d actors, or are we not ? This
is the sort of cursed thing that only happens in
the third act! It isn’t real! It’s a d—d bit of
acting!” Then to the landlady, “Put that man
out! ”
But the landlady cried out again, “It’s me
beautiful son come home with a fortune.”
Bodger wheeled on the miserable Flidger and
roared at him, “D’ye hear that? She says the
coal man’s her beautiful son and that he’s come
back from the sea with a fortune. The dirty
blackguard! If a son of mine came back to me
like that and told me he’d made his fortune, I’d
flog him within an inch of his life ! You hear
me ! I’d Jlog him ! ”
At that, the alleged coal man sulkily disengaged
himself from the landlady’s embrace and slouched
meanly to the foot of the stair. “ I’m er son, I
tell yer,” he whined. “ I’m ’er son, an’ I’ve made
me fortin’.”
“You’re not!” yelled Bodger. “You’re a
filthy ruffian, and you’re acting ! I know it’s all
d——d acting !”
“It’s true, I tell yer ! ” growled the man.
“It’s lies !” yelled Bodger. “It couldn’t be
true. Good God ! d’ye think I'm mad? You’re
too dirty to be anything but a coalheaver. I tell
you you’re a dirty coalheaver, and that you’re
acting.”
“Oh, am I!” snarled the man. “ Well, look
at this —an’ this!” As he spoke, he drew from
his pocket two chamois-leather bags, and, calling
to the tearful landlady to hold up her dirty apron,
emptied a heap of gold coins into it with a rush.
“ Oh, me beautiful son ! ” wailed the, landlady,
doing a sort of waltz with her laden apron heid
out in front of her, while the dirty son caught up
handfuls of the gold and let it trickle through his
fingers.
Bodger flung out an accusing arm. “More of
your d——d acting !” he roared. “ It’s stage money
it isn’t real. It’s all d d acting!” He turned
round to Flidger and howled: “ Haul out that
acting criminal from upstairs, and let us have him
down to help them.” '
“You come ’ere and see f’r yerself if it ain’t
reel!” growled v fhe, man; and Bodger went down
like a man in a dream and saw that he spoke the
truth.
Instantly he turned to Flidger and yelled,
“ He’s robbed a bank! He’s robbed a bank, by
the Lord, and we’ll all be in gaol in the morning !
Let’s get out, Flidger, before the police get us!
It’s a den of thieves and actors, and if we’re
found here we’ll get ten years apiece and a Hog-
ging—and serve usd d well right ! Where’s my
hat?”
Then the wizened man stepped in front of the
perfectly insane Bodger and yelled, “ Don’t yer
b’leeve me now'! Don’t yer b’leeve I’m ’erson nor
nothin ’ ? ”
And Bodger howled back, “NO, you black-
guard ! 1 don’t, and I won’t believe anything
about you ! I know you’re a dirty ruffian and
that you’re acting and that you’ve robbed a bank.
And if you try to tell me any more lies or act to
me, I’ll give you a d d good flogging ! Out of
my way, you ruffiau !” He stormed past the man
and the slowly-collapsing landlady who held a
fortune in her apron, and Flidger, with a battered
straw hat on his head and bearing an ancient bell-
topper and a large knobby stick in his arms,
followed him down the hall and out of the house.
Outside, Bodger assumed and
the stick, and they linked arms and walked away
together into the night, Bodger—who had been
thrown completely off his mental bslmce—gesti-
culating with the enormous stick and giving five
thousand different reasons why it must be all lies
and acting'.
And, back in the house the}' had left so vio-
lently, the landlady was sitting dazed in the
middle of her own grimy and insulted hall, sur-
rounded by gold pieces, while her unheroic-look-
ing son told her—in remarkably bad grammar-
how, after many patient years “puttin’ in,” he
had at last drawn the third horse.
KODAK,
Art.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN a land that ye may know of rose a strong,
determined foe of
Cant and Maunder, aged Shoddy, and the
Ancient Rot of years ;
Published large defiant verses, dragging dead
things to their hearses—
But alas! his views were Modern, so his efforts
met with sneers !
Came the Critics, animated with conceptions anti-
quated.
Fillet! with vast respect for Mummy and the Cere-
ments of the Tomb ;
His were thoughts men needed badly—but his
works they pondered sadly,
And pronounced with solemn unction certain
heavy words of doom !
“ Sir,” they said, “ your verseful vigor may appea 1
to smith and digger,
And we hear you’re making money and are quoted
in the mart;
But alas ! you do not write of things the Dead
Men did indite of—
And you flout the ancient canons, and we fear it
isn’t Art! ”
Then the writer, being cheerful, thought to
please these parties tearful,
So he ceased to rail at Maunder and to scoff at
gods of Cant;
Ceased his writing altogether—straightway
slipped his poet’s tether,
And commenced to splash on canvas like a festive
elephant! -
Smith the gloomy, Jones the sweater—folk who
bowed ’neath custom’s fetter—
These he pictured, these he painted, likewise
laborers at toil ;
Parsons monkey-lipped and doleful—these in
manner strong and soulful
Were depicted true to pattern, both in crayons
and in oil !
Pompous folk—yea, men Right-Thinking; curate-
creatures sadly slinking—
These as well the painter wrought on; bishops,
too, and fat Jay Pees ;
Flunkeys sleek and choir-girls smirking, loafers
idle, strong men working—
Such he pictured as he saw them, then he said :
“Just look at these !”
So the Critics came in dozens—dreary folk, yea,
parsons’ cousins—
And they gazed upon his paintings, whilst their
jaws in horror fell ;
“Sir,” they gasped, “these things are awful—we
must have them made unlawful.
You have wrecked the Arch-Commandment—you
have dared the truth to tell !
“ It is true, the world may buy them—sinful folk
may gladly eye them
Men, alas ! are full of badness and the globe is
paved with woe ;
But these pictures will not do, sir; you have
chosen subjects new, sir,
And the M isters did nbt do so in the sacred Long
Ago !
“We, the Critics, rise to warn you, righteous folk
will surely scorn you.
Bishop Smith will preach about it; Deacon Jones
the prayer will start;
You have scoffed at old Tradition, and at persons
of position—
You may sell your wicked pictures, but we say it
isn’t Art! ”
So the painter dropped his palette, and he seized
the sculptor’s mallet.
“I will sculp,” said he, “some subjects that these
folk will surely hail ;
Drunken monarehs. Justice sleeping, Envy base
and Malice creeping—
And the dead man on the gallows, and the convict
in the gaol! ”
Then once more the Critics hied them to his
workshop, and decried them—
“lt is vain!” they said; “lie's Modern, as we
wrote long years before ;
!Tis the same audacious scoffer—yea, no praise
have we to offer,
For such themes are most improper, and these
sculptures we deplore !
“You must bow to old Tradition—to Cellini and
to Titian—
You have written, sculped and painted ; heed
these words that we impart;
Try the old romantic themes, sir—dreatn the
ancient, fusty dreams, sir—
All these works of yours are Modern, and we say
it isn’t Art! ”
But the Painter-Sculptor-Writer said: “Col-
lective Blatherskiter,
You are ignorant and foolish, and no more I’ll
strive to please ;
You have made a joss, of Mummy—yea, have
raised a futile durum}',
Now I rise to wreck your idol and your Old
Tradition wheeze !
“With the eras Art advances—you are lost in
ancient trances.
Ye are brooding over Maunder, over aged things
and slow.
All the men whereof ye prated in their day were
execrated—
They were Modern then, and rebels— hang the
sacred Long Ago!”
